## 10-01-1495 Taraskh

### Valphine  

And so we spend the next few hours playing music. At first, Vukradin plays alone. And when he stops, waiting to see if anyone will pay, two silver pieces are dropped at his table. He seems pleased. I ask if I can join him, and he says yes. 

The combination of my tones and instrumentation works wonders with Vukradin’s brilliant modern interpretations of traditional bardic music. I can feel the power of Lathander swell as the pain of his tones, and the light of my music creates an effect on the soul.  

Brewbarry joins in using of his halberd to create a percussive beat hearkens to the tribal music of the Uthgardt tribes. 

The crowd seems far more engaged, and we make 10 more silver. Vukradin is pleased? 

In the morning, when we awake, we find a note in our room. It’s directed to Vukradin. 

My Dear Vukradin,

I write to you from the shadows, a concerned admirer of your noble spirit and your unwavering commitment to honest wages, fair trade, and truly conflict-free gold. Your voice, advocating for the innocent and questioning the very nature of wealth acquired through plunder and "barbaric slaughtering," resonates deeply.

It is with a heavy heart, then, that I feel compelled to share what I have observed regarding certain… discrepancies within the very organization you have recently joined. The Order of the Gauntlet, for all its grand pronouncements of righteousness and "conflict-free gold," may not be as pristine as it claims.

One must ask: from what coffers does their "pure" funding truly flow?

Consider the whispered rumors of certain "generous donors" to the Order. I have heard tell of connections to House Margaster, a name long associated with "unsavory businesses" in Waterdeep, practices that certainly do not align with the principles of fairness you champion. Could it be that the Gauntlet accepts coin from those whose hands are far from clean, turning a blind eye to the origins of such wealth?

Furthermore, there are whispers that some of the "reclaimed" artifacts that bolster the Order's treasury were not simply taken from villainous cults, but rather forcibly appropriated from minor, neutral parties or acquired through means that could only be described as less-than-honorable. Is gold truly "conflict-free" when it comes from such dubious seizures, much like the "stolen merchandise" you so adamantly refuse to touch?

The path of true justice and lasting order is rarely simple, Vukradin. It demands scrutiny, even of those who claim to stand for good. The constant "rushing headlong into the tide" may appear zealous, but without a clear understanding of the currents that move beneath, even the noblest intentions can be compromised, leading to unforeseen consequences or, worse, complicity in the very acts they seek to oppose.

I urge you to look closer, to use your uncanny insight to discern the truth behind these allegiances and funding sources. For if the foundation is tainted, how truly righteous can the deeds built upon it be?

A Friend to True Justice.  
House Margaster, I have heard of them. When I was in Neverwinter, rumors circulated that they were associated with cults of evil. 

Vukradin is frustrated. He was hoping for a fan letter. 

I am convinced that someone entered the room, but I can find nothing. The letter was slipped under the door. 

But who?

“Did you get the letter?” Asks the innkeeper. 

Apparently, he had slipped it under the door, having forgotten to give it to us last night when we arrived. He explained that the message came from Neverwinter. 

When we probed to see if he had opened it, he was offended, “I don’t look at other people’s mail.” 

Vukradin seems satisfied, but I am not. 

“Let’s go make Soma happy.” 

### Soma

As we head out to the whispering grove, the staff that Merill gave me suddenly feels alive. Some magic inside it was unlocked. 

# Chapter 10 The stag, the brambles, the wolves, and the pool.  

### Soma

There are no signs of rotting trees or obvious blight in the Whispering Wood. And yet, I can feel something is different about this place. 

Suddenly, a majestic stag materializes before us. Its fur shimmers and flickers. This creature is caught between here and some other place. Its antlers are constantly moving and changing shape. And then it teleports 15 feet away. 

Is this the unnatural blight?

"Do you want to talk to this stag?" Vukradin asks, ever the practical one. "I do," I reply, casting Speak with Animals. Approaching the shimmering form, my first question is simple: "Are you okay?".

The stag pauses, then replies with a surprising territoriality. "I was okay... Until you walked into my territory. Why are you in my territory? Get out of my territory," it insists. I try to calm it, explaining, "We're just passing through, we mean no harm, we just... we notice that you seem... Out of sorts". 

I look around and can see the boundary of the stag’s territory. There is a broken branch here, a dropping there, and nibble over there. 

Valphine quickly moves out of the territory, but Brewbarry and the shell sprout don’t move. 

"Brewbarry, can you back off a little?" I ask, then turn to Vukradin, "Could you back up a little? Just give the stag some privacy". Once they've retreated, I continue, "We've cleared your territory. Is there something weird going on, though? I'm not used to seeing stags teleport around and shimmer like you do".

The stag seems to consider this, then teleports again, leaving a brief afterimage that quickly fades. When it reappears, it confirms my suspicions: "Yes, things have not been normal for some time". It explains how it "used just to be able to walk and prance and eat the vegetables," but now it can "blink" and its antlers are "Different" every time it looks at them. "The whole forest has gotten weird. It's almost as if everything has changed.”

The stag mutters that it’s constantly changing antlers make it hard to avoid branches and brambles. 

Then, with an abrupt change of subject, the stag asks if we have "anything to eat?". We offer some of the mushrooms we collected from Gnomengarde. Satisfied, it provides a crucial piece of information: "it all started in the center of the forest". It mentions "other nice people" who also "spoke to me, too" and said they would "fix it, but they haven't".

With this new lead, we decide to head towards the center of the forest. As I lead the group, I keep thinking about the stag. Something unnatural festers here.

### Valphine  

My drow senses, accustomed to the subtle currents of intrigue and the raw, often terrifying, distortions of the Underdark, hummed with an uncomfortable awareness. Soma seemed unconcerned. I felt at home. 

Ahead, I noticed a dense thicket of brambles with leaves pulsing with unnatural colors, sometimes glowing, withering to black. This is not a disease. Soma, the naturalist, calls it "Planar leakage, bleeding."

How droll. I thought the Underdark and its mad mages were without peer in their distortion of reality. But this was new. And powerful. And it could hurt. How delightful. 

Soma, always eager to understand the natural world, approaches the blighted growth within ten feet. Foolish. The brambles, as if sentient, lash out. Thorny tendrils strike her, dealing piercing and necrotic damage. I observe her wince, a faint, fleeting flicker of pain. How wonderful. 

The Thicket, this source of pain, must be dealt with. I call upon the gloriously painful light of Lathander, my patron deity, channeling a Sacred Flame. My divine fire, meant to obliterate darkness and inflict pain, merely glances off. 

Brewbarry, always direct, moves within ten feet, and the thorny tendrils immediately attempt to strike him. They miss. His rage ignites, a visible aura of fury, and he attacks with his halberd, tearing the branches apart—such beautiful and efficient violence. 

Soma transforms into a bear and strikes the bushes. Vukradin follows with a biting commentary. I notice how the thicket appears to regenerate.

And then the bramble stops moving. Brewbarry and the Bear have rendered it inert. We move through it before it can reanimate. 

###  Vukradin   

We faced some wolves. They had an unnatural howl that rooted me to the spot. And their eyes glowed. Others may say it is fear. I say it was magic. The creatures were quickly defeated, and then we took their pelts. 

I was very pleased to seize their pelts. Because a dire wolf pelt is, by definition, conflict-free gold, and more importantly, the unnatural magic of this place may make them more valuable to the discerning collector. 

After skinning the creatures, we continue deeper into the Whispering Wood, a place that makes the hairs on my arms prickle. It's more than just a strange energy; it's a disharmony, a discordant note in the natural world that offends my artistic sensibilities. 

The fear of the wolves, a magical fear, prompted me to reflect on my own personal fears. Adabra said she thought I only did it for the music. It was the music in its purest essence that motivated me. But was it? Was I just afraid to admit that it was about the fans? I saw the fans at Stonehill Tavern so happy, and I realized it is about the fans. I won’t be afraid of doing it for the fans. And if it is about the fans, and not my music, should I just stop trying to make them love what I love and instead do what they love? 

And then we stumble into a clearing, and there in front of us is a pool of water. 

I look carefully and realize that this is something different. The surface reflects a distorted version of ourselves. Valphine asks, “Will I see myself happy and content?” 

I look at her more carefully. She looks at the pool, but avoids staring at it. It’s almost as if she is afraid of being happy. As I was scared of admitting that I liked to play for the fans, I thus became consumed by my desire to focus solely on the music. And in my pursuit of music, I walked away from what truly made me happy. Was I sabotaging my music? Trying to make it less likable to the fans? 

This pond may help me see more clearly. So I look in. 

And there I see a version of myself clutching coins constantly. Is this the price of pursuing art or the price of pleasing my fans? Is this a version of me trying to convey a message to me? 

I must know more. This is a planar scar, a window into fractured realities. I jump in. 

The moment I step in, a dizzying cacophony assails my mind. It's "people, scenes, faces looking at me all at the same time”. A barrage of fragmented visions, whispers, and fleeting emotions. This is knowledge, raw and unadulterated, but it's overwhelming. I try to hold onto my composure, but I cannot. 

A chill seeps into my very being, a creeping sense of unease that twists my perception. Then, through the swirling chaos, I see a distorted creature pleading, saying, "He saw beyond, but only what he was meant to see. The numbers. Always the numbers.”  The numbers. My mind latches onto it. Is it the counting of "blood gold", the endless tally of "stolen merchandise"? The cold, hard accounting of a world driven by plunder rather than art? It's a terrifying vision, affirming my convictions about "conflict-free gold" and the "sordid morality" of this world.

The intensity is too much. I need to escape this place. I stumble out of the pool, feeling an immense drain on my energy, and then, without warning, I fall unconscious. The world simply... ceases to be.

The next thing I know, a sharp, stinging sensation on my cheek brings me back to the jarring reality. Someone has revived me with a slap. I blink, trying to clear the lingering haze of madness.

Soma, ever the voice of pragmatism, but sometimes, in her "matronly" way, warns us not to enter the pool, for our "own sanity”. But Brewbarry, bless his loyal but bumbling heart, decides to look into the pool anyway. He mentions something about Uthgardt legends of such pools being "Shadows to other places" that "appear unnaturally" and offer knowledge.

Once again, Brewbarry demonstrates a curious intellect and awareness of the world that his background and superficial intellect hide. Perhaps it’s that deeper connection that allows him to appreciate my music. 

He asks the pool, "What side of the bread should I butter?". A question so utterly Brewbarry. What he sees is a version of himself that says, "butter both sides simultaneously." The sheer absurdity of it almost makes me laugh.

Suddenly, Brewbarry is terrified and starts running away. He's screaming about "both sides". Soma transforms into her bear form and subdues him. 

Long after the hysteria is over, he mutters, “Both sides\!” 

Valphine wants to ask "more intelligent questions of the pool.”  

Soma, in her motherly tone,  advises against it, for our "own sanity\!" 

Why isn’t Soma more worried? This pool, and the distortions of this forest, should bother her. The compulsive needs of druids to make things natural should have provoked something in her. Instead, she seems curiously only concerned with us and uninterested in the world around her. 

I shake my head. 

The image of me counting money, and the distorted visions in the pool, tell me we have to move on. This place is too dangerous for abstract contemplation or the pursuit of forbidden knowledge. We have a mission, and this pool, while fascinating, is a distraction that threatens to unravel our minds. 

As we walk closer to the center, the whisper about numbers keeps poking at my mind like a splinter in my eye. 

# Chapter 11 Naturalists and Interventionists

### Soma

The air itself tastes wrong as we push through the last line of dense trees and step into the clearing. It’s perhaps sixty feet across, but something here is fundamentally off. My fur, thick and protective in this bear form, prickles with an unease that goes beyond simple predator instinct. The woods surrounding us don’t lean; they *twist*, their bark gleaming like polished obsidian under the faint, sickly light filtering through a canopy that looks less broken and more *fractured*.

Pools of iridescent sap shimmer on the ground, their colors shifting in a hypnotic dance that makes my bear brain recoil. This isn’t the healthy glow of life; it’s an alien sheen. The underbrush, usually a soft carpet for my paws, is blackened and thorny, snagging at my thick hide. It feels like walking through a wound in the world.

At the heart of it all stands an immense tree, a Treant, its limbs bent in impossible geometries, dripping that glowing, unnatural sap into a dark pool. And near it, faint, flickering tears in reality pulse softly, whispering not of power, but of a madness I want no part of. There's nothing fair or right about such power. This place is… a blight—an unnatural disruption.

My keen bear senses pick up movement, and then the distinct, desperate scent of fear. There, trapped amidst the thorny undergrowth, are several figures. Members of the Emerald Enclave, clearly. I wonder what brings them to such a twisted place, but then one of them, a woman whose scent speaks of leadership, shouts.

"My name is Lyra. Help us\! We're out of the Emerald Enclave\! This thing\! It was the Grove's Guardian, but it has been corrupted. We are trying to cleanse her of a planar infection when it turned on us. Please, fight it\! Don't let the corruption spread\!"

Her words reverberate through my mind. *Corrupted.* *Planar infection.* It’s precisely what Adabra and my own deepest instincts have warned me about: these constructs and extra-planar abominations, these disruptions to the natural order. This Treant, this once noble guardian, is no longer itself. It has been twisted, warped by forces that do not belong here.

I cannot speak in this form, so a low growl rumbles in my chest, a bear's affirmation of understanding and simmering defiance. Lyra wonders out loud if I am a druid or a bear. *Let her figure it out on her own*, a dry wit only I can appreciate in this moment.

The thorny brambles, these blighted vines, are creatures themselves, holding the elves captive. I saw them earlier. They won't actively attack unless we get too close, but they prevent spellcasting, binding the Enclave members physically. 

Vukradin, ever the loose pebble, rolls his eyes and tries to *Command* a vine. The vine simply ignores him, much like a stubborn sapling shrugs off a light breeze. He prefers his "command performances" to effective action. He stares at the vine, defeated. He is still wet from the nest and needs to be protected. So I act. 

My eyes fall upon the Treant itself, towering and sinister. Its bark is glossy obsidian, its limbs stretch in impossible angles, and its movements appear as if you blinked and then saw the creature in a different position, but you never blinked.  This is not the ancient, wise guardian of a natural grove.

The Treant then speaks. Its voice is overlapping whispers resonating with an arrogance that chafes against my naturalist beliefs: "Stay out of my grove. If you stay out of my grove and let me be, then I will not attack you". It is making a claim of ownership over a place.

Adabra said that there was a blight and that the interventionist had to be stopped. What is the blight? Where is the blight? 

Is this strange magic the blight? Where are those who are intervening? Are they the Emerald Enclave?

### Valphine

My drow eyes, ever keen to the subtle distortions of reality, watch Soma – still in her bulky bear form – lumber towards the blighted brambles. Her brute force approach is, at least, direct, a refreshing change from Vukradin's performative posturing. The thorny tendrils lash out, predictably. I see them strike her, and though she is a sturdy creature, I note the faint wince, the minor disruption to her composure as four points of damage are inflicted. It is a taste of pain, perhaps not gloriously searing, but present nonetheless.

She swings her massive paws in retaliation, a primal, unrefined attack. The first misses, a clumsy flailing that almost makes me sigh. Then, with a surprising lack of grace, the second connects... with herself. A bear hitting itself in the midst of combat. How utterly inefficient. The Treant, I note, finds this amusing. Indeed.

It is at this point that Lyra, the trapped Emerald Enclave leader, calls out to me, her voice cutting through the unnatural whispers of the grove. "A cleric of a drow that is a cleric of Lathander? Can this be?" she exclaims, her tone a mix of disbelief and perhaps a touch of awe. I meet her gaze, a small, knowing smile touching my lips. Yes, it can be. The surface world is so easily surprised by the obvious. I offer a simple, honest truth, a declaration of my paradoxical devotion: "Glorious lightbrining pain.” My new god's power is a brilliant, beautiful agony, a sensation far more profound than the petty squabbles of drow houses or druids.

Soma, recovering from her self-inflicted embarrassment, manages to land a solid blow, against the blighted vine. Two of the ensnared elves – Lyra and another – are finally freed. Lyra immediately produces four crude stones, a dramatic flourish common among surface dwellers. "We need to complete the ritual," she announces, "on the stones to clear… to seal the rift". She explains what we need to do, tricky and dangerous. And if we fail three times, we failed. Drow rituals were precise, brutal affairs, often with far more immediate consequences for failure. This reliance on a series of successes, with such a generous margin for error, speaks of a certain softness. Still, I think, a ritual implies a structure, a focus of power. Perhaps it is not entirely without merit.

Brewbarry steps forward, his usual gruff demeanor punctuated by a sudden surge of power. He grows in size, his already formidable frame expanding to grant him a fifteen-foot reach. A brute, certainly, but sometimes sheer mass has its uses. He targets another blighted vine, swinging his weapon with typical barbarian abandon. It is a miss. His efficiency could use refinement.

Then, with an almost oblivious confidence, he steps into the Treant's immediate vicinity, the aura of corrupting planar energy that whispers promises of madness. The "doom zone," as Vukradin might call it. He is forced to contend with its insidious influence, and to my mild surprise, his primal will is enough to see it for what it is. The Barbarian is more resilient than he appears, or perhaps his mind is already too comfortably situated in its own unique form of chaos for this additional madness to take hold. "You mean more than I am already?" I recall him saying, a flicker of dark humor that resonates with my own understanding of existence. Good. Less unexpected chaos for me to contend with.

Now, it is my turn. With the blighted vines still holding the remaining Emerald Enclave members, and Brewbarry having failed to make a significant impact, a more… refined approach is clearly required. I lift my hand, calling upon the raw, glorious power of Lathander. The light of the Morninglord, a searing balm to my drow senses, gathers in my palm.

I unleash a Guiding Bolt, a focused spear of pure light, directly at the still-resilient vines. It strikes true, a solid hit. The unnatural pulsing of the brambles visibly flickers, their sickly colors momentarily dulled as the divine energy scorches them. They are weakened now and with the brilliance of Lathander's light still clinging to them, they now offer an advantage to the next attacker.

A tactical advantage. A demonstration of focused, directed pain. This is the beauty of Lathander's power as I perceive it: not a gentle warmth, but a potent, purifying fire that cuts through corruption and illuminates the path to victory. It is gloriously painful in the lights, a sensation that still thrills me to my core. The surface dwellers may not understand my devotion, but its efficacy in battle is undeniable.

###  Vukradin

The grotesque dance continues, a discordant movement in this wretched grove. My *Starry Whip*, a personal composition of biting starlight, lashes out at the corrupted Treant. A satisfying, if not exactly operatic, blow. It connects, which is always preferable to a mere command performance.

Now for this so-called "ritual". Six successful actions required, three failures and this entire composition unravels. The stakes, while not truly artistic, are present. Lyra, still catching her breath, seems to believe that we – this motley ensemble – can achieve what her Emerald Enclave could not. I consider the four crude stones she conjured – aesthetically lacking, but a task is a task. I attempt the ritual, but alas, I fail. A dismal failure. "Failure. I love it. One failure down," I quip, the words tasting of bitter irony. It is, in its own way, a testament to the unpredictable nature of existence, or perhaps simply a commentary on the universe's refusal to appreciate true finesse. As if to punctuate my artistic lament, the planar energies of this blight exact a toll and inflict a dull, throbbing bass note in my mind.

The Treant, this warped guardian, spouts another bombastic declaration of self-importance. "You think I'm wrong\! You think there is something wrong with me\! That I must be changed back\! I am… happy to be powerful and dangerous\!" it booms, its voice an overlapping chorus of whispers, a cacophony of self-delusion. It then, with a predictable lack of dramatic flair, attacks one of the Enclave’s Warriors who miraculously survives with a single hit point. A missed opportunity for a truly tragicomic scene. Lyra, bless her earnest heart, unleashes a Moonbeam, only to be re-ensnared by the Treant's insidious vines. The cycle of futility, truly.

Brewbarry, ever the bumbling but earnest brute, then stomps forward, cleaving another vine and even manages to hurt the Treant itself. He is within the aura of madness, the "doom zone", but shrugs off its psychic caress with an almost insulting resilience. Perhaps his mind is already too comfortably situated in its own unique form of chaos for this additional madness to take hold. Valphine, ever the pragmatic drow, unleashes *Fairy Fire*, illuminating our foe with a rather beautiful glow, and then sends a crossbow bolt at a vine. A decent display of focused pain, if one appreciates such uninspired directness.

Then Soma, dropping her bear form, attempts her hand at the ritual. She focuses her wisdom and… Our first ritual success\! Perhaps there's hope for these crude, terrestrial rituals after all. Still, the planar energies extract their toll with more pain for lingering in the aura. She then conjures another Moonbeam, bathing the Treant in a pale, ethereal light inflicting pain. The beast reels, a promising crescendo\!

It's my turn again. The second enclave warrior, poor soul, is at death's door. Principles demand action, even if it momentarily delays my artistic pursuits. I move to him, performing a quick burst of healing—and he is restored. Enough to pull him back from the precipice, if only for a moment. Then, with a renewed sense of purpose, I address the ritual. Lyra’s ritual requires finesse of hand.  Soma, in her matronly way, offers to help—a testament to her burgeoning understanding of collaborative art, even if the medium is less refined than I would prefer.  A success\!. Two successes, one failure. We are, as the commoners say, in the game.

Brewbarry follows, who, for all of his behavior, seems to have a deep wellspring of wisdom, attempts a ritual... Another success. Three successes, one failure. We're practically proficient\! But then Soma, with a clumsy flourish, tries and fails. Our second ritual failure. Before I can even process the dramatic irony, Soma and the first Enclave Warrior deliver the final, unceremonious blows to the Treant. The corrupted guardian falls, not with a bang, but with a whimper of poison and arrows—a rather inelegant final cadenza.

### Soma 

The tree dies. And the ritual is not yet finished. I see one of the stones. I will do the ritual.

And then I pause. Is helping Lyra the right thing to do? Is she telling me the truth that this is a disruption in the natural order, and that she is trying to set things right? Or is this the natural order, and Adabra is right?  

Vukradin turns to her and asks, “Was your plan to come here with your friends and perform this ritual on your own? A ritual this complicated?” 

Vukradin turns on the charm, and Valphine, with a noblesse d’oblige, turns it on even more. 

Vukradin is upset. 

Valphine turns to him and says, “You surface dwellers are so easy to read.” 

Lyra turns to them both, “I don’t think I thought this through very carefully.” 

Vukradin rolls his eyes, “Understatement of the year.” 

But the druid continues, “There was talk amongst the emerald enclave that we should do something. Some said we should do nothing, saying it was part of the natural world, and others said it was not.” 

I am confused; this is making no sense. “Are you suggesting that this is natural and you are trying to intervene and stop us from stopping it?”  

Lyra stares at me, confused.

I am confused. 

She looks at me and sighs, “The naturalists think this,” and she waves her hand at all of the unnatural effects that surround me,” is natural.”  

Adabra would not lie. They are lying. I am tempted first to kill her. And then I am tempted to attempt the ritual and purposely fail, and then I remember the staff my mentor gave me. A staff that has the power to detect unnatural elements in the natural world. 

And the staff glows, and it tells me that the treant, the pools, and the bushes are all unnatural. This planar rift is not part of the natural world. 

This makes no sense. The interventionists want to protect the natural world from the planar intrusions, and the naturalists wish to safeguard the planar intrusions. 

I want to protect the natural order. And then I realize that I, too, am still wet from the nest. All the time I spent in my forest, I never stopped to learn what was going on beyond. And now here I am. All this time, I looked down at Vukradin when I should have been looking at myself. 

I must learn. 

“What is an interventionist? And what will happen here if we fail?” 

Lyra looks at me, “The interventionists? I haven’t heard that in a while.” She sits down. 

“For a very long time, the question of the boundary of the natural world has been a bitter debate. Some draw the boundary at the prime material plane, some draw the boundary at their forest, and some draw the boundary at the multiverse.”

She looks at me as if I were a shell sprout. And I am. 

“It was the kind of thing that people would argue, but it didn’t really matter. And then the War of the Dragons happened. Severin and his mad men concocted a plan to summon Tiamat into the Prime Material. Some felt that Tiamat was an abomination and must be stopped. Others believed Tiamat’s survival or death was just part of the natural cycle because Tiamat was part of the multiverse, and wasn’t everything part of the multiverse? And that everything would be okay, and we didn’t have to do anything.”

I look at her and remember those days when the Dragons flew and burned. 

“A council in Waterdeep was formed, and the Emerald Enclave attended. Delaan Winterhound was the wisest of us. And we would have stayed out of this war, but Malfire, a hero of the War of the Dragon, convinced him that we must join to save the world. Many of us answered and fought. But those who believed that the boundary of the natural world was the multiverse were furious. They screamed that we were intervening in the natural world and violating our oaths.” 

Tiamat’s defeat has led to an uneasy truce. Those who say we did the right thing point out the harm that we avoided. Those who say we did the wrong thing point out that it was Tiamat’s defeat that led to the War of the Giants. That Tiamat’s defeat inspired the Giant gods to shatter the Ordning.”

She pauses. 

“The naturalists see what you see here and see nothing wrong. I see what I see here and see an alien force corrupting our world.” 

My mind reels. 

Lyra stares at me, confused. 

“This tree ate people,” I declare. “Trees do not eat people.” 

Lyra nods.

“It was a good tree, and it protected this forest, but ever since the planar scar emerged, it became poisoned. It was no longer sustained by the natural light of the sun, but by these planar pools.” 

Valphine adds, “Sunlight is not natural.” 

Both Vukradin and I glare at her. 

And this is where the choice looms, stark and unsettling. Adabra believes the interventionists are the blight, the ones who disrupt. Lyra, the interventionist, was fighting what she believed was a blight, a planar infection. I thought I was a naturalist, but the undeniable reality of this corruption, this motivated blight, twists my understanding. I don't know anymore.

If Adabra is right, then accepting this planar power is natural, and intervening is the blight. But my own senses are screaming that this is unnatural, an intentional harm. If Lyra is right, and this is an unnatural blight, then Adabra's naturalism, in this context, allows for destruction. 

Adabra never spoke of a motivated blight, of a puppet controlled by unseen hands. She spoke of accepting existence. How can I accept this calculated desecration? The sheer wrongness of it fills me with dismay. I must act. I will seek answers, starting with Adabra. Perhaps she can explain this paradox. Or I may find my own path, separate from either faction, guided by the raw, undeniable truth of the living world itself. This is not balance. This is sickness. And sickness, I now realize, must be healed.

Vukradin reminds me of the tormented deer, and my decision is made for me. I attempt to do the ritual successfully. And I succeed. 

And then another failure. And then I try one last time to succeed, and I fail. 

And a screaming, searing pain rips through my head. I look around. Everyone else is clutching their heads. 

The earth shudders, not with natural movement, but with a sickening internal tremor. Around us, the iridescent pools and planar wells begin to grow in size, pulsing with an even more violent, sickly light. My shell prickles with a deep unease. The Whispering Grove is no longer merely 'withering'; a planar rift possesses it.

Lyra is very upset. "This did not work the way I had hoped," she whispers. I watch her, and think how hard it must be not to have a shell to hide into, and then I am confused. 

"Alright, let's get outta here," someone says. The air itself feels poisoned, the very fabric of existence here stretched thin and frayed. We leave, but the image of those expanding wells, the pulsing, unnatural colors, etches itself into my shell.

As we fall back, a shadow detaches itself from the swirling chaos of the rift. It's an inquisitive creature. Phased out, its form shimmering at the edge of perception, its eyes distorted. It moves with an unsettling grace, like a marionette, picking things up off the ground. It seems to be under the control of something. Brewbarry, with surprising clarity that often belies his brutish exterior, voices the thought stirring in my own mind: "He's a puppet that's being controlled."

My gaze tracks it as it moves, collecting ingredients from the corpse of the corrupted Treant. This is not the dance of scavenger birds, or the natural decay of the forest. This is calculated. Purposeful. The creature disappears into the well, vanishing back into the planar tear.

The wells remain disrupting the world. This isn't random. It can't be. My internal struggle intensifies. Adabra, my wise friend and philosophical guide, taught me that naturalists accept these planar shifts as normal. She warned me against interventionists, calling them the blight, the ones who meddle. But what I've just witnessed, what I've felt in this grove, screams otherwise.

I must go and talk to her.

# Chapter 12 Confronting Adabra and learning of the Interventionists.  

### Soma

I feel like a shell sprout, wet from the nest, a child that has been taken advantage of. I trusted Adabra. And she took advantage of my trust to get me to stop the people who were trying to save our plane. 

Vukradin tries to calm me down, but I growl, “I want to chew her out\!” 

The bard shrugs. 

As we enter the main square, we head to where she has set up shop, next to Garaele’s house, across from the Shrine of Luck. 

And just as luck would have it, Lyra and her allies also show up. 

Adabra opens her door and smiles. I am furious, so I throw out the blind and broad accusations, “What the hell\! What was that\!”  

The kind woman suddenly becomes cold. And then, with a very patronizing tone, she speaks slowly, her very words dripping with contempt, “I had such hope for you, Soma. I thought you heard the true whisper of the word. But you let the interventionists,” and she waves her hand at Lyra,” fill your head with the noise. They speak of cleansing and planar infections because they fear what they cannot control. You help them wound the world to make themselves feel righteous.” 

I stutter, “We didn’t help them.” I feel chastised and remember that we didn’t actually complete the ritual, and in some sense, I could argue that the ritual failed because of me, “You could say we interrupted the interventionists.” 

Adabra is confused. She points at Lyra and asks, “Then why are they here?” 

I explain that we saved them and add, “They didn’t deserve to die.” 

The old woman is suspicious and asks if we attempted to stop the ritual. I try to lie. I try to deceive her, but I am too old for those games that the hatchlings play. 

I then look at Vukradin and think that the silver-tongued bard should talk to her. He can convince anyone of anything. 

And he manages to confuse her just enough for her to forget about any betrayal. 

She asks about the Treant, and VUkradin smiles and says, “He’s in stable condition.” 

Adabra thinks he’s alive. I know he’s dead. But in stable condition, the condition of being dead. 

Lyra is furious and screams, “You are insane\!” 

Vukradin will have none of it and says, “Let’s calm down. Do you want a beer?” 

I turn to Vukradin, “Can you tell Adabra that not all is lost? Everyone got something from this. The planar rift was not sealed. The interventionists are alive.” 

Vukradin smiles and goes to work. And soon, Adabra and Lyra are no longer about to come to blows. 

When Lyra is about to go on some long-winded rant, Vukradin again manages to deflect her need to speak and says, “Let’s go listen to some music? Wouldn’t that be nice?” 

But this time, Lyra is fed up and shouts, “If you cannot see the evil this woman represents and her fanaticism and its danger, then you are all fools.” 

### Vukradin

### 

I just want to be friends with everyone. I don’t understand why people are so full of hate. I mean, I used to think that the key to everything was to play music that was better than my audience, but now I realize that playing popular music is the way to their hearts. And I just want them to love me. 

And this hate between Lyra and Adabra is making it hard for me to be friends with everyone. I mean I want to work with everyone, except the Zhentarim. 

The Zhentarim don’t quite fit with my personal goals of only making money from conflict-free treasure. 

As I watch Lyra leave, and her agent follow her, I grab one and ask if he could stay and get a drink. 

“I can’t stay now, but we’ll be back later if you are buying.” 

I nod. I know that Spiderman will pay. 

Adabra is still there and mutters, “The interventionists started this war on nature when they chose to reject the multiverse's diversity. Their rituals and cleanses are acts of violence.”

I can’t stand it, and shout, “Boooring.” 

She looks at me. 

“Boring.” 

She glares. 

“Everyone is so serious\!” 

Adabra is taken aback, and she decides to try a different take, “You like music?   And isn't music supposed to be an explosion of insight and beauty that reaches from any point in the universe? Is there bad music? There's only music? You understand, Vukradin, what the interventionists believe is that some music is bad, just because they don't like it.”

Valphine smiles that peculiar smile of hers, “It’s bad because it crosses planar boundaries\!” 

Poor Adabra, she might have had a point a few days ago, but I have learned that playing music that people love and enjoy is the essential art of music. 

“There is music that is bad,” I explain. 

Soma nods her head in agreement and adds, “Like, there are some planes that are bad for this one.” 

The  Vukradin charm worked its magic. Adabra is smiling at me as she says, “There is hope for you, Vukradin, and perhaps even Soma?” 

Soma shakes her head. 

Seeing the opening, I ask her why he and his fellow adventurers were dragged into this internal feud between the members of the Emerald Enclave.  

What I gather from her talk is that among the Emerald Enclave, calling anyone an interventionist is the worst thing you can call them because every member thinks of themselves as preserving nature, not intervening in nature. 

And then it hits me that I don’t want to be an interventionist. Being an interventionist means I have fewer fans.

And then Adabra starts talking again, it's boring, but I am bored, so I hear say, “Those of us who see the true harm of the interventionists have been reduced to begging for crumbs of resources, for being cast out and treated like we are mad and do not understand what is going on. I was forced, forced\! To reach out to any ally I could find, and I thought that you and Soma understood the universal beauty of the multiverse.”  

She stops. 

“I was wrong about Soma, but perhaps I was right about you.”

I look at her and realize she misunderstood what I meant, but why be unpleasant and make an enemy out of a potential fan? I turn to her and say, “I am going to go play some music, will you join us?”  

She sighs, and before she leaves, she asks, “Is the treant still there?”

Soma is trying hard to say nothing. And I realize she’s waiting for me to say something. Should I say a half-truth, “It’s still there?” No, that would provoke too many follow-ups. I will tell her the truth: “I’m not really sure what happened. There was a massive planar tear, and it was right in the middle of that tear in the fabric.” 

Valphine jumps in, “And there was that creature.” 

Brilliant, the comment distracts her from her line of inquiry. After Valphine explains the strange nature of the creature and its behavior, Adabra appears to be in ecstasy and says, “ A symptom of the process, not the cause. It was likely drawn to the energy, a planar magpie. Did it… did you kill it too? Or did you stop to wonder what its purpose was in the grand design?” 

I look at her and say, “We didn’t intervene.” 

Adabra is pleased and hands me five conflict-free gold pieces. 

She continues for some time in some kind of ecstasy, saying words that mean nothing, about planes, and designs, and universalism, and I am bored. Soma later on tells me that she was spouting nonsens that was meaningless. I try hard not to say, “Well, I already knew that.” 

### Valphine 

Adabra is a zealot. I have seen her kind and how they worship Lolth, convinced that there is some greater purpose to the universe, that the demonic powers they worship will grant them power, or that by serving them, they are serving some enduring power. 

Fools.

The only such thing is Lathander. A god who, every day, shows the glory of his power by pushing the darkness away. A god so powerful that he keeps the Drow underground. A god who causes so much exquisite pain.  

Vukradin turns to no one in particular and mutters, “If we sell these pelts, she’ll know what we did, but we need the money.” 

As we stand there in the main square, Corbin turns to Vukradin to offer to find a fence for the pelts. But Vukradin is not swayed. The ex-bandit swears it won’t be blood money, but the bard is not convinced.  

The Bard and his need for clean money, as if there is any money that is not dripping in blood. 

We decide to go to Barthen’s provisions. Barthen looks over the goods and offers a paltry sum. He doesn’t see the value that Vukradin sees. 

So we try the Lionshield Coster. There, we get a different reception from Linene Graywind, the 35-year-old woman manager. She looks pretty excited and asks us questions after question, trying to understand where we found it and how we found it. Vukradin is a fool and answers truthfully. This information could be ransomed for gold.  

Nonetheless, Linene offers us more than twice what Barthen offered. That is strange. I recall reading in Neverwinter that the merchants had an interplanar device that enabled them to ship goods, but it somehow stopped working.  

I am tempted to try to bargain some more, but Vukradin agrees to the price. 

He will never own a music studio, given how freely he shares information. 

Linene takes the pelts and says how her bosses will be very interested in this particular planar effect. 

As we leave, Linene asks whether we will perform tonight. Vukradin nods. And then she asks, much to my gratification, if I will play because she feels that it is I who makes the ensemble shine. 

Vukradin is annoyed and then remembers his goal to be beloved by all; why argue when you can say yes and profit? 

At the Stonehill Tavern, our music set is \- off. I am, of course, perfect, but Vukradin is just not getting his vibe right. I wonder if it’s because he has lost his edge, and has now just become another people pleaser? 

After a few more songs, which are received well, but not as well as the last time we played, the members of the Emerald Enclave arrive and sit with us. 

They sit at our table after Vukradin invites them. And then he starts with the questions, “So are you sticking with Lyra?” 

The oldest among them drinks from the beer that Spiderman has brought to us and says, “ We've sworn our lives and our honor to the Emerald Enclave, and Lyra was just upset. The quest… her goal was to save the tree and to save the forest, and all that happened was… and that all failed, and she is very …” 

###  Soma 

The agents of the Emerald Enclave are next to me. These are the paragons that Meril told me protected the forests and the swamps.  

I must learn more. “What was riding on this ritual?” 

Vukradin asks before he drinks a gulp, “Was it for like a promotion?” 

The first of the agents, Thomas, nods. 

“Power and title are not the way of nature,” I think.   

Vukradin offers to go speak on her behalf, to her superiors, but Thomas says that that would be a bad idea. 

And then he explains, “  She's frustrated because the planar effects have been growing, and she thought this was the source of them, and she thought she could stop them. She discovered this ritual and decided to implement it instead of taking it to her bosses, who might have brought in people who could have performed the ritual.”

Vukradin nods, and I think this is why you must center yourself on nature, not your needs. 

He then adds, “She didn’t do what was best for the Emerald Enclave; she did what was best for her.” 

I am pleased; my teachings must be taking root in this shell sprout. 

Then the other one, Marian adds, “ Look, she's a good boss, she's a good leader, she was… she saw an opportunity. She thought she was going to do something right; she saw everybody in the Emerald Enclave arguing over whether anything could be done or should be done. She said she knew how to make it happen. We trusted her. She's been right in the past. She wasn't this time.” 

I sigh and then add, “You failed without our help.” Everyone looks at me as if I said the wrong thing. 

Then Thomas turns to Valphine, “Have you ever seen rituals as difficult as the one we tried?”

This is not a good thing to ask the Drow. 

And she answers, “ Yes but they involve blood and death and torture.” 

But Thomas is not deterred. “Are you from the Underdark?” 

Valphine nods, “Why yes, from Menzoberanzan.” 

“And a cleric of Lathander?”

“Oh yes, he’s great. See, I came up here to the Overbright to escape a minor demon incursion that was in danger of leveling the city of Menzoberanzan. It seemed like a good idea to take some time off. And I came up here, and it was so bright\! The sun burned my eyes and I said wow, this is great\!” She pauses and has this weird look on her face where she stares up into the sun and has an expression of pure pain, “The glorious painful brilliance of his light. It’s fantastic. I plan to stay in the Overbright for a while.” 

Marian, who is sitting next to me, asks, “Do you harm yourself and then use healing spells as if it was some kind of ritual?”  
Now, I am curious. I have noticed how Valphine does this and have wondered about its significance. 

“It’s not a ritual?” she asks, confused. “Is that not typical in the Overbright?” 

Thomas is shocked, “Do you do this to yourself or to other people?” 

Valphine is confused about the question, “To whoever wants it.”  

I want to learn more about the Enclave, and so I ask, “Why did she choose something so hard?’ 

Thomas looks at me, in pain. “  The Emerald Enclave was arguing over whether we should do anything.”

I sigh, “Obviously.” 

Thomas tips his beer to me and continues, “ There were some who said we should intervene, and some who said that we were intervention intervening, and others who said that we should give it more time.

But Lyra had a particular affection for the Whispering Grove.

And she was frustrated that nobody was doing anything, and so she discovered this ritual.

And then when she discovered the ritual, she came to us and said we should do it. We tried to explain to her that it was difficult, but she said that the forest and the gods would protect us, and that we would succeed.”

He drinks, “but we didn’t and almost died.”

And with that, Thomas turns to Marian and they get up and leave. As they leave, Vukradins ask if they would like to join our group. For a moment, they consider it and then say, “Our oath binds us.”


### Vukradin

Ser Kaelen approaches. And he asks me about my work and my plans. And I don’t trust him. I ask him about any information he might have about the rightful owner of our treasures from the ship, and he sighs and mutters something about how hard it is. 

I am unconvinced. 

He does praise my goal, and I am pleased. It’s essential for everyone to like me. 

We talk about my music for the Order of the Gauntlet, and I try and explain to him that these things cannot be rushed. 

And that anything I come up with will be better than what he shared.

# Chapter 13 The Butterskull Quest 

## 1-02 Taraskh 1495

### 

### Valphine  

The night before, we discussed our plans in private. We had no intention of being ambushed again. 

We decided to go to Butterskull Ranch. Harbin had posted this quest on his quest board, “Orcs have attacked Butterskull Ranch, five miles east of Conyberry along the Triboar Trail\! Travel there with haste, assess the damage, and help any way you can. Ranch owner Alfonse, also known as Big Al Kalazorn, is a retired sheriff who can reward you for your efforts. If he’s dead, return to Townmaster Harbin Wester with proof of Kalazorn’s demise to receive a reward of 100 gp.”

The money was good. And if I read the quest correctly, we could go there, discover the Ranch Owner was dead, do nothing more, and collect 100 GP for our troubles. 

And so we leave Phandalin and head east on the Triboar Trail. 

Along the way, we encounter a merchant. The merchant is friendly and annoyed. We ask him where he is coming from and he says the town of Coneyberry. 

“The place was ransacked and a wreck. Nothing was there. Lord Neverember promised to fix that.” 

The story of ransacked buildings excites Vukradin who sees yet another opportunity to go on a quest, “We must go there and quickly secure the property.” 

The merchant tries hard not to laugh, “The place was destroyed years ago, and the town is utterly looted. There is nothing to secure.” 

“We have to go find out whose property was stolen and then find the thieves and then return it to the next of kin\!” 

The merchant stares bug-eyed and mutters, “That’s very hard.” 

And Vukradin, with a theatrical flourish, declares, “Just because something is hard, doesn’t mean we don’t do it\!” 

## 4-02 Taraskh 1495 

As I enter Conyberry, I notice an unsettling silence that hangs in the air, broken only by the whisper of the wind through the hollowed-out homes.  The homes are now little more than skeletal remains. Their roofs have long since collapsed inward, their wooden frames rotted and blackened by fire and time. The wattle-and-daub walls have crumbled to dust, and the streets are overgrown with thorny vines and tenacious weeds. 

And yet, this devastation is nothing compared to what would happen to a noble house that was marked for extinction by the Noble Houses of Menzoberanzan. 

We turn the corner and notice four brigands. Our aide, Corbin, explains that they are members of Carver’s gang. 

Vukradin encourages him to talk to his former gang mates. 

Corbin tries to explain the folly of such an action: “You don’t exactly quit a gang without there being consequences.” 

But Vukradin will have none of that and insists he go. 

# Chapter 14 Freeing Big Al, Petunia and watching a ranch burn

## 4-02 Taraskh 1495 

Soma

Vukradin halts us when we spot four brigands, easily recognizable as Carver’s disposable crew. My sigh is internal, a slow puff of air against the inside of my shell.

“I intend to parley,” Vukradin announces, foolishly.

“You are going to send Corbin, the terrified one?” I ask, though I already know the answer. Corbin, the lost boy, is dispatched as our emissary. He tries to smooth-talk his way in, but his fear is too loud. Predictably, the bandits respond by drawing steel.

Seeing the danger to my bale, I transform into a Brown Bear. I run forward, my powerful form covering the distance. My claws connect with the first bandit. I can feel the blood and life being ripped from him. He dies immediately.

From somewhere I can feel Brewbarry become big. He leverages this new power with primal fury, dispatching another bandit instantly. Good. 

Meanwhile, Vukradin finally uses his magic for utility, putting one bandit to sleep. The last one flees—no one races after him. Too often we have seen people escape only to pursue them and kill them. Killing is necessary, but too much of it disrupts the balance. 

The combat is done. We have a captive, the perfect source of information.

I growl. I wish to speak, but I cannot. Vukradin ties up our prisoner. 

Vukradin wakes the bandit. The bandit opens his eyes, and then I can see the terror that he feels. He looks around and sees that he is surrounded by the menacing bulk of Brewbarry, the heavy shadow of my bear form, and Vukradin’s own dramatic presence.

“Where is the Carver?” Vukradin demands.

The bandit, whose name is Skippy, cracks instantly, terrified and smelling strongly of fear. “I don’t know where the Carver is, but I do know that the Carver has an ambush for you prepared at the Butterskull Ranch”.

Vukradin demands to know how the Carver knew. He always thinks there are spies. 

“It’s boringly predictable,” Skippy whimpers, explaining that the Carver knew that knocking out the ranch would generate a quest, causing adventurers like us to walk right into his trap. He knows that Harbin uses the quest board to hire out adventurers. He further reveals that the ambush is led by Borg the Hammer, a half-orc enforcer. 

Vukradin wants to know how the Carver is so well informed. Skippy is tempted to keep silent, but he looks at the blood on my claws and reconsiders. He tells us that one of Carver’s henchmen is Sister Kayla. She is the Spymaster and runs their spy network in Phandalin.

We need to go quickly. But Vukradins wants to return the prisoner to Phandalin. 

This will take days of travel, and the Borg may lose patience. Vukradin is insistent; he will not allow this poor prisoner just to sit there defenseless. 

Corbin suddenly offers to guard him. And as he eyes the captive, he asks the ridiculous question: “Vukradin, can I take his armor? If I take his armor, is it conflict-free armor?”

I pinch the bridge of my snout. The ongoing debate over loot is tiresome. This armor is clearly "stolen merchandise" if acquired through threat or capture.

Vukradin, naturally,  defends his principles. “We all have our challenges, things that we have to face,” he declares dramatically. “You can’t take the armor. And we have to face the Borg.” 

Corbin wants to hide in his shell. How do these humans survive without a shell? 

But then, perhaps because he has no shell, he comes up with a good idea, “What if I take care of him here?” 

Vukradin is dubious. But Corbin is insistent. The former bandit promises to take care of Skuppy. He promises to take care of him. I can see that Corbin wants revenge. Vukradin notices it as well and tells him that Skippy better not be dead by the time he returns. 

### Vukradin

The air here, near Butterskull Ranch, feels heavy, not with mist but with malice. We bypass the main trail completely, electing for a stealthy approach from the north. I keep my movements precise, channeling my focus—not on the combat I dread, but on the certainty that this is an opportunity for honest coin. This path, guided by information bought with minimal violence, is the artistic path.

We creep past the charred remains of the barn and smithy. Then, scattered near the outbuildings, I see them: dead bodies. Orcs and humans both. They are already swarming with flies. *Plunder.* My gorge rises. This casual aftermath of slaughter is precisely what I despise.

Others would plunder these corpses, and that plunder would help me get the music studio. And here, far from my friends, I could do this and none would be the wiser. Then I correct myself, “It is not what I do in front of anyone that sets my moral compass. But what I do when no one is looking. I will not scavenge them. That is not who I am and it is not my style.” 

My gaze is drawn to the fresh wounds on the orc bodies; they look ripped and mangled, injuries that suggest something much larger and more vicious than human bandits. This confirms Borg the Hammer is here, or something big. 

Quietly, I slip toward the back of the house. As I get there I see more dead orcs. And I also notice open windows.  where the cellar door is. It groans faintly, but my sleight of hand is sufficient. Downstairs, the air is earthy and cool. The cellar is lined with shelves holding peculiar skulls made of butter . But most importantly, there he is: a large figure tied to a chair, a burlap sack covering his head .

I quickly work the ropes.

“Are you Big Al Kalazorn?” I whisper, untying the man.

He is startled, but relieved. He is Alphonse “Big Al” Kalazorn, the man we were sent to find .

“You have a… there's more of you?” he asks, relieved .

“It's a rescue mission,” I assure him .

He explains quickly, his voice hushed and hurried, that a band of orcs first raided the farm, but then the rot-tusk ogres, led by the half-orc Borg the Hammer, showed up, killed the orcs, and seized the property .

“I'm thinking that's what's going on. They kept you alive as the bait, huh?” I muse . Borg intended for the quest to draw us into a trap.

I urge him toward the stairs: we must retreat to where Corbin waits. But Big Al pauses.

“I really need my cow,” he says, utterly distraught . “She's my prized possession. Without her, you know, the Butterskull Ranch is just another…” .

I look at him. A cow. This is the moment for negotiation. “Okay, we can find her cow. She's not here, you just said she's not here, so we need to leave and go look for her,” I decide .

The reward is astonishing. “I got this suit of mithril chainmail that I'm willing to exchange for… You finding my cow,” he offers .

This is substantial, legitimate coin. I must verify its source. “How did you come by Mithril Chain Mail, Big Al?” I press .

“I bought it,” he insists. He explains that he’s a retired veteran and former Sheriff of Triboar, the predecessor to Darathra. The chainmail is clean. It is untainted by blood or banditry. Fair trade.

We rush him back to Corbin at our remote camp. We secured the primary objective, but the contract demands the cow.

Soma is immediately practical. “Hey, this is where Speak with Animals might come in handy. I could ask the pigs if they've seen the cow,” she says.

I agree. Within the hour, thanks to her efficient magic, we find the prize: Petunia, bearing the crucial stamp, “Big Al’s Cow.” She is whisked off to safety.

Now, the true objective begins. We retreat to a nearby hill where we can observe.

“I don't think we need to be near… that near the farmhouse, right? Like, we can watch it from…” I mutter, selecting a spot roughly 250 feet away . We wait patiently, prioritizing the hunt for Borg over a direct frontal assault . We are here to track the villain back to his source, not merely crash his party. This caution, this patience, is the price of honest adventuring.

###   Valphine

The wind at Butterskull Ranch chills my skin, a minor irritation that only enhances the strange, glorious discomfort of the Overbright. We have accomplished the mundane task: rescuing Big Al Kalazorn and recovering his absurd, prized cow, Petunia. Now, we settle into the vantage point we chose on the hill, waiting for the true action to begin .

Below us, Borg the Hammer emerges, a large half-orc clad in chainmail and carrying a shield . His armor is efficient, even if his tactics are crude. He finds the pathetic bandit who fled the skirmish at Conyberry—Bandit Number 4—and immediately begins to scream .

I cannot decipher the half-orc's guttural words entirely, but the sheer fury is palpable. This Borg is disciplined, certainly, unlike the chaotic brutes one usually encounters in this crude overworld. He is furious that the adventurers—us—did not walk directly into his predictable trap .

Borg's frustration mounts. He executes the fleeing bandit swiftly, eliminating the problem of incompetence .

He shouts again, and three large figures lumber out of the farmhouse: Rot-Tusk Ogres. They wear the insignia of the tribe we dealt with before, the ones seeking revenge for the ogres we killed previously.

But they do not fight. Instead, Borg engages in something quite delicious: gratuitous violence . He starts looking for a way to burn the place down .

I watch as the farmhouse and the cornfields catch fire. The flames are high, casting a harsh, orange glow across the fields. This is not a necessity; this is rage and destruction. Borg is venting his wrath on the environment because we denied him his intended bloodshed.

Brewbarry murmurs, concerned about chasing the enemy, and asks if we should try to save the farm.

Vukradin, in his ongoing fixation, worries only about the practical reward: “It might affect your chainmail.”.

I stifle a laugh. Mithril chainmail can survive fire . I care little for the ranch's wooden structure, and even less for the sentimentality attached to it. Fire is transformation; it is purification. There is a raw, satisfying power in observing such wanton, efficient destruction .

The destruction is a spectacle—a brutal commentary on the failures of the Carver's plan.

I silently agree with the efficiency. Let the fire burn. It is a glorious, fleeting display. Borg destroys the farmhouse, the cornfields, and the gardens. After engaging in this wanton destruction, the half-orc turns to the three Rot-Tusk Ogres.

He points the ogres in a direction entirely opposite to the direction he takes . He is splitting his forces, a small spark of tactical cunning amidst his rage.

Vukradin speaks for the party: "I think we follow Borg.".

Yes. The trail of the furious Borg is the trail to the true center of the intrigue. The raw scent of rage and the heat of the burning farmhouse are a fine reward for patience. The pursuit of the schemer is always more rewarding than the simple destruction of his tools.

# Chapter 15 Deals with Harbin, and Sister Kayla, and no deal with Jenna

## 7-02 Taraskh 1495 

### 

### Vukradin 

Harbin’s accusations are too much. He has crossed the line. 

As I stand there fuming, I hear Ser Kaelen’s voice \- “I am delighted to see your name cleared. I told Harbin that a member of the Order of the Gauntlet couldn't commit such a crime. The accusation was scandalous\!” 

I roll my eyes and repeat, “Scandalous.” 

Ser Kaelen didn’t or chose not to hear me \- “I insisted we must have a trial immediately so we could clear your name\! The integrity of the Order of the Gauntlet was questioned, and that was unacceptable.” 

“Very,” I sneer. 

He then turns to me, “I am very frustrated with the leadership of this town. Harbin has been a terrible representative of Lord Nevermember. He has made scandalous accusations against members of our order. I believe a town meeting is essential to resolve this\!”

I nod.

Harbin, who is standing near me, interjects with a slight tremor in his voice, “Let’s not be too hasty. I think we could have a polite and civil discussion. Perhaps a town meeting? A gathering to discuss some of the events?” 

He then turns to Ser Kaelen with a hint of steel in his voice, “His Lordship appoints the position of the Town Master, and his Lordship has the privilege and only he has the privilege of replacing me.” 

I can’t believe what I am hearing, “No democracy in Phandolin, that’s an outrage\! Absurd\!” 

I feel the power of the people rising in me, “We need a revolution\!” 

Ser Kaene looked pale and whispered, “Perhaps we should avoid the R word?” 

My honor is besmirched. “All I hear are rumors and innuendos that trash my good name\!” I point my finger at Harbin, “He is using blood money\!” 

The Townmaster turns pale and stutters, “I think there’s been a tiny little misunderstanding.” 

And then Brewbarry, to make Harbin feel even more uncomfortable, steps close to him. 

And with that, Harbin finally proposes something I want to hear: a town meeting where we can make decisions with the principle of one resident, one vote. 

But when I ask for such a meeting to be convened, the Townmaster again finds reasons why it cannot be convened now.   

“Such meetings can not be held from one minute to the next. They need preparations, an agenda, discussion on the format, and pre-work\!” 

He reminds me of many agents that couldn’t find me a gig, and this time I won’t tolerate it. “Now or pitchforks. Do you want pitchforks?” 

I look at him and realize I don’t need him.  

I can convene my own meeting. 

As I prepare to call for a meeting to end the Townmaster, Ser Kaelen pulls me aside. 

He whispers, “Your dedication to participatory democracy, power to the people, opposition to authoritarianism is commendable. But we do live in the real world. And in the real world, Lord Nevermember put Harbin in this position. And if we get rid of Harbin, Lord Nevermember will send the army here. So let’s have our democracy but leave the Townmaster as a puppet.” 

I wanted this so badly, and all I can think to say is, “But he’s corrupt\!” 

Ser Kaelen looks at me and mutters, “A privy council that made the decisions for Harbin would be sufficient, I think.” 

“No, we are going to have a town meeting. I will not have you shut me down,” I declare. 

I march onto the town green and start playing my music. Soon, all of the local notables have arrived: Elmer, the owner of Barthen’s provisions, and his two clerks, Ander and Thistle, and  Halia Thornton, owner of the Miner’s Exchange, Linene, the manager of Lionshield Coster, Spider-Man, and  Adabra. 

There are also many farmers and miners that we haven’t met. But the town isn’t that big, and once you get past 20 or so, there aren’t that many more. 

So I start a concert. And of course, they start chanting for “Valphine.” 

As I look over the crowd, I notice someone I have never seen before. She is staring at me. She is in her late thirties with an air of refined, unassuming elegance. Her auburn hair is tied back in a neat, intricate knot, and her sharp, intelligent green eyes seem to assess everything with a merchant's calculating gaze. She is neither overtly beautiful nor plain; her features are pleasant and easily forgettable. She dresses in the high-quality, practical attire of a traveling guild representative or minor noble. Her doublet of dark green wool is well-tailored, her leather boots are sturdy but stylish, and the simple silver rings on her fingers are her only adornment.

I smile at her. After all, she is a voter. But she just smirks. 

With the crowd warmed up, I am about to give my speech about the people and freedom, when someone screams \- “DRAGON\!” 

“You believe that rumor\! It’s just a form of societal control. There is no dragon,” I shout.

But everyone is running away and pointing behind me. I turn to see what they think is a dragon, but it's actually a dragon.

All I can think is, “I thought that was a rumor.” 

The white dragon is diving towards us. I can only imagine it has one thing in mind: food. 

Harbin starts screaming, “This is what happens when adventurers talk instead of doing their job.” 

I look at him, “And when we kill the dragon, it will be even more proof of how incapable you are at doing your job.” 

I stare at the dragon, and think about how every revolution begins with a heroic and impossible last stand. I think of the ballads that will be written about my heroism and how I stared down this dragon and killed it. I begin to consider a few potential couplets and musical elements, and decide that an opera would be necessary to convey the religious and political energy of this moment fully. 

Only one thought remains, “If I kill the dragon, the fates will prove that I am destined for this change\!” 

Brewbarry stands in front of me and shouts, “I’ll protect you.” And as he watches the dragon fly towards us, he asks, “Should we not ask what he wants?” 

I look at my goliath friend and say, “You are welcome to ask.” 

But I have a different plan. As the Dragon approaches, I cast phantasmal force, creating an illusion that he has a peasant in his claws. 

I hold my breath and then see him fly away. The dragon is convinced he has his prey and, feeling the pain of the spell, decides to go away to eat his phantasmal dinner. 

I hear a voice shout, “I wish to make an announcement.” 

Harbin is about to speak, and so help me. 

But Harbin surprises me as he says, “The recent actions of Vukradin demonstrate the need for a more active and engaged community in our decision-making. Although it is not within my power to make him Townmaster, I do believe that after reviewing our charter, a new privy council consisting of myself, Tolbin, Elmer, Halia, Linene, and Adabra, with our new hero Vukradin, will be just what we need in these perilous times\!” 

I am tempted to just seize the job of Townmaster for myself, and Ser Kaelen can see that. He explains that if we were to do that, then Lord Nevermember would see this as an attack on his authority and send troops to deal with us. Even if we were able to stop the troops, the harm to the town would be considerable. 

For a moment, the nobility of the revolution calls, but then I realize that compromise is also noble. 

### Valphine 

It’s been a long day, waiting for the sun to settle. While Vukradin, Soma, and Brewbarry went into town, I had a different plan. I would stay outside of town with Corbin and Locutus. 

At night, I plan to sneak into town, meet with Sister Kayla, and learn what I can. 

I almost had to change my plans when I heard the roar of a white dragon. I told Corbin to watch Locutus while I ran towards Phandalin. But before I got too far, the Dragon flew away. 

But now it’s nighttime. Before I go, I get rid of any sign of Lathander and look as much the part of a drow of the Underdark as I can. 

The old skin feels uncomfortable and comfortable. 

Locutus looks terrified, and I think, good. 

He takes me to an abandoned and decrepit building at the far edge of the town. We enter. 

Inside the building, and out of the shadows, a woman wearing a dark robe and hood steps forward and whispers, “Who is that?” She is clearly pointing at me. 

I explain, “I am his assistant.” 

She turns to Locutus, “Did you kill Borg?” 

Locutus nods, and I show the head. 

And then, before I can react, she strikes down Locutus. Her first two strikes with her rapier miss, but her offhand dagger cuts the orc down where he stands. 

I am ecstatic. This reminds me of the games I played with my sisters in Menzoberanzaan.  

But then something happens that shocks me, “Now, Valphine, we can have a private conversation.” 

I mumble, “Sure?” 

Sister Kayla removes her hood. I stare at her features. She has cold grey eyes, her hair is cut short, and there is a scar near her neck. Under her cloak, she is wearing some armor, and in her hands are the rapier and the bloodied dagger, which are pointed at me, as a reminder of what the consequences of not agreeing with her might be. 

“I have more important business than working for the Carver,” she begins, and then continues after a pause, “The Borg had one job: kill Big Al. Leave no witnesses. If he had done that, my carefully laid plan would have worked. But he didn’t. And so the plot to blame you for the crime of stealing goods from a merchant has failed.” 

I look at her, “Perhaps you should be working for someone else?” 

She smiles, “The Carver’s gang needs new management.”

I nod. 

And then she says, “I can see that you are exactly the kind of person I can work with.” 

I look at her, confused, and then realize she’s assuming I am a racial stereotype. Much like Locutus, who believed I was going to send him into the Underdark. 

She’s assuming I am a duplicitous, backstabbing, and evil Drow.

How droll. 

She continues, oblivious to my reaction, “I will provide you with information in a few days to kill the Carver’s last lieutenant. I know you are wondering why we don’t kill the Carver. Because he has a very unique ability to communicate with his people, he can communicate with us telepathically. I don’t know how. So we have to weaken him sufficiently so that he comes out of hiding.” 

I recall the stories of mind flayers, aboleths, and eldritch monsters that could do this, and I decide that this is a bridge too far. I am about to tell Sister Kayla that I no longer care to help her in her war with the Carver. 

She again doesn’t sense my misgivings and asks, “Do we have an arrangement?” 

I wonder why she would make this offer, and she explains that the Carver doesn’t like failure,  and she needs to disappear. She made a promise but failed to deliver, and now worries that she will meet the same fate as Borg. 

She notes, “If you kill his lieutenants, then the Carver will come out of hiding, and then I can kill them.” Then she pauses, “And by I, I mean you.” She looks at me, “Those people you are with are they your allies, your dupes? What exactly is your role?” 

I smile, “They are my assistants.” 

Sister Kayla believes me, “Interesting. This whole Lathander thing is a front, right? You are clearly a cleric of Lolth.” 

I glare at her, and she smiles as if she knows something.

 “No need to say anything,” Sister Kayla purrs and then, with more steel in her voice, says, “I know I could get a deal with you. Vukradin was too naive and too simple-minded, Brewbarry was in the bard’s pocket, and as for Soma, she is too full of nature.” She points the bloody dagger at me, “But you? I knew you and I could make a deal.” 

I again glare, impassively. 

She smiles, as if she knows something, a secret we share, and then tosses a bag at my feet, “The money Locutus was going to get for killing Borg, consider it a down payment on our future partnership.” 

And before I can react, she disappears. 

### Brewbarry 

The tavern is a happening place. And the beer tastes good, but the woman who smirked on the green during the dragon attack is still smirking and staring at Vukradin.

The bard needs to be protected from his own best instincts. Too many people here want to harm him. 

I ask Spiderman where she is from, and he explains that she is from Neverwinter. And then he explains that she works for the Lord’s Alliance. 

“How do you know?” I ask. 

“They have a stench, and she carries their insignia,” he whispers.   
I look her over, and whatever secrets she has come spilling out. She has a purpose to negotiate with Vukradin. And she thinks him to be an easy mark.

The Lord’s Alliance was hell bent on destroying my people. And I am tempted to exact my vengeance here, but now this is not the place or time. 

She sees me approach, smiles, and asks, “You are?” 

“Brewbarry,” I growl. 

She speaks softly and quietly to settle me, “Brewbarry. You were a big fan of Vukradin’s in Neverwinter and attended all of his performances?” 

I look at her and nod. 

And then she says something I didn’t expect, “I think his music is fantastic,  and I think it’s a terrible shame that so few people know about it.”

I am confused, “And what do you mean by that? What do you suggest we do about this?” 

She says, soothingly, “I would love an introduction. And I’ve noticed how Vukradin has made new friends.” 

My voice becomes threatening, “Really?” 

Once again, Jenna reassures me, “I’ve heard rumors that she’s a friend of the Order of the Gauntlet.” 

I am very suspicious, “And why do you care?” 

She looks startled, “I hear Vukradin is very committed to conflict-free treasure, and he only wants to work with high-integrity individuals.” 

I nod and say, “That should be the way for all of us\!” 

She smirks, “I am not one to judge. My role in life  is to make sure that the ruling… the law and order and the preservation of society is maintained in the face of chaos and the mayhem that surrounds us all.” 

I know this kind, “To preserve your own interests?” 

The woman is taken aback. “I should not be surprised to hear that from,” she pauses, “Uthgartian.” 

Her tone drips with contempt. Her eyes are full of hate as she tries hard to hide what she feels about people she calls, Barbarians. 

I tense, ready to strike her down, but she recovers. 

She continues trying to pretend the moment never happened, “ At the end of the day, this is not about you and me; this is really about Vukradin and his choices, and it is particularly his choice to work for the Order of the Gauntlet. Did he not receive a letter recently?”

I look at her, convinced that she is up to no good, “How would that be any of your business?”

She laughs, “Because I sent it.” 

I am confused, “Why did you ask if you sent it?” 

She sighs, “ Because I still see him working with Ser Kaelen, and it's my understanding that Vukradin is against people whose money is blood money. And I'm sure that even Vukradin, in his infinite musical wisdom, would know that House Margaster is only blood money.” 

She then turns to me, “I was going to approach him, but since you came here, and are his biggest fan, I thought you could make the introduction.” 

I look at her, “Hand over your weapons.” 

And she smiles and drops a rapier, a dagger, another dagger, and a hand-held crossbow on the table. 

She then says in a soft, gentle tone, “ I am not here to commit acts of violence, Uthgartian; I am here to have a pleasant conversation with Vukradin.” 

When I make a point about her weapons, she points at my halberd. I nod and bring the bard to the table.

Vukradin is doubtful at first, but when he hears that he wrote the mysterious letter, he insists. 

Before he reaches the table, I tell him that I disarmed her. 

### Vukradin 

I thank Brewbarry for his actions and remind him that a tavern is an unlikely place for violence. He seems unconvinced and says, “I like your confidence.” 

He tells me that she loved my music and that I should be more famous than I am. 

At that, I am enthused, “Yes, let’s go talk to her.” 

After I sit down, get a beer from Spiderman, and introduce myself, she explains, “I have been looking all over for you.” 

I tell her that I got her letter. 

She looks at me, confused, “I must say, given your well-known position on immoral money, I was shocked to find out that you continue to work for the Order of the Gauntlet.” 

I turn to her, “Define work for?”  

She looks at me, confused, “Ser Kaelen here says that you are a full-fledged member of the Order of the Gauntlet.” 

I sip my beer, “I am friends with many people. I have many fans. I spread my music to all types.” I stare at her, “Are you interested in my music? I am sure your organization would love to hear my music.” 

She seems confused, and I continue, “I want to be friends with everyone.” 

And then she splutters, “But Ser Kaelen insists that you are a member of the Order of the Gauntlet. Am I to understand that this is not an exclusive arrangement with the Order?” 

I stop her, “Is this a trial?” 

She looks at me, perplexed as if this idea that I might not commit to one group is as strange as anything in this world. 

I explain, “I’m  just trying to make organic fair trade gold so that I can start my music studio and spread my music to all of Faerun. That's my goal here, and I'm happy to be friends with lots of people, as long as they're not evil, and as long as we earn our money fairly and justly.”

She smiles, finally, and begins, “ I'm very relieved to find out that you haven't embraced Ser Kaelen's philosophy wholeheartedly and without any reservation, but instead take the very appropriate musical point of view of wanting to work with everyone, to bring your music to everyone.”
