There was a history there. Whatever it was, it lived in the space between them like a held breath.

Kaella started screaming the moment we were clear of the tent.

Vukradin called back something reassuring about Corbin being a friend, a former colleague, that the two of them would get along perfectly. I said: "I've been teaching him some of our sacraments."

I did not explain which sacraments. Some things are better left as questions.

I walked on. Behind us, the screaming continued.

Life, as I have always found, is pain. The distribution of it is simply a matter of arrangement.

---

## **Vukradin — Planning the Journey to Ice Spire Hold**

The two paths to Ice Spire Hold were not complicated mathematics. Fifty-five miles through mountain terrain as the crow flies. Cutting directly would cost one level of exhaustion. Swinging through Phandalin first would cost two. You could dress it up in heroic language — *rally reinforcements, shore up the town's defenses, make our farewells* — but what you were really doing, if you went home first, was arriving at a dragon's stronghold already half-dead.

I am many things. I am not that.

The second consideration — and I did not say this aloud, not precisely — was what it would look like to my opponents on the Privy Council. I had only recently learned I *had* opponents, which I will admit surprised me more than it should have. Of course I had opponents. A man who refuses blood money and says so at volume, in public, at council meetings, will accumulate opponents the way a traveling musician accumulates drink tabs. But knowing this in the abstract and feeling the specific weight of *they will use every delay against you* are different things. A detour through Phandalin was a detour through their rhetoric. I could already hear it: *Vukradin abandoned his post. Vukradin left the dragon problem to fester while he came home for a hot meal.* No. We would go directly.

That left the problem of arriving exhausted, which I solved with what I consider a masterwork of diplomatic persuasion, even if Xanth made it easier than it should have been.

---

Xanth had opinions about everything, which I respected, and delivered them in three connected stories before arriving at any point, which I found charming the first time and merely characteristic thereafter. He had already warmed to Soma in the way that people warm to Soma — with the helpless adoration of someone who has just realized they are standing near something genuinely good — and the sight of Meril's Staff had affected him in ways he didn't entirely explain, or perhaps explained at length and I stopped following.

"Xanth," I said, when the moment seemed right. "I have a proposition for you."

He regarded me with the particular expression of a person who has heard many propositions and found most of them unworthy.

I laid it out. We needed to reach the foothills. He was fast. His hooves would carry us further and faster than our feet, and he would be spared the mountains entirely — his limits, honestly stated, and I had no intention of insulting him by asking him to climb rock faces. Take us as far as the area near the gold mine. That was all.

Xanth looked at me for a long moment. Then he looked at Soma. Then, with the air of a man who has decided that some companions are worth inconvenience, he agreed.

"Soma, that, that is, I could kiss you," he said, which was technically addressed to Soma but seemed directed at the universe generally. And then, turning back to me: "Vucravinios. You are asking me to be a horse."

"I am asking you to be a *centaur,*" I said, "which is considerably more distinguished."

He was not entirely convinced. But he went along with it, which is the outcome that matters.

---

The transport arrangement, once we worked it through, had a certain ungainly poetry. Xanth would carry myself and Valphine on his back. Soma — and I will never stop finding this remarkable, not because I don't understand wild shape but because understanding a thing and watching a tortle become a bear are entirely different experiences — would carry another rider. Boney the horse, who does not tire, would handle the rest. It was not elegant. It was, however, functional, which is what I needed it to be.

The indignity of being cargo. I have performed for worse audiences.

---

Kaella was the last piece of business before we could leave, and it was the piece I spent the least time examining.

We had her. She was alive, which was more than I had expected after the fight, and she had told us what we needed to know about Ice Spire Hold — or enough of it, the rest being inference and risk. The question was: what do you do with a captured spymaster when you're about to march into a dragon's stronghold and cannot take prisoners with you?

Corbin answered before I finished formulating the question.

"That will be my pleasure," he said, when we told him to watch her.

Then: "Can I beat her?"

I want to be clear that I heard this as a procedural question. A question about parameters. He was asking, I thought, for guidelines — how much force is appropriate if she attempts escape? What are the rules of engagement for a prisoner guard?

"Until she has one hit point," I said.

Kaella started screaming. Not escape-screaming — indignation-screaming, which is a different register entirely, higher and more pointed. She was calling Corbin a lunatic, and then she was calling all of us various things, and I said, with complete sincerity: "Not a lunatic\! He's a friend\! He was part of your crew\! I'm sure the two of you will get along perfectly."

Valphine said, very quietly, "I've been teaching him some of our sacraments."

She said it the way she says most things — with the particular serenity of someone who finds human suffering aesthetically interesting. I have learned not to ask follow-up questions about Valphine's sacraments.

The screaming continued as we walked away. I told myself this was the most humane available option, which I believe to be true, and I did not think about what Corbin's expression had looked like when he said *that will be my pleasure*, which I believe I was right not to examine too closely.

As long as she stays above one hit point. I was quite clear about that.

---

The long rest, at least, was simple.

We settled in. The night was cold in the way that nights near mountains are cold — a specific, personal cold, as though the air has decided you individually and has views about your presence. I kept my instrument near. I did not play anything substantial, just ran my fingers across the strings in the way you do when you're thinking without quite thinking, chord shapes without resolution.

Tomorrow: a centaur ride, a bear, a skeleton horse, and fifty-five miles of mountain between us and a chained dragon and a man who walks without rest or breath or soul.

I have, in my career, played in worse venues. Usually I get a greenroom.

---

## **Soma — The Unnatural Trail**

The wrongness came to me before the trail did.

I've walked old-growth forests that have forgotten human feet. I've passed through groves where the soil remembers every death it's fed on. I know how a healthy place feels — the texture of it, the pressure it puts back against you when you move through it. This was the opposite of that. A negative space. The land pressing inward instead of outward, registering something it could not process.

I didn't say anything to the others. What would I say? *Something is wrong with the air here.* They would have looked at me the way shell sprouts always look when you say a thing they can't verify.

Then we found the footprints.

Human in shape. Perfectly spaced. The trail ran straight through the terrain — over rocks, not around them, through obstacles that any living body would naturally avoid. There was a rhythm to it that made my jaw tighten. Not the rhythm of someone marching with discipline. Something else. The rhythm of a mechanism.

Vukradin said it first, because Vukradin is quick when he wants to be: something about a construct, not a person.

"Or a guy who's under some sort of, like, demonic spell or something," I said. "Like, who's possessed."

I wasn't guessing. I was naming what my skin had been telling me since we turned onto this path.

---

The stone giants came next — a gathering of them in the defile, speaking to each other in low resonant tones that I felt more in my sternum than heard with my ears. Brewbarry and Valphine understood the words. Something wrong with the rocks. The giants had noticed that much.

One of them spoke about the trail-maker without much interest. *The little one with the obsidian sword.* That phrase lodged in me.

Vukradin asked whether they'd tried to stop him.

"He is a little one," came the answer. "Is of no concern of ours."

I had spent enough time watching the party fail to move immovable things to know that arguing with a stone giant's philosophy was like arguing with weather. But I said it anyway.

"I wouldn't be so sure."

Not a lecture. Not a warning with teeth in it. Just — *I wouldn't be so sure.* The kind of thing you say when you've watched enough forests die to know that a disturbance doesn't wait for you to decide it's your concern.

Vukradin tried the diplomatic angle — *is there a way we can help you?* — and the giants did what giants do. They pointed down the path. Their version of help was for us to stop filling their valley with small-creature noise and go deal with the problem ourselves.

"You would no longer be here and not talking to us."

I almost admired it.

---

The staff told me the rest.

I pressed Meril's Staff against one of the displaced rocks — the ones that shimmered faintly, sitting at wrong angles, as if reality had tried to put them back and couldn't quite remember where they'd been. The moment the staff made contact, I understood.

Psychic energy. Not magical distortion in the way a spell leaves a mark — something deeper than that. Something that had moved through here and bent the world around itself without meaning to. Without noticing. Whatever had walked this path had been so thoroughly emptied of ordinary selfhood that it warped the fabric of things simply by passing through them.

"That guy is possessed," I said. "There's… nobody walks like that. Possessed by, I don't know."

I genuinely didn't know. That was the part that sat cold in my chest. I know possessed creatures. I know the signature of a will that isn't its own. This was something past that. Past possession, past corruption, past the kind of thing you can cast out with the right words or the right fire.

The rocks phased along the trail — there, not-there, there again, like a wound that hadn't decided yet whether to close.

---

The giants brought their philosophy to bear on the evidence: *Hekaton has said, and the failure of the breaking of the sundering has shown, that giants must take care of giant things.*

Brewbarry and I both said it, almost at the same moment.

"He will soon."

The giants didn't move. Of course they didn't. They had chosen their stillness the way you choose a wall, and nothing we said was going to change the architecture of a decision that old. Still — they gave us two potions before we left. Which was something. Maybe they heard us, even if they didn't act on it.

---

Vukradin asked one last question before we left the defile. Whether anyone had been with the Carver.

"No," the giants said.

Traveling alone. That landed differently than I expected it to. A thing that warps rocks and walks through obstacles without noticing them, traveling alone through mountain passes, heading toward a dragon.

I thought about the obsidian sword as we moved away from the giants — the words *level-draining* sitting in the back of my mind the way a bad smell sits in a room after you've opened the windows.

"Obsidian Sword, though," I said. "Do we know anything about that?"

Brewbarry and I answered together, more or less: level-draining.

Vukradin said he didn't want to come anywhere near it.

Nobody disagreed with that. Not even me, and I have walked toward most things in my long life without much fuss.

This one, though. This one I was watching my feet for.

---

## **Brewbarry — The Stone Giant's Pass**

The mountain narrows itself like it's trying to squeeze you out. That's what the pass felt like — a place that wanted fewer people in it, ideally none.

I understood the feeling.

The rocks were wrong. Not fallen-wrong or cracked-wrong, just wrong in a way that made the eyes slide away from them, shimmering at their edges like they couldn't fully decide to exist. I've seen a lot of mountains. My people were mountain people, in the sense that mountains were where we ended up when everywhere else had asked us to leave. This kind of rock wasn't anything I'd seen before. It wasn't wind damage or freeze-thaw. It was something that had walked through here and left a mark the stone couldn't shake off.

The Carver's path cut straight over everything. Not around. Not through the easier terrain. Straight over, because whatever was walking didn't need the easier terrain. The footprints in the compacted earth had a rhythm to them like a millstone: even, mechanical, relentless. I looked at those prints for a moment longer than I meant to.

Then I heard the giants.

Stone giant speech sits below hearing and above feeling — it's the frequency of things that don't move unless they choose to. My mother taught me the language when I was small enough to sit in her lap and still have room to spare. She said you learn it with your chest, not your ears. She was right. I heard the rumbling before I understood it, and I understood it before the others knew anyone was speaking at all.

They were troubled. That was the word I kept finding for it, in their language and in mine. The rocks were wrong and the giants knew it, and it had made them gather in the pass like something enormous and immovable that had, nonetheless, decided to worry.

There were several of them. The word "several" feels inadequate. There were several of them the way there are several mountains when you look at a range — you understand the number but the scale defeats the counting.

I told the others what I was hearing. We approached.

The giant who spoke for them delivered information the way they deliver everything: like setting a boulder down. Final. Present. Not up for discussion.

"The little one with the obsidian sword came through this path."

Vukradin took that in. "Did you try to stop him?"

"He is a little one. Is of no concern of ours."

I know that voice. Not from stone giants specifically — from every authority I've ever met that decided something small enough to ignore was small enough to let burn. I didn't say that. I stood behind Vukradin and listened.

Soma said, quietly, "I wouldn't be so sure."

Vukradin tried a different angle. "Is there a way we can help you?"

The giant pointed. Down the path. The direction we'd already been going. The meaning was plain as the stone his finger was made of.

Vukradin, admirably, pushed it further. "So, how would us dealing with him help you?"

The giant considered this with the patience of something geological. Then:

"You would no longer be here and not talking to us."

I almost laughed. I would have, if we hadn't been asking these beings for passage, possibly for their help, and absolutely for their indulgence in not simply stepping on us. But I recognized the sentiment. My tribe had said gentler versions of it to me more than once, toward the end.

Vukradin pressed on. The dragon, he wanted to know. The white dragon. Didn't that concern them?

The giant who answered had clearly been waiting for someone to ask about history so he could decline to let it change anything.

"When Am broke the sundering, some thought that it was time for giants to seize power over the little ones. Those giants failed, and they were tempted by — and Am was tempted by the power of Tiamat and her plans to rule the world with her dragons. We have learned from Hekaton that now the war with the dragons is over, and the war with the little ones is over."

Valphine, dry as the inside of a tomb: "So you're not interested in fighting dragons."

"Yes. The dragon can stay where the dragon lives, and the dragon does not bother us."

Soma and I said it at the same time. "He will soon."

The giants looked at us. Not moved. Not concerned. The way a cliff face regards the weather.

I know what it is to have a philosophy built from disaster. My exile wasn't a lesson I chose — it was a lesson I survived, and I built rules around it afterward to keep the same thing from happening again. The giants had done the same thing, just with more blood and more centuries and enough failed wars to forge an ironclad theology out of the wreckage. *Giant things. Giant concerns.* Hekaton had said, and they had decided Hekaton was right, and nothing a collection of little ones said in a mountain pass was going to shift what Hekaton had settled.

I understood it. I didn't agree with it. Both things were true at the same time. Both sides simultaneously.

Valphine tried a different approach. Evidence. "The corrupted one seeks to corrupt the dragon."

"Do you have any proof?"

And then Soma pressed Meril's staff against one of the phasing stones.

I wasn't sure what I expected. The stone didn't glow or crack or speak. But something surfaced in it — the truth of what had happened there, readable if you knew how to read it. Psychic residue warped into the rock itself, following the exact line of the Carver's path. The giants leaned in, the way mountains lean in, which is to say the weight of their attention shifted by a degree that felt enormous when you were standing at its base.

They acknowledged what they saw. And then:

"Hekaton has said, and the failure of the breaking of the sundering has shown, that giants must take care of giant things."

Vukradin tried one more thing. Just one more. "Was there anybody else with the little one?"

"No."

The Carver had come through alone. Alone, carrying something made of obsidian, and the rocks had not recovered from where his feet had touched them.

We talked about the sword afterward — Soma and I putting words to what we were both thinking. Level-draining. That was the word. The kind of weapon that takes something from you that healing can't put back. Vukradin said he didn't want to come anywhere near it, and nobody argued, because nobody had anything intelligent to add to that.

The ascent was what the ascent was — the mountain asking for something physical in return for passage. I gave it what it wanted. I have Goliath blood and the Gauntlets of Ogre Power and a life's worth of climbing things that tried to stop me, and the pass got what it deserved in that negotiation, which was nothing. I hauled myself up and kept moving and tried not to look too long at the places where the rocks were still phasing, still uncertain, still marked by the footsteps of something that no longer needed to think about where it walked.

Before we left, one of the giants — without ceremony, without a word — pressed two potions into my hands. Greater healing. Hill giant strength. Wordless. The gift of someone who has nothing left to say but still, at the last moment, remembers what it means to hand something to a traveler going somewhere worse than where they are.

I held them for a moment. The giants' patience had run out long before their generosity, apparently. Or maybe they were the same thing. Maybe the potions were just another form of *you would no longer be here and not talking to us*, delivered with something that looked, if you were being charitable, almost like care.

I put them in my pack.

The pass widened ahead of us. The phasing rocks thinned, and then stopped, and then there was just mountain — old and cold and indifferent in the normal, honest way that mountains are. The kind of indifference you can trust.

Behind us, the giants said nothing.

The Carver's footprints kept going forward, even and mechanical, ahead of us in the stone.

---

## **Valphine — The Glacier of Whispers**

The Glacier of Whispers announced itself before we could see it properly. Something in the air changed — a pressure behind the eyes, a frequency the cold had no right to carry. I noticed it the way I notice most things: catalogued, filed, assessed. The others felt it differently. Brewbarry rolled his massive shoulders as if shaking off a blow that hadn't landed yet. Soma went still in the way she does when the world is speaking to her in a register the rest of us can't quite hear.

Then we crested the rise and I saw the ice.

Violet. Not the pale lavender of certain mushroom caves in the Underdark, not the bruised blue of deep glacial pressure — this was a deliberate, saturated violet, the color of something that should not be a color for ice. And in it, faces. Dozens of them, perhaps more, pressed against the inner surface of the glacier from whatever space exists on the other side of frozen stone. Mouths open. Eyes wide. Every one of them screaming in absolute silence.

I stood there for a moment longer than was strategically necessary. I noticed that about myself.

I know what it is to be trapped in darkness. The Underdark does not offer the mercy of unconsciousness — it keeps you precisely aware of your imprisonment, aware of every wall, every weight of stone above you, every absence of light. I left. Most of those who serve Lolth do not. What they become, pressed into that service for decades, for centuries, while something vast and cold and indifferent decides whether they are useful or merely dead — I know the shape of that. I recognized something in those faces that I would rather not have recognized.

I have spent some time cultivating the discipline of moving past recognition into analysis.

The Carver's footprints crossed the glacier in a straight line. That was the detail that settled everything into clarity. Not a path. Not the wandering track of someone picking their way across treacherous ice. A line. The same inhuman geometry we had been following since the foothills — over rock, over ice, never around any obstacle, as if the concept of *around* had been removed from whatever was making these decisions. Each print carried a faint psychic residue that the violet light seemed to feed on.

The whispers arrived when I was still studying the footprints. They did not ask permission. They simply appeared in the space behind conscious thought, the way certain prayers do, the way certain threats do.

*He walks without rest, without breath, without soul.*

Not metaphor. Not poetic warning. A technical description.

I carry the knowledge that there are forms of possession which unmake the host entirely — where the driver inhabits the body but the original inhabitant is simply gone, not imprisoned, not screaming somewhere in the architecture of their own skull, but *absent*, the way a room is absent of furniture. This was that. The footprints confirmed it. The whispers confirmed it. Soma had said it with her quiet druidic certainty — *that guy is possessed* — and she was right, she was usually right about such things, but she had said it with the concern of someone who hoped there might still be a person inside to save.

I did not think there was a person inside to save.

Something surfaced in me then — not memory exactly, more the sensation of a door I had mentally labeled *later* opening without my permission. My priestly training. There had been something, some particular doctrine about this region, about Lathander's light and its relationship to psychic resonance, to the whispers that cling to old wounds in the landscape. I had considered it peripheral at the time. The kind of local legend that priests embroider their sermons with when the fundamental theology isn't sufficiently interesting. I had paid it the attention it deserved, which was partial.

Standing at the edge of a haunted violet glacier, with silent screaming faces pressing against the ice on all sides and a psychic impression of something soulless walking through everything that had ever lived here, I understood that my teachers had not been speaking in metaphor.

Lathander's light, specifically invoked, specifically directed — not as comfort but as *ward* — could hold the psychic pressure at bay. Grant the mind a clarity that the glacier's resonance could not penetrate. For ten minutes. A window.

*This is just some new thing that I can do?*

I had said it aloud before I considered whether to. The GM — the circumstances — had seen fit to hand me a tool I didn't know I'd been carrying, and my first reaction was taxonomic. *Is this new, or did I simply not know?* There is a distinction. I find the distinction matters to me more than it perhaps should, this question of what I have always been capable of and simply failed to examine.

The glacier did not give us time for extended philosophical inquiry.

We crossed. The party made their peace with the whispers through whatever resource each of them calls on under pressure — Brewbarry through some combination of stubbornness and skull thickness, Vukradin through the particular furious clarity that his principles produce when under assault, Soma through that deep rootedness that I have never fully understood and have stopped trying to deconstruct. I kept Lathander's blessing held in reserve, present in my hands the way a weapon is present, unused but drawn, watching for the moment the glacier found a crack in someone's resolve.

It didn't come. The party held.

I emerged on the other side with the faces still burning in my peripheral vision. Not literally — they were behind us now, receding in the violet ice, their silence permanent and uninterruptible. But certain images settle into the archive and do not fade on request.

I know what it is to be trapped in darkness. I do not think I will forget what it looks like from the outside.

---

## **Vukradin — The Drake's Lair**

The young one lay still in the snow, and I knew before I reached it what the Carver had done.

You see, most creatures — most *people*, even — will walk around a thing in their path. A nest. A territorial beast. A mother's lair. You go around. You respect the inconvenience of life. But the Carver had not gone around. He had walked in a straight line, as something walks when it has already decided that nothing in front of it has standing to matter, and where the young drake had been sleeping the snow was black and the eye sockets were hollow and the cold had settled into the stillness with that particular finality that means: this happened quickly, and with no hesitation, and nothing was afraid. Only the victim, I suppose, and only briefly.

I stood there longer than I meant to.

Above us, the mother circled. She had come home to this — her young dead, the pass filled with strangers, the thing that had done it long since vanished — and she was describing the same arc over and over, those wide, searching loops that are not really hunting, that are something older and worse than hunting. Her cries came back to us off the stone as echoes, so that the grief arrived twice, staggered.

Soma said it quietly, to no one in particular: *"Poor Mother Drake. It was one of your kids that got, you know…"*

"Mothers are resilient," I said.

And Soma — Soma who is always listening to the thing underneath the thing — said simply: *"We are."*

Two words. I felt them land.

I looked back up at the circling drake, and I thought: this creature does not know who we are. She does not know we are not responsible. She does not know we are, in some sense, on her side — that the same creature who killed her young is the reason we are here, cold and improperly dressed, in a mountain pass that smells of necrotic energy. She only knows she came home and the world had been made wrong, and now there are strangers standing over her dead.

And I thought: we do not need to add to this.

"We don't need to fight her," I said.

Brewbarry, to his eternal credit, nodded. He understood immediately.

"Save it," I said. "Save it for the real guy."

There is a thing I have noticed about violence: it is very easy to use it as punctuation. A creature circles, presents a threat, and the combat instinct fires before anyone has asked whether the situation requires it. The Carver had killed without hesitation; I saw no particular reason to prove that we shared the reflex. There was a creature in pain above us. There was a child lying still below. The appropriate response was not another addition to what the Carver had already done here.

I raised my instrument.

I want to be precise about this: I did not play something cheerful. I did not play something that tried to explain or fix or reframe. A grieving mother does not need to be persuaded that things will be alright. She knows they won't. The young is dead. That is the situation and it will remain the situation. What I played instead was something that simply... named the shape of it. The Moonlight Sonata has a patience to it — it does not rush toward resolution, it sits with the difficulty of a thing and lets the phrases fall like stones settling into water. It does not argue with grief. It says: yes, this is heavy, here, I'll hold the other end.

And then, without me asking — which is the only way Brewbarry does anything, I've noticed — he joined in. I don't know exactly what he contributed, because I was watching the drake, not him, but I felt it. Two voices in the cold. Two unlikely figures standing in a mountain pass over a dead young drake, making the only offering we actually had.

The circling slowed.

She came down in widening descents, the way a bird comes down when it has decided something is safe but has not quite finished deciding. She landed some distance from us, and she looked at the young one, and she lowered herself to the snow beside it.

She let us pass.

I kept playing until we were well clear of the lair, until the echoes stopped coming back, until the mountain had swallowed the sound. Then I put the instrument away and walked on without saying anything, which is unusual for me.

The Carver had made a straight line through that lair without breaking stride. We had stopped. We had played. We had let a mother have her grief without adding to it.

I am a politician now, apparently, which means I am supposed to think about what things cost and what they return. But some things you do not do because they are useful. Some things you do because the alternative is to be the kind of person who keeps walking.

I know what kind of person keeps walking in a straight line through a living thing's home.

I would like, very much, to remain a different kind of person.

---

## **Soma — Carving a Path**

The wall stopped us cold.

Not metaphorically. Literally. Boulders the size of houses, seamed with packed ice, filling the path from cliff face to cliff face like the mountain had simply decided we were done. I looked left. I looked right. I looked up, briefly, in case there was something obvious I was missing. There wasn't. Just more rock. More ice. The wind off the glacier cutting through every gap in my shell that I'd forgotten I had.

The others were doing their own calculations. Brewbarry sized it up the way he sizes everything up — as a thing to be hit. Vukradin was probably already composing something about it. Valphine looked at it the way she looks at everything that inconveniences her: like it had personally offended Lathander.

I crouched down.

This is the part nobody sees, when I do what I do. The part that isn't dramatic. I pressed my awareness down through the frozen surface, through the ice, through the cold-hardened dirt beneath it, looking for the seam. The thread. There's always a thread — some place where the stone is sitting against other stone rather than fused to it, where centuries of settling have left the barest memory of give. The mountain isn't solid. Nothing is solid if you know how to listen.

I found it. Barely. A thin tongue of workable earth running beneath the base of the largest boulder, deep enough that the ice hadn't locked it completely.

*Enough,* I thought. *Maybe.*

"I'm trying to carve a structure out of it," I said, to whoever was listening. "Use Mold Earth to pull things around — move the stones aside."

I didn't explain further. There wasn't much else to say. Either the path was there to be made, or it wasn't, and talking about it wasn't going to change which one was true.

I pressed my hands flat against the stone and let the working begin. This is the part that feels like borrowing. Like reaching into something that belongs to itself and asking — not demanding, never demanding — *can I?* The earth doesn't always answer the same way twice. On good ground, in living forest, it's almost eager. Up here, on a frozen mountain that has never cared about anything smaller than a glacier, it was more like asking a sleeping giant to shift. Politely. With no guarantee it would bother waking.

The stone moved. Grudgingly. An inch, then another.

Vukradin called out something about bardic inspiration — that particular kind of energy he broadcasts when he wants you to know he's paying attention.

"Well, I don't need it," I said, without looking up. "I got it on its own."

Whether that was confidence or stubbornness I couldn't have told you. I was forty-five years old and I had moved earth in harder places than this. I had been cold before. My hands know stone the way they know my own shell — not as something foreign, but as something continuous. The same stuff. Rearranged.

The boulder shifted another inch.

I am old, and I have seen much, and I am not going to die today. Not on a mountain. Not before we find out what comes next.

My hands stayed flat against the stone. The cold moved up through my fingers and into my wrists and I let it, because that's how this works — you have to be of the same temperature, the same stubbornness, the same patience as the thing you're asking to change.

The mountain had not answered yet.

I waited.

# Chapter 34 The Carver is not the Carver

---

## Vukradin — The Ascent to Icespire Hold

The mountain had been watching us for weeks.

From Phandalin, on the clear days when the light came in low and golden from the east, you could see it — a white peak at the edge of everything, patient and permanent, the kind of landmark that makes you feel simultaneously small and oriented. I had looked at it from the market square, from the Privy Council steps, from the window of the inn when I was working on a new piece and needed something to rest my eyes against. I had looked at it so many times that when we finally began the ascent, the recognition that landed in my chest had a quality almost like reunion.

"So we've seen it before," I said, more to myself than anyone. "Oh. Interesting."

Soma looked up from the path ahead of her, steady and unhurried as she always is. "We can see it from Phandalin."

"It's like Mount Hotenow," I said.

I meant it as a compliment. I meant: it is one of those mountains that is so perfectly itself that it becomes a symbol — not just a peak but a reference point, a fixed note in the chord of a landscape. A mountain that gets painted. A mountain that earns its name. The others received this with varying degrees of enthusiasm.

The path was where music becomes necessary, as a practical matter. Silence invites attention to the wind, and the wind at that altitude has opinions about you specifically and they are not flattering. So I began composing as we climbed — a marching song, something in three-four because three-four wants to move forward, a little push built into every measure. I was still working out the bridge when we hit the first stretch of real difficulty and I had to put the composition aside to concentrate on not falling.

"Brr," I said, which is not a line from any of the songs I have written, but which felt true in a way that the most carefully constructed lyric could not have matched in that moment.

The cold was a living thing up there. It had texture. It came from below as much as above, up through the soles of boots and into the ankles, and it came sideways on the wind with a persistence that felt almost personal. I looked at Brewbarry, who was moving with the particular unconcerned ease of a man whose relationship with discomfort is a casual and largely unthreatening one.

"That won't be a problem for Brewbarry," I noted. "He'll be fine."

He appeared not to hear me, or to need the observation confirmed. Either way, he kept moving.

We found our footing on the path — that is the best way I can describe it, the way the group settled into a rhythm, each person contributing the thing they were best at: Soma's patient attentiveness to the terrain, Valphine's navigation, Brewbarry's absolute immunity to the conditions that were making the rest of us miserable. The exhaustion that should have accumulated on a climb like that somehow did not grip us the way it might have. I like to think the song helped. I was still humming the verse when the fortress came into view.

Two structures. Joined by a stone bridge. Stone and ice and altitude, exactly as ancient and indifferent as it looked from Phandalin, except now we were standing in front of it rather than half a region away. Icespire Hold. I had a moment of the sort of feeling that does not translate well into words — the gap between a thing seen at a distance and a thing seen close up, the way the imagination fills in details that the reality then replaces. I had imagined something forbidding and dramatic. The reality was more than that. It was simply there, the way mountains are simply there, beyond the human vocabulary of threat or welcome.

The alternative route presented itself as a two-hundred-foot wall of sheer ice rising alongside the main path.

"That appears to be the only safe path," I said, studying the routes available to us. Then, because the question seemed natural: "What are the unsafe paths?"

No one answered in a way that made the ice wall sound appealing, and I took this as useful information. We were not climbing the ice wall. This seemed like a reasonable collective decision.

The rock slide was another matter. A section of the trail — perhaps twenty feet of it — had gone wrong at some point, boulders and rubble collapsed across the path in a way that would have stopped a less determined group.

"There's a rock slide\!" I said, spotting it, which is the kind of announcement that is true and yet somehow insufficient to the situation. The others, to their credit, treated it as a problem to be solved rather than a reason to abandon the enterprise, and in fairly short order we had either climbed over or navigated around the worst of it. Soma, at some point during this process, shifted — there is no other word for what Soma does, and I have watched it many times now and still cannot quite describe it adequately — into the shape of a wolf spider larger than a man. A giant wolf spider, specifically, as she herself would later feel it necessary to clarify, with some dignity.

"I'm a giant wolf spider," she said, navigating the rubble with eight legs and complete competence, "not... it's a medium-sized beast, not a... not a little rinky-dink spider."

The clarification landed in the middle of a rockslide on the slope of a mountain at altitude, and I found that I had no adequate response to it. I filed it away as something that would eventually find its way into a lyric, and kept moving.

We came up over the last rise and the fortress was fully before us. And there, on the roof of the main structure — blending into the snow with a kind of casual mastery, white against white, enormous against enormous — was the dragon.

"Oh," I said. The cold had a new quality suddenly. "You can see the dragon."

I had known, in an abstract sense, that there would be a dragon. This was, after all, Icespire Hold. There is a dragon in the name. But abstract knowledge and the actual sight of the creature were in different categories, and my mind was doing the work of moving the information from one category to the other.

"The dragon has a name?" I asked. "Cryovain?"

"Yeah," said Soma, from somewhere to my left, four of her eight legs braced against a boulder. "It's the first I've heard of it."

Cryovain. It was sleeping. Its scales — white, almost translucent at the edges — were barely distinguishable from the ice beneath it. It was the size of a structure. It was sleeping in the way that large, dangerous things sleep, which is to say with complete apparent unconcern about anything that might be observing it.

I looked at it for a long moment. I thought about the marching song I had been composing on the way up, the three-four rhythm, the verse I hadn't finished.

"The obvious path is a trap," Soma said, in her particular tone of patient certainty.

"I don't know," said Valphine, thoughtful. "The gatehouse just seems so obvious."

We stood there on the edge of the approach, the wind working at us steadily, looking up at the fortress and the sleeping dragon on its roof. From Phandalin, on a clear day, you can see this mountain. I had looked at it dozens of times and thought: *that is far away.* Now I understood that far away and here are the same place, separated only by the willingness to climb.

---

## Valphine — Bypassing the Gatehouse

The approach to Icespire Hold had a certain theatrical quality that I appreciated more than it deserved. The winding path cut straight up to the gatehouse — obvious, exposed, the kind of route that announces itself like a herald with a fanfare. Which is precisely why I suggested we take it.

"Maybe we should just take the obvious path," I said, because someone had to say it, and because watching the others talk themselves out of efficiency has become one of my minor pleasures on long journeys.

Soma, to her credit, had already been thinking further. She always does — methodically, patiently, like someone laying stones. "If we come in from the side entrance, we might actually avoid an encounter or two." She wasn't wrong. She rarely is, on tactical matters. The gatehouse would be watched. Side entrances exist precisely because whoever built the front door expected you to use it.

Vukradin took exactly as long to be convinced as I expected. "Alright. Sold. Do it. Go for it." He commits quickly when the logic lands — it's one of the more functional things about him.

The cliff was perhaps fifty feet of icy rock, which in practical terms means it was not climbable by anything without claws or a rope. Soma looked at it with the calm assessment of someone for whom this is simply a question of which shape fits the problem. "Giant wolf spider, yeah... Skitter up the wall, drop a rope down."

I watched Vukradin's expression shift into the particular configuration that means he has an idea he's very excited about. This is rarely a good sign.

"What if—" he began, with the careful enunciation of someone who knows they're about to be interrupted, "and stick with me for this a minute—I have that spell that can make you see whatever I decide. You think it's real? So we can just have you experience that, and then you will have seen it."

The logic of this deserves a moment's examination, which is more than it merited in practice. He was, as best I could reconstruct it, proposing that Soma could understand spiders more deeply by *believing* she had seen one. As a theological proposition this has some distant merit. As a plan for climbing an icy cliff, it does not.

It didn't work that way. The cliff remained fifty feet tall. Soma remained patient.

"I can't do anything in this campaign," Vukradin said, which is the kind of statement that collapses under its own weight if examined too closely, given that he had just volunteered to solve a physical problem with an illusion. I did not say this. Filing things away without comment is a skill I have been practicing since before his grandparents were born.

Soma's pragmatism cut through cleanly. "I'm not here to piss off the mountain" I had no idea what she meant by that, but the effect was decisive, and Soma began to change.

There is something worth noting about the transformation — the way she simply *becomes* the spider, efficient and without ceremony, without the performance of metamorphosis that you might expect. One moment she is Soma: slow, careful, a tortle with a staff. The next she is eight legs and purpose, moving up the cliff face with the absolute confidence of a creature designed for exactly this surface. I watched her go and felt something adjacent to envy. Not for the shape. For the certainty.

The rope came down. We climbed.

Vukradin, characteristically, composed a song for the occasion. "Up the ice, fire climbing, we will go..." floated down from somewhere above me, and I focused on my handholds and thought about whether there was a theological interpretation of this moment that Lathander would find meaningful. Probably. The light was extraordinary up here — the cold amplified it somehow, everything sharp and precise, the snow catching it and giving it back twice over in ways that hurt beautifully to look at. I didn't mind the climb.

When we reached the top and moved onto the bridge, Vukradin surveyed what we had achieved — the gatehouse below us, the position we had taken — and delivered his assessment with the timing of a man who has thought about worst cases rather more than most.

"Now we will be surrounded on the bridge, and attacked from both sides."

The image of it was so precisely calibrated to every tactical error we had avoided by climbing instead that I almost laughed. He had, without meaning to, described exactly what happens to people who take the obvious path.

"Down the rope\! Down the rope, everybody\! Down the rope\!" He was already, apparently, planning the retreat.

I let the silence sit for a beat and then walked forward and pointed out a path that would avoid getting trapped like duergar during a hunt in Menzoberranzan. The rubble-strewn ground ahead bore the marks of an earthquake — old damage, heaved stone, corridors opened where walls had been. Cryovain had moved through here, that much was clear: a hallway smashed not by time but by something large and deliberate, the stone not crumbled but *broken*, the way things break when something enormous has decided they are in its way.

I catalogued it. The direction of the damage, the scale of it, the way the destruction was oriented. An animal making a path. A large one, moving with the comfort of something that owns this space entirely.

Somewhere ahead, the dragon was waiting. Not for us — it wouldn't know to wait for us yet — but it was there, present in every piece of broken stone, every scorched surface, every silence that felt slightly too heavy to be only cold air.

I find that clarifying.

---

## Soma — Soma Scouts the Roof

The wall was stone, old and rough, and in spider form that was enough. Eight legs found purchase where two never would have, and I pulled myself up the face of the fortress with the patient, methodical efficiency of something that had been climbing things since before the concept of hesitation. The wind off the peaks was cold and sharp. I didn't rush.

I crested the edge and went still.

The roof opened up before me, and what I saw there made the cold feel like a distant, irrelevant thing.

Cryovain. I recognized the shape of a white dragon even like this — even reduced to this. He was chained at the center of a summoning circle, the markings carved or burned into the stone beneath him, and he wasn't moving. Not sleeping. Not still in the way of a creature that has chosen stillness. He was held. His scales had gone the color of old ice left too long in shadow, and his eyes — something had gone wrong with his eyes. The angles of his face were off. The hollows where alertness should live were empty in a way that wasn't unconsciousness.

*Where's his nose,* I thought, and I meant it literally. Something had been taken from him, or something had been done to him that had taken it by degrees.

The Carver stood over him. Performing something. Moving in the way that people move when they are speaking words they believe to be important, and perhaps they are right to believe that. I held myself against the parapet and watched and did not breathe, which is easier when you have as many legs as I did. He didn't look up.

I watched Cryovain long enough to understand what I was seeing. The stillness wasn't resignation. Underneath all of it — underneath the chains and the circle and whatever had been done to hollow his eyes — there was something seething. The kind of rage that has been compressed past the point where it still resembles rage and has become something closer to madness. He wasn't waiting. He wasn't biding time with any intelligence behind it. If those chains came off, he would not think. He would not choose. He would simply destroy everything within reach, and we would be within reach.

I filed that away. Carefully.

The Carver had not seen me. I confirmed this by remaining exactly where I was and continuing to not die, which is my preferred method of confirming these things. Then I climbed back down.

---

When I told the others what I'd seen, Vukradin leaned forward the way he does when he's already building an argument before you've finished speaking.

Vukradin began, "Oh, he has chained the dragon and put it in a summoning circle."

"He's looking very, very zombified," I said. "Like, where's his nose?"

The question stood on its own. Nobody had a good answer for it.

Then came the part I had been thinking about on the way down. "I mean, he's in chains. Are they real chains? Are they, like, spectral chains? I worry that killing the Carver or something would, like, make the chains evaporate."

"Well, we're gonna have to kill the Carver regardless." Vukradin, certain as stone.

"I would like to understand," I said, carefully, "you know — is he load-bearing on the dragon kind of thing."

There was a pause that told me my meaning had landed.

Valphine considered this with that particular expression she wears when she's found an angle. "The enemy of my enemy is my friend."

I looked at her.

"I don't think the dragon's gonna see it that way."

"When the enemy of my enemy is released," she said, with the patience of someone explaining something she considers obvious, "then my enemy does not have the resources he's looking for."

"You'll just," I said. "You'll talk him out of it."

She seemed to take this as a reasonable plan. I let it go.

"Yeah, he doesn't look like a sane fella," I added, which I meant as a footnote to the entire conversation.

Vukradin, undeterred by all of this — and I say this with the weary affection of someone who has watched him be undeterred before — offered: "Actually, after we save the dragon, the dragon may be very grateful."

I thought about those eyes. The thing behind them that wasn't waiting for anything anymore.

"Oh boy," I said. "So, there's a dragon down there that's just kind of being held down. Alright. I guess I relay that to the team."

There was more, though. Someone — I had missed something, apparently, in my look at the chains. Someone who understood such things better than I do worked out what had actually been done to Cryovain.

"Yeah," I was told. "He is. The sword isn't."

The Carver had not simply chained a dragon. He had taken one.

"Oh," I said.

From beside me, Vukradin: "Oh…"

We sat with that for a moment. A dragon — Cryovain, who had terrorized Phandalin, who had been a threat and a monster and a problem to be solved — was somewhere inside that fortress, hollowed out, held down, wearing his own rage like a second set of chains. Not freed. Not tamed. Dominated.

I thought about what would be left of him if we broke those chains. I thought about what he would do to the first thing he could reach.

I thought: *this is going to be complicated.*

---

## Vukradin — Confrontation at the Summoning Circle

The orc quarters hit you before you see them — a wet, charred smell like a fire that gave up halfway through the job, and underneath it something organic and deeply unpleasant. Whatever furniture those orcs had brought in, they'd mostly burned for warmth or out of boredom, and what remained was black-rimmed and collapsed inward. I found myself remembering, almost involuntarily, what I knew of how they'd come to be here in the first place.

"Oh, right," I said, to no one in particular, more to myself than to the party, "and then the dragon came and scared them all out, and then they had to go attack all the people."

Which gave me an idea, the way an unexpected chord change gives you an idea.

"Right," I said. "We need to secure Icespire so the orcs can return safely."

Soma looked at me the way she sometimes does — patient, deeply patient, the way someone looks at a student who is almost there.

"Eastern Homeland Reclaiming," she said.

Exactly. The humanitarian framing was better, actually. More accurate. I filed it away.

Then Valphine found the secret door, because Valphine always finds the thing, and immediately I had the same reaction I always have to a detour when there is a destination already identified.

"Why would you want to go over there?"

The dragon was *right there.* We had just opened the door. The dragon was right there.

"We're going to the dragon," I said, as plainly as I could manage. "It's — it's right there, you just opened the door, it's right there."

Valphine peered through and apparently saw nothing of consequence, because she emerged and said, simply, "Let's go to the dragon."

"We're not fucking around," Soma said, which is not how I would have phrased it, but I appreciated the alignment.

We went to the dragon.

---

I heard the ritual before I understood what I was hearing. The sound of it was wrong in the way that a note struck flat in the middle of a quiet room is wrong — not loud, but *incorrect,* in a way that makes your spine know before your mind does. We came up through the stairs and there was the Carver, in the middle of something dark and deliberate, and there was Cryovain, chained, the great white body of the dragon bound by something I could feel more than see — a magical restraint that vibrated at a frequency that set my teeth together.

Brewbarry didn't need a cue. He went in bellowing, red-eyed and enormous, the rage in him already burning by the time his feet hit the floor.

My hand was already at my voice, the spell already forming, the geometry of it already calculated — *how far, where is the center, does the circle fall cleanly over the ritual's focal point* — and then I cast it.

Silence. Not quiet. *Silence.* The kind that removes all sound from a space entirely, that drops like a dome over the summoning circle and swallows the Carver's working whole.

The ritual sputtered. Choked. The wrong-frequency sound cut off like a string snapped.

"Interrupting the ritual\!" I heard myself announce — to the room, to my companions, to the version of this that would become a song later, I suppose. "I am saving the world\! *Huzzah\!*"

"They were intervening," Soma said, very calmly. "You interventionist."

I accepted this.

Then the Carver moved.

Not stumbled, not panicked — *moved.* With complete precision, a single fluid action, out of the silence zone and into open air without drawing a single attack. Legendary, that footwork. The kind of footwork that tells you everything about what you're actually fighting, if you're paying attention.

I was paying attention.

The arcana knowledge arrived with the clarity of a well-notated score: the black obsidian sword at the Carver's hip was not a weapon. Or rather — it was not *only* a weapon. The man was a vessel. The *sword* was the Carver. The sword had always been the Carver. Everything else was clothing.

I processed this in approximately the time it takes to resolve a fermata.

Before I had finished processing it, I cast Hold Person.

The veteran's body locked. Completely. Mid-motion, mid-breath, the limbs seized and he dropped into stillness the way a marionette drops when you let go of the strings — not collapsed, just *stopped.* There.

"He is paralyzed\! *Excellent\!* Well done, team\!"

And then — "He's paralyzed."

Said again, quieter, because something had changed. Because I had just looked at what was actually in the room and understood what the paralyzed man meant and what it didn't.

The silence spell dropped.

And the sword spoke.

Not through the man. Not through a mouth. Through the space inside my skull, in a voice that had no interest in being pleasant about any of it.

*Coward's magic. You mute the ritual because you lack the strength to shatter the vessel. And now, now I shall have everything I always wanted.*

The chains binding Cryovain dissolved. Not broke — *dissolved,* like they had never had any confidence in their own existence.

"The Carver —" I started. Stopped. Started again. "The sword — the — oh. The Carver is the sword."

"Yeah," Soma said. "The human is just a vessel."

Of course. Of course he was. And the sword — the Carver — had just skipped the ritual entirely, the slow careful consumption it had been building toward, and done the expedient thing instead. Domination. Now. Not controlled, not refined, just — *seized.*

"It's not happy," Soma said, looking at the dragon.

It was not happy. Understatement as a form of art.

"Don't unlock the dragon," Soma said to all of us, unnecessarily, because none of us were reaching for any metaphorical locks, "go, guys —"

"We're gonna talk to it," I said.

Soma looked at me.

"To the dragon," I said.

She continued looking at me.

We were not going to talk to it.

Someone — I think it was a voice from somewhere outside the moment, the information simply arriving fully-formed — made clear that we had three rounds. The sword had seized the dragon but not completely. Three rounds before whatever was left of Cryovain's mind was gone entirely and we were left with a fully-dominated white dragon in an enclosed space.

"Alright," I said. I straightened. There was a silence in me that had nothing to do with the spell — the specific silence of being genuinely, cleanly frightened, the kind I can't talk my way around. I have learned, over time, not to perform composure in those moments. It wastes energy. "That's fine. Let's kill a dragon. It'll be fine."

I looked at Valphine. She was already calculating, I could see it — the way she always is, measuring distance and divine fire and what Lathander's light can do against a thing this large.

"Didn't you always want to kill a dragon?" I said to her. There was something almost genuine in it beneath the bravado. "Isn't this — like, a rite of passage? For the *drow?*"

And somewhere in the back of my mind, in the part that never quite stops thinking about composition, a quiet and slightly horrified note: the black obsidian sword that had been glowing purple and vast with accumulated power was now just — a sword. Black. Still. Like something that had emptied itself out completely to get what it wanted.

It had gotten what it wanted.

---

## Brewbarry — Battle Against Cryovain

The sword was already in my hand before Vukradin said it.

"Brewberry, kill the dragon."

I didn't correct the name. There wasn't time, and also — he could call me whatever he wanted. He always could.

What I needed was the Dragon Slayer Sword. I'd been carrying it for this. For exactly this. The weight of it felt different now, with the dragon filling the sky above the battlements, all white fury and snapping teeth. I could feel the cold coming off it like a wall. I had the sword. That was enough to move.

Valphine had already put something into Cryovain — a bolt of radiant light that hit like a small sun, and I saw the dragon flinch and reorient, its attention briefly scattered. Good. That was the door. Soma was already repositioning — I caught the moment she shifted form, the spider giving way to something larger, heavier, a bear hauling itself into close range. She burned both her remaining wild shapes to do it. I heard her mark it off without complaint. That was worth me sacking around for. That's what she said. I filed it away. Soma never wastes her breath.

I went straight at the dragon.

The first blow landed clean. The Dragon Slayer Sword connected with something that wasn't just scale — there was resistance, and then there wasn't, and I felt the impact travel all the way up my arms in the best way, the way that means you've done something real. The second blow came right after. Also clean. Both of them, landing the way I always hope they land and rarely do. The sword was made for this. I was made for this. In that moment, neither of those facts felt strange.

Cryovain did not appreciate it.

The dragon's head swung toward me. The mouth opened.

The cold that came out of it wasn't like winter. I know winter. I grew up in it. This was something that winter would run from — a cone of frost that hit me full in the face and didn't stop, pushing through my armor, pushing through the rage that was supposed to keep me standing, finding the places underneath where things are softer and hurting them. I didn't go down. I am not saying it didn't hurt. I am saying I didn't go down, and that felt like the most important fact available to me in that moment.

What I noticed, when I could notice things again, was that the dragon was moving.

It beat its wings once and lifted — thirty feet straight up, pulling itself above the battlements, out of easy reach. Out of my reach. I looked up at it. I looked at my sword. I could calculate the distance, and the calculation was not good. I am not a calculator, normally, but I know when something is outside of what I can do, and a thirty-foot leap at a white dragon while I was still feeling the frost settle into my chest — that was outside.

Vukradin had taken cover behind a section of stone wall, tucking himself against it, looking out through one of the arrow slits. I could see his face and I could see what was on it. He was doing the thing he does when he's looking at a problem that doesn't have clean edges. I recognized it. He doesn't like problems without clean edges. He looked at me, he looked at Soma, he looked at whatever Cryovain was doing forty feet above us.

"I got nothing, guys," he said. "Other than Fireball."

Soma said: "You're actually gonna use it?"

"Nope."

I understood that. The fireball was his. It came from him. He'd made that choice about it already, somewhere private, and the choice was no. I didn't argue. I don't argue with Vukradin about what he keeps for himself. He has kept things back from me that he's told other people, and I have kept things back from him that I've told no one, and we understand each other on this.

But I could see him working. His jaw was tight. He was thinking something through.

What he landed on was a thorn whip.

I want to be honest about my first reaction, which was: a thorn whip. Against a dragon that had just breathed frost on me and was now circling thirty feet overhead with contempt in every wingbeat. A thorn whip is something you use on things that are afraid of thorns. I was not confident this dragon was afraid of thorns.

Then it hit.

Not just hit — it was the kind of hit that lands so perfectly that even the thing being hit seems surprised. The whip caught Cryovain somewhere important and the dragon made a sound that was not the sound of something in contempt. It was the sound of something that had just been reminded it could be hurt.

Vukradin went, "I hit\! I even got a critical\!"

He said it the way you say something when you didn't quite believe it was possible and then it happened and now you have to reorganize your face around the new reality. I know that feeling. It's a good feeling.

"Crushed it\!" he said.

I looked up at the dragon. I looked back at Vukradin. I still had the Dragon Slayer Sword. The frost was still settling somewhere in my chest. We had time — not much time, the window was counting down, the Carver was threading something dark into the edges of this fight, but we had *some* time — and Vukradin had just put a thorn through a white dragon's pride with a piece of vine.

I have seen a lot of things. That was a good one.

# Chapter 35 A dragon defeated, a bard tempted, a barbarian's vengeance.

# 

---

## **Brewbarry — The Dragon's Mountain Fortress**

The mountain fortress of Icespire Hold didn't look like somewhere you'd bring a dragon to heel. It looked like somewhere the mountain itself had given up trying to keep the cold out.

We found Cryovain inside a summoning circle on the rooftop battlements — a great ring of burning light, geometric and wrong, the kind of geometry that doesn't belong to any stonecutter or architect I've ever seen. The dragon stood at the center of it. Or was held at the center of it. The distinction mattered, and I wasn't sure which was true.

The Carver was doing something to him. That's the only way I know to say it. The obsidian weapon, the mental amplifier, whatever Vukradin wanted to call it — it was working. Cryovain's eyes had a film over them, a glaze I recognized from men I'd seen after too many days without sleep or water. Presence, but not quite home. He was fighting it. Or he had been. Hard to say how much of the dragon was still left in there.

Cryovain was enraged. Of course he was.

He launched off the stone and climbed the air in one beat of those enormous wings — thirty feet up in an instant, and suddenly my halberd was swinging at nothing. I stood there for a moment with the weapon raised and the wind from his ascent hitting me in the face like an open hand.

Valphine sent a bolt of divine light up after him. It carved a bright line through the cold air and missed. The mountain swallowed the light.

Then Soma did something, and a column of silver moonlight descended out of nowhere, out of sky that didn't have a moon positioned for it — the kind of thing I have learned not to question about Soma. The moonbeam found the dragon and the dragon made a sound that I felt in my back teeth.

That opened something in me. I won't dress it up. I am Goliath. I was built for this.

I grew.

There is no cleaner way to describe what happens when the rage comes properly. I am already large. And then I am more. My reach extended, the battlements shrank, the thirty feet of air between me and the dragon became manageable. I brought the halberd up and then I brought it down, and then again, and then again — each strike finding the place where scales meet scale, where the neck joins the shoulder, where size means you can put weight behind every swing that would stagger a lesser creature. Cryovain was not a lesser creature.

But I hit him hard enough to matter. Several times.

I felt each impact as much as I delivered it. That's the honest account. The cold bites through everything when you're that exposed. Dragon breath is not like standing in bad weather. It reorganizes you. The first time it hit, I understood in a bodily way — not a thinking way, a bodily way — that I was at the edge of what I could absorb. The second time it hit, I was somewhere past that edge, running on a kind of forward momentum that isn't quite decision-making.

My body was doing the math. The math was not comfortable.

Soma, somewhere behind me, was saying don't kill, don't kill — he was just a victim. I heard her. I was not sure what to do with the information in that moment. Cryovain had just breathed ice through me twice and the Carver's influence was still inside him, still pressing, still trying to make the ritual stick. The Carver needed three more rounds. I had fewer rounds left than that if I took another hit like the last one.

This is what the tribe never understood about me. They thought the rage was the absence of thought. They were wrong. The rage is very clear. The rage is math.

The dragon came down. The halberd went in. I don't remember the exact sequence — that's also the honest account. What I remember is the sound the circle made when it broke. The burning geometry guttered and went out, and the Carver's essence — whatever it was, whatever had been reaching through that obsidian instrument into the mind of a white dragon — dissipated. Like breath in cold air. Like it had never been more than that.

---

## **Valphine — The Fall of Cryovain**

The breath comes without warning — or rather, it comes with every warning, because it always comes, because dragons are not subtle — and Cryovain turns that vast pale head and *exhales*, and the world becomes cold and white and wrong. Brewbarry is in the path of it. Of course Brewbarry is in the path of it. I watch the frost pour over him like a wave and I think, with the calm detachment of someone who has already calculated the outcome, that he should not still be standing after that. And yet.

He is still standing.

I have spent enough time around Goliaths now to know that this should surprise me more than it does. It doesn't. I file it under *things that are true about Brewbarry* and move on.

Vukradin is already moving — not in the way he usually moves, which is toward a conversation no one asked him to start, but with real intent, bardic inspiration threading through the space between thought and action like a needle pulling silk. Whatever he does, it lands. The crack of it finds Cryovain somewhere that matters. Above us, Soma's moonbeam has been burning steadily through all of this, patient and relentless, a column of silver fire that does not negotiate.

They have done their part.

The dragon is still standing. Not by much.

I have missed this creature several times today. A fact I am aware of with complete precision, because I track failure the way I track everything: clinically, without sentiment, and without looking away. Lathander's light does not always land where I aim it. I have made my peace with this. The pain of the miss is itself an offering — I mean that literally; I have constructed a theology around it and I stand by the construction — but there is a difference between offering and *purpose*, and purpose has not yet been served.

I raise my hands.

I call the radiance.

The sacred flame comes down.

The number is 3\. Not an impressive number. Not a number that features in triumphant accounts. Three is the number of a thing that barely existed, a flicker, a candle, the minimum viable expression of divine will. Three. I have spent the better part of this fight producing more ambitious numbers than 3 and watching them accomplish nothing, and now here is this small, undeniable, sufficient 3\.

The dragon collapses.

*"It's 3, so he died,"* and yes — yes, that is precisely correct. The dragon died because 3 was what was needed, and 3 was what I had, and there is something almost funny about that if you are the kind of person who finds the gap between effort and outcome funny. I am. I do.

*"And the Carver's essence dissipates, and it too is gone."*

I stand in the silence after that and I look at what remains: the vast cold body of something that was, a moment ago, a threat of cosmological significance, now simply a very large dead thing on a very cold roof. The Carver is gone — not fled, not retreated, *gone*, unmade by the same stroke that unmade its vessel. Two deaths for the price of one flickering, modest, wholly undramatic 3\.

Lathander's will is not always legible. I have made my peace with this also. But sometimes — not often, not as a matter of habit, but *sometimes* — you are given a moment that is not subtle at all, that is in fact the most direct possible statement a god could make, and the statement is this: *I was waiting for the right moment. That was it.* All those misses, all those small humiliations of wasted light, and then this — the final breath of a dying dragon extinguished by the smallest, truest flame I could produce.

I exhale.

The cold bites at my skin, and I let it.Soma — The Fate of the Veteran

The man was already down. Already still. Vukradin's Hold Person had taken the fight out of him completely — he lay there on the stone like something a wave had deposited, a veteran soldier reduced to a pair of open, terrified eyes. You could see him in there. That's the thing nobody mentions when they talk about paralysis. The body stops. The person doesn't.

"Don't kill, don't kill\! He was just a victim\!"

I heard myself say it and I knew, even as the words left my mouth, exactly how much weight they carried. About as much as a dry leaf. I've been traveling with these shell sprouts long enough to know when a decision has already been made by the time someone's asking for opinions.

Still. I put myself on record. That matters to me, even when it changes nothing.

The debate happened the way these things always happen — fast, slightly chaotic, everyone talking past the actual question. Was he a victim? Probably. Most people who end up working for something like the Carver are victims at some stage of the process. That doesn't mean they're safe. That doesn't mean you turn your back on them. But it also doesn't mean you take a halberd to a man who can't move his hands.

I tried to do it differently. I used the bear claws — not to kill, never to kill, just to weaken him further, to remove the possibility of threat without removing the man entirely. Then I moved the moonbeam over him. More pressure. Less life in him. The logic being: if there's less to fear, there's less reason to finish it.

That logic did not survive contact with Brewbarry.

He's not cruel. I want to be clear about that. There is not a drop of cruelty in that Goliath. What there is, is a very long tradition of how combat ends, and a battle-rage that does not consult the options quietly once it's been running. He looked at the paralyzed veteran on the stone and he made the calculation the way he always does — the man was a threat, threats are finished, this is what finishing looks like.

The halberd came down.

"I win, you lose — tradition upheld."

He said it the way you'd say *the sun rises in the east.* Not triumphant, exactly. More like... confirmed. A fact of the world that he was present to witness.

I stood there for a moment. The veteran's eyes, which had been the only moving thing about him, weren't moving anymore.

I thought about saying something else. I ran through the options the way you run your tongue over a sore tooth — not because you expect it to help, just to assess the damage. What was there to say? That I objected? I had objected. The objection is sitting in the air right where I left it, preserved perfectly, completely ignored.

"I… apparently I am."

I don't know what I meant, exactly. Apparently I am what — present? Complicit? A druid who just put a moonbeam on a paralyzed man so a Goliath could finish him more efficiently? Yes. Apparently. The universe does not care about intentions. I know that. Meril knew that. The moonbeam was mine.

Brewbarry had already moved on. He has a gift for that — not callousness, just a very clean relationship with what's done being done.

"I hope this will inspire a song in your new music studio."

He said this to Vukradin, I think. Or to the air. It was the kind of thing Brewbarry says at the end of things — an offering toward the future, a reason to keep going. The music studio is his north star as much as Vukradin's. I find that quietly touching, even now.

"I just want to celebrate our victory."

Yes. Of course. There's a dragon dead on this rooftop, and a creature that was trying to enslave it, and the Carver's vessel cooling on the stone. By any accounting that should be worth something. I didn't argue.

Brewbarry declared himself thirsty. There was no drink. He remained thirsty and undeterred.

I noted my position for the record and accepted that the record would be filed somewhere no one would ever read it.

*I'm on the record as not being pro-killing, but that's okay.*

That's what I said, out loud, to whoever was listening. It came out lighter than I felt it. I've learned to do that — state the thing plainly, let it sit, don't add weight to it by explaining. The plane of existence is safe. The Carver is stopped. A man who probably had no real choice died faster than I wanted him to.

The universe is still standing. I'm still standing. Brewbarry is standing and thirsty.

That's the accounting. I don't have to like it to accept it.

---

## **Vukradin — The Spoils of Victory**

The treasure was a block. That is perhaps the most honest way to describe it — not a chest, not a pile, not the scattered artful coinage you see in illustrations. A block of fused ice and residium and gold and gems and trade bars, all melted and refrozen together into something that had lost its individual components entirely. Three thousand gold, more or less, by estimation. None of it spendable. None of it separable, apparently, without the services of a planar theorist, because Soma identified — with the flat certainty she reserves for things that are absurd and true simultaneously — that the ice had been fused through some kind of planar trickery.

So: a fortune I could not spend, welded into a frozen mass by magic I did not understand, atop a tower I had just helped clear of a dragon and a body-snatching alien intelligence.

I stood there looking at it and thought: *yes, this is approximately how things go for me.*

The obsidian sword was there too — the Carver's weapon, the instrument of the domination ritual, the thing we had spent the better part of the last several hours trying to stop from completing its work on a white dragon. I picked it up. I examined it. And then I noticed the crystal in the pommel, and my heart did something complicated and involuntary.

It was a mental amplifier. The gem could project music — *directly into the minds of an audience.* No room acoustics to fight. No back-row listeners who couldn't quite hear. No bad nights where the lute is slightly out of tune and nobody tells you until afterward. Just thought, translated directly into experience, received by every mind in range with perfect clarity.

I stood there holding it and understood, in my bones, what this instrument could do. What *I* could do with it.

I said: "It seems like this was sent by the devil to tempt me, yes. Yes, save me."

The GM said something about how a lot of care and attention had gone into the selection of that phrase — *mental amplifier* — and I was already three steps ahead, because I had already named it.

"It is absolutely the Bluetooth sword. Done."

There was protest. There is always protest when I name something correctly on the first attempt. I accepted the clarification — "Magical Bluetooth swap, got it, sorry, thanks for clarifying" — because I am a reasonable person and I am capable of updating my terminology when presented with additional product information. The underlying concept remained sound.

But here is the thing. Here is the thing about the gem.

It had to go back to its owner.

I know how this sounds. I know exactly how this sounds, standing on a frozen battlement holding the single most useful instrument for my particular professional ambitions that I have ever encountered, freshly looted from the corpse of an alien entity that had been trying to use it to enslave a dragon. I know. And I said it anyway, because it is true: the gem belongs to someone, and that someone is not me, and until I can establish through proper documentation that the item has no rightful owner or that the rightful owner consents to transfer, I cannot keep it.
