That is the principle. That is what I said I would do. "Handcrafted for my personal quest," I told it, because I am aware of the irony even when I cannot act against it.

"Save me," I had said.

I meant it.

---

The main block of treasure was the next problem. Three thousand gold, mostly unspendable, and already I could feel the shape of the argument forming: *who does it belong to?* The gold a dragon hoards over a long life does not start out belonging to the dragon. It starts out belonging to somebody. Merchants, probably. Villagers, probably. People the dragon killed or displaced or simply robbed by existing in a radius that made trade impossible. The fact that it had then been fused into a residium-ice block by planar magic did not change the underlying ownership question. It just made it harder to divide.

I said this. I framed it as a criminal analogy because I think those land better than philosophical abstractions: scratching the serial number off a stolen item does not make it yours. That is what the ice was. Scratched-off serial numbers. The moral situation was unchanged.

Soma looked at the sky and said: "Look, the universe is safe, right? Like, or, you know, the plane of existence is safe, that's what matters most, nothing else."

She is not wrong. She is almost never wrong about the large shape of things. But the large shape of things and the specific ethical obligation are not the same question, and I was operating on the specific ethical obligation, which required me to declare the gold stolen goods and begin the documentation process.

"I am enraged," I said, "at all the corruption and evil that is stinying me from my music studio."

*Stynying.* I meant *stymying.* It came out wrong because I was genuinely upset and because Soma's cosmological calm has a way of making my particular frustrations sound small, which they are not, they are enormous, they are everything I have been working toward for years.

Harbin Wester would try to claim credit. The Lord's Alliance would try to claim the gold. Some functionary in Neverwinter would produce documentation explaining why the frozen block of dragon treasure was, actually, technically, the property of the Crown, and I would be left having saved the world and earned nothing for it except a sending stone message from Ser Kaelen telling me to come celebrate in Phandalin. "Conflict-free triumph," he had said. He had studied my vocabulary. He had absorbed it and fed it back to me in twenty-five words. I did not know whether to be moved or suspicious, and I was not sure those were different reactions.

"He's about as close as someone we can actually trust as anybody," I said.

Beat.

"No, I don't trust him. He's as close as anybody."

That sentence contains everything I currently believe about the people around me.

---

And then the statue.

The situation was we had to make, we made a choice, and the particular individual we wanted to return the blue quartz mermaid to was no longer available for us to return it to.

We chose the dragon. We chose to come here, to stop the Carver, to save Icespire and by extension Phandalin and by extension whatever cosmological safety Soma was gesturing at with her plane-of-existence framing. We made the right call. I believe that.

"Well, then that, you know, fuck them," I said. "We gotta go. Her next of kin? And they're in Neverwinter."

But while I was saying it, there was a voice underneath the words — not a voice from outside, just the particular internal noise that shows up when you are doing the right thing and paying a price for it simultaneously. *You could have your music studio. All you have to do is compromise a few principles here and there.*

"You don't get it," I said.

Which is true. The voice doesn't get it. That is the whole point of having principles — they are not negotiable tools you deploy when convenient and set aside when costly. They are the thing you are. The moment you start treating them as items in a cost-benefit analysis, you have already lost them. I know this. I have always known this.

"I just wanted my goddamn music studio," I said, "and I ended up having to go save the world."

The block of gold sat there, unfissured, inseparable, belonging to someone we had not yet identified.

The gem sat in the pommel of the sword, perfectly designed for a purpose I was not permitted to use it for yet.

The Carver was gone, and the world was, apparently, safe, and the plane of existence was intact, and the right owner of the blue quartz mermaid was unavailable. 

I just wanted my goddamn music studio.

---

## **Soma — Unwelcome Claimants**

The moment Sylvine opened her mouth, I knew exactly how this was going to end.

Not because I am wise. Not because Mielikki whispered it into my ear. Because I am forty-five years old, and I have seen this particular performance staged by this particular kind of creature — smooth voice, hands clasped, eyes wet with convenient devotion — more times than I can count on both hands and my shell. She hadn't even reached the question mark before I had the whole script mapped.

*Are you Vukradin the Bard? The one who returns the treasure to those that it has been stolen from?*

Shell sprout. Shell sprout with backup.

The backup being Runa, who stood behind her sister with the expression of someone waiting to remember her cue. They had the look of women who had rehearsed this approach on the road — who had heard enough about the famous, principled, treasure-returning bard of Phandalin to know exactly which door to knock on.

I give her credit for the detail. *Returns treasure to those it has been stolen from.* That is a precise hook. That is a hook designed and tested for a specific catch. Someone had done their research.

Vukradin, for his part, gave them nothing to work with.

"No," he said, with the flatness of a man who has been awake too long, "I haven't done very well with the return."

A lesser con artist might have recalibrated. Sylvine just pivoted without losing a syllable of warmth, turning to her sister like they were sharing a private miracle.

"Runa, didn't I tell you that that saint, Vukradin, would come and get our treasure for us? I knew it, I knew that if all we had to do was pray to the gods, a kind soul like Vukradin would come here and deal with that dragon, and give our treasure back."

And Runa: "Blessed be\! Blessed be\!"

I did not roll my eyes. I am above rolling my eyes. I noted the performance with the professional detachment of someone who has watched the same river flood the same valley and knows better than to stand in the same spot twice. The *saint.* The *kind soul.* The gods invoked as collateral. The treasure presented not as a claim to be proven but as a thing already returned, already ours to hand over, the paperwork mere formality.

Brewbarry said, beside me, with what I can only describe as genuine admiration for the audacity: "Well, that's the quickest we've ever found treasure's owners before."

Yes. Remarkable speed, that. Almost as if they'd been waiting.

I watched Vukradin's face while Sylvine performed. He's getting better at reading people — the exhaustion has burned off the naivety, if not the idealism — and I saw the moment he decided. Not doubt. Not consideration. Just refusal, arriving fully formed.

"Lady, I don't know who you are, I don't know how you think that all 3,000 gold that this dragon hoarded over its long life is somehow yours, but I don't really believe you. You're probably gonna try to steal this, like everybody else steals. You're one of them. Why don't you just get out of here?"

I've heard him angrier. This was the tiredness talking — not hot anger but a kind of flat, accumulated exhaustion with the whole category of person standing in front of him. The phonies. He's been counting them.

Sylvine switched registers instantly. One beat of theatrical injury: "I am hurt. You are a thief\!" And then she held it just long enough for Vukradin to respond—

"I am tired of all these liars and cheats in this land. Get out of here. There's nobody left worthy."

—and then she dropped it entirely.

That was the tell, if anyone needed one. Wounded pride takes a moment to recover. Wounded pride lingers, escalates, demands an apology or at least a witness. This woman let it go in under a second because the injury was never real to begin with. It was a stage prop. She set it down when she was done with it.

"Second plan\! We kill them all\!"

There it is. I had been waiting for it since the question mark.

What followed was the kind of fight that happens when people who trained together try their training against people who have survived worse things without training at all. Brewbarry announced the terms of engagement with characteristic precision — "Try not to die too quickly, I want to enjoy this" — and then made good on the invitation. When the last veteran went down, he issued his verdict with the gravity of a man recording something for posterity.

"Root Smasher stands, you don't. That's the story."

One of the veterans — not quite dead, not quite committed to seeing the fight through — found his way to a practical conclusion: "Screw this shit. There's more money to be found elsewhere."

Career advancement. Yes. Sensible.

I watched him go.

I am on the record as having no objection to that particular outcome.

---

## **Brewbarry — Skirmish in the Fortress**

The moment Sylvine Wintermoon stopped performing and said *"Second plan — we kill them all,"* the rage came up clean and simple, the way it always does. No hesitation between the feeling and the body. Just the halberd in my hands and the red at the edges of everything.

Valphine was already moving. Her spirit guardians bloomed outward, divine light with teeth, and the bandits who'd been creeping toward us through the fortress corridor got their first lesson about walking into a cleric's radius without asking first. I heard them take it. I watched them not enjoy it.

Then one of them reached her. Got through the guardians, got a blade in, and the light went out — the concentration broken, the aura gone. I noted this the way you note weather. The math had changed. Fine.

Soma went bear. There's a sound to that shift — I've heard it enough times now to not flinch at it — and she put her weight into the nearest veteran hard enough that he staggered. Good. One number smaller.

Then someone made a grab for the treasure slag. I saw it from the side: a bandit's hands on Vukradin's, trying to pull the hoard piece free while the fight had his attention split. Vukradin held. Of course he held. I've seen him keep hold of a principle while getting stabbed in an argument — a man who can do that doesn't lose his grip on gold he's already decided is complicated.

And then Vukradin sang something, or said something, or whatever the shape of his magic was in that moment — and two of the veterans stopped moving. Just stopped. Held where they stood, faces caught between one expression and the next, the kind of frozen that means their minds are still running but their bodies have taken a vote and declined to continue.

I looked at them standing there. Paralyzed. Alive inside, somewhere, trying to get out.

*And yet* — there was no and yet. Not today. Not after the dragon. Not after the Carver. Not after all of it. The mercy was used up somewhere further back up this mountain, and these men had walked into a fortress with weapons and a plan, and the plan had been *kill them all.*

I cut them down. That's the full account. The halberd makes a certain sound when it connects with something that cannot move to absorb the blow, and I made that sound until the ledger was closed, and then I looked at what was left standing.

One bandit. Looking at his two colleagues on the floor. Looking at Soma's bear. Looking at me.

He made a decision. It was, honestly, a good decision. He said something about better opportunities elsewhere and he left. I let him. The rage doesn't care about fleeing men — it cares about the ones who stay and keep trying.

The fortress went quiet.

Vukradin's message to Ser Kaelen happened while I was still coming down from it — that transition from the red edges back to the ordinary colors of the world, which always takes a moment longer than I expect. I heard him say, *"The dragon is dead, so is the Carver. We're heading to Neverwinter. Spread the word."*

I thought about the dragon. About fighting *with* it and then fighting *against* it and then watching it fall. About what that actually costs a body, mine and everyone else's.

"Fighting a fucking dragon," I said. "That was conflict."

Nobody argued the point.

---

## **Vukradin — A Message from the Gauntlet**

The spell takes twenty-five words. I know this the way I know how many beats a phrase wants before it lands — exactly twenty-five, no more, and the clock on the battle is still running when I start composing.

I will be honest with you. I drafted it in my head three times while Brewbarry was still finishing things. The first draft ran long. The second draft was too spare — it felt like a battlefield dispatch, and I am not a soldier, whatever the Order pin on my collar implies. The third draft I actually sent:

*The dragon is dead, so is the Carver. We're heading to Neverwinter.*

Clean. Factual. Leaves nothing open that I did not intend to leave open. I am moderately proud of it.

Ser Kaelen's reply arrives like it was hand-calligraphed by committee.

"Praise Torm\! Your righteous victory brings light to the north. The gauntlet awaits your arrival in Phandalin. We shall celebrate your conflict-free triumph together."

I stand there on the battlement with dragon frost still melting off my collar and read those words again. *Conflict-free triumph.* He put my vocabulary in his mouth and handed it back to me like a gift. Like he had been taking notes. I am not sure whether to be flattered or to feel like I just caught someone reading my letters.

There is a second message underneath the warmth, of course. Not *Neverwinter.* Not *yes, go where you said you were going.* The gauntlet awaits your arrival in *Phandalin.* We shall celebrate in *Phandalin.* And then, because even a Sending cannot contain him, the short version of a longer argument: *Well done. Come to Phandalin quick. Issues with the Emerald Enclave.*

Issues. With the Emerald Enclave. After everything else, now that.

I hear myself say it out loud before I have decided to. "He's about as close as someone we can actually trust as anybody." The words sound fine until they land, and then I hear what they actually mean, and I add the correction immediately: "No, I don't trust him. He's as close as anybody."

Which is to say: the bar has gotten very low and he has cleared it. Which is to say nothing.

Somewhere nearby, someone mentions the copper wire — the material component for Sending, which means we have been passing voice over copper wire this whole time, which means the world invented the telephone and forgot to tell anyone. I say *yes, per phone*, because it is true, and also because the alternative is to keep thinking about what *conflict-free triumph* means in the mouth of a man who has never once, as far as I can tell, done anything without a second purpose.

What does he want us to see when we arrive? What has he arranged? The Emerald Enclave — that is Adabra's world, and Adabra has been cooling on me for weeks, and I have not asked myself hard enough why.

I just wanted my goddamn music studio, I think, not for the first time and certainly not for the last. And I ended up having to go save the world. And now the world is saved, provisionally, and Ser Kaelen is waiting in Phandalin with open arms and a press release already written, and he used my words, and I do not know whether he loves me or is managing me, and I am not sure the difference matters.

Brewbarry's voice cuts through all of it with the efficiency of a man who has no patience for ambiguity when there is a clear action available.

"I heard 'celebrate,' say no more. Let's go."

I look at him. He is covered in dragon blood and is already thinking about the drink he does not have. His face is completely at peace. The reply said *celebrate* and that is all the information he required. I find, against all expectation, that this is the most comforting thing anyone has said to me since the battle ended.

We are going to Phandalin. Ser Kaelen will be there, ceremonial and over-composed, and I will smile and shake hands and let him have his celebration, and I will listen for what he does not say. That is fine. I have twenty-five words too, and I know how to use them.

# Chapter 36 A Gem of a Problem, A Rat of a Solution

---

## Soma — Growing and Strategic Planning

The cold was still in my bones from the rooftop when it happened — that particular stillness that settles after a fight, when the body finally stops insisting it's about to die. The others were taking stock of themselves. I was doing the same thing I always do: noticing what changed.

Something had shifted in me. I could feel it the way you feel a tide turning before you can see it — a quiet deepening, like another ring added to the heartwood. Vukradin, characteristically, had not reached the same place. He'd given everything he had up there. I noted this without comment.

Valphine was already exploring what she'd become, turning the new thing over in her mind the way you turn a stone in your hand to understand its weight. There was something about bonds — a way of threading her presence between allies so that courage could travel along the connection. She described it with that particular intensity she brings to all divine matters, and I thought: *that's a cleric thing*. Real, useful, but built on the assumption that proximity to other people is safe.

Then came the discussion I've learned to recognize: the hypothetical combat mathematics that the others love and that I endure. Someone raised the possibility of an ability that would let a protector throw themselves into harm's path — teleport to a wounded ally and absorb what was meant for them.

I listened.

Valphine discussed things. She shared how she could have blessed us before we went into combat, but didn't because the pain was purifying. Then she mumbled about what spells she should keep in her toolkit, and whether an abjuration that would keep out planar creatures would help her with our emerging planar disturbances.

I thought about what I could now do with abjuration — a proper warding zone, the kind that would seal off extra-planar creatures, and protect my  home. But those spells are not the druidic.

The discussion moved to tactics, the way it always does after a fight — everyone suddenly very wise about what should have happened differently. I half-listened and held the staff, and felt the subtle presence it always carries now, and thought: *one more thing I don't fully understand that Meril left me responsible for.*

I am old. And I really want to be an owl bear.

---

## Brewbarry — Harvesting the Dragon

The dragon was still warm.

That's the thing nobody tells you. You think cold, you think ice — it's a white dragon, it breathes frost, it had been sleeping inside a mountain since before most of Phandalin's oldest residents were born. But the body was warm. Not warm like a fire. Warm like something that had been alive very recently and was only now beginning to understand it wasn't anymore.

I stood over it and didn't say anything for a while.

I have killed things. My tribe taught me to kill things before they taught me much else. Deer. Wolves. Men, sometimes, though that is not something I think about when I want to sleep well. But I had never stood over something like this. Never felt the particular silence that settles when a creature that large stops breathing. The battlements had been so full of noise — the cold and the wind and the thing's own roaring — and then suddenly they weren't. Just us. And it.

Cryovain. That was its name, apparently. It had a name. I find that harder to deal with than I expected.

Soma was already moving. She always moves like she knows exactly what she is doing, which I respect, even when I'm not completely sure she does. She knelt beside the carcass with a focus that reminded me of the best hunters I'd known — that total quiet you get when a person is really paying attention, when their hands know the work and their mind is somewhere deeper than thought. I have seen it in old men at the end of long hunts. I had not expected to see it from a tortle on a rooftop.

I helped where I could. Held things. Moved the body when it needed moving. A dragon, even a young one, is enormously heavy in the particular way of something that was made to be impossible to move, and the cold had started settling into the joints. My hands found purchase. I am good at this part — the physical work, the bracing and hauling. Vukradin is better at the talking. Soma is better at the knowing. I am good at holding things still.

She went for the throat first.

I will not pretend I was not squeamish. I grew up in the mountains. I have cleaned game. But there is something different about watching someone reach into a dragon's throat with that much precision and intention, knowing exactly what they are looking for, and finding it. The breath pouch came free carefully — a strange, swollen thing, heavier than it looked, and when it moved, you could feel the cold coming off it. Not temperature cold. Something older than temperature. The kind of cold that means something.

We held it, and I thought: there is a sound in there. Contained. Waiting.

I thought about what it would cost to become something that carried a sound like that.

The scales were easier to think about, or at least easier to look at. Soma worked them free from the hide with the same methodical attention, and they were beautiful in the particular way of things that were not made for beauty but ended up that way anyway — supple and pale and catching the light like polished stone. I held one up and turned it. My reflection came back distorted, cold-colored. I looked strange in it. I always look strange in things.

We talked about what to do with them.

I understood parts of this conversation better than others. Dragon scale mail — that I understood. Armor made from the thing that tried to kill you. There is a Uthgardt logic to that, even if my tribe would have done it differently, would have made it louder, would have worn the skull. Vukradin mentioned costs. He always mentions costs, which I appreciate even when the numbers make me go somewhere quiet in my head. The math of the surface world still sometimes feels like a language I am reading phonetically — I can say the words, I am not always certain I understand them.

But the breath pouch. That I understood differently.

Something made from the cold that was supposed to kill us turned around, made into a tool. That is not just resource management. I have heard Vukradin use that phrase, and I think it means counting things. This was something more. This was taking what was done to you and deciding it was yours now.

I don't have much that is mine. A sword that glows faintly wrong. A helm that makes my eyes look like something you should be afraid of in the dark. The exile took most of what I had, and I became something after that — something I am not proud of, something I drank my way into and am still, slowly, drinking my way back out of. The mine shares are something. The studio is something, or will be. But those are futures. Partial stakes in things not yet real.

These scales were real. This pouch was real. We had killed something enormous and terrible, and now it was material.

I held a scale in my palm for a moment longer than was probably necessary.

Phandalin was next. The Mountain's Toe Mine on the way. I knew both of those things already. I let myself have one more moment with the weight of the scale in my hand — the texture of it, the way it was just warm enough to be unsettling — and then I set it down with the others and helped pack what we had taken.

We were going home. Or at least, the closest thing I currently have to one.

That felt like enough.

---

## Valphine — Inspection and Strike at Mountain Toes Gold Mine

The mine sat quiet at the mouth of the mountain, which was itself the problem.

I noticed it before we were through the gate — that particular absence of sound that means something has gone wrong with money. No picks. No cart-wheels grinding on stone. No vibration in the earth under my feet, the deep percussion of work. Just wind, and the nervous shuffling of the Whiskered Gang, who were arranged outside the entrance in a way that suggested they had been standing there for some time doing precisely nothing.

Zeleen Varnaster saw us coming and rearranged his face into something optimistic.

"Hey, boss\! What's up?"

I looked at him for a moment. I let the silence sit.

"Are you working hard today?"

It was a simple question. I delivered it the way you might read an inventory list — flat, procedural, without particular feeling. Zeleen's expression crumpled into something resembling a man watching his house burn down.

"They've been working really hard. I promise you, boss, they've been working really, really hard."

He gestured vaguely toward the interior of the mine, where no sounds whatsoever were occurring. I filed that away and said nothing. Surface dwellers have a touching faith in assertion. They believe that if you say a thing with sufficient conviction, it becomes more true. Drow society had disabused me of that notion approximately eleven minutes into childhood.

We went inside.

Brewbarry's confusion at least had the virtue of being genuine. He looked at the equipment — idle picks, empty carts, tools that hadn't been touched — and something in him simply couldn't reconcile the image.

"Disconnect," he said. "What is wrong with those tools? I mean, what happened to Rock and Stone?"

The answer, when it came from the foreman Dwolin the Grout, was delivered with the particular gravity of a man who has spent his entire adult life being right about something no one else understands.

"You have to understand, gold. A gold vein is shy and must be treated with absolute respect. If you don't treat it with the respect, it doesn't come out."

I processed that. A shy gold vein. The earth itself, apparently, had feelings about the scheduling.

Horia Stonetallow stepped in beside him with the weary affection of someone who has explained a difficult colleague at great length on many previous occasions.

"Dwolin is the best seeker of gold I have ever known. He's got his little rituals, and his little gimmicks, and his little things…"

She trailed off in the way people do when they love someone who is also exhausting. I had some sympathy for the position. I found myself thinking of the early days of my temple, when I'd discovered that spiritual conviction and organizational coherence did not always arrive together.

But that was a problem for later. The present problem was a mine that was not mining, and a gang of were-rats who appeared to have concluded that standing near the mine counted as doing their job.

I called the light.

Daylight erupted from a point above us — not the soft glow of torches or the polite illumination of a lantern, but the full, searing brilliance of midday sun compressed into an enclosed space, pouring into every corner. The were-rat guards flinched as one, a ripple of physical discomfort running through them. Good. Lathander does not do gentle reminders.

I surveyed the scene in its sudden clarity.

"This is…" I paused, letting the light do its work. "Lathander won't be pleased."

Then I turned back to Zeleen.

"Are these dwarves mining?" I asked. "Did you tell me that these dwarves are mining? Are these dwarves mining?"

He opened his mouth.

I gestured to the empty minecart beside us.

"Is there gold in this minecart?"

"No, but Dwolin says that we need the Earthstone Gem before we can get the gold."

"And have you gotten this earth stone gem for the dwarves?"

Stunned silence, which is a particular type of answer. Then:

"Boss, we're paid to protect the mine, not to go on fetch quests."

Brewbarry had reached the end of his patience, which, to his credit, he had extended further than I might have expected.

"The only reason you're protecting the mine is because the mine is useful to us to acquire gold. If the mine is not producing gold, then you're not doing your job."

Admirably direct. I followed through.

"It means… pull your fingers out of your rat's ass, and do whatever is necessary for the dwarves to be happy miners. We want to hear picks."

Zeleen looked between the two of us with the expression of a man searching for a loophole in a contract that contains no loopholes.

"Okay, so what does that mean? Like, so we can stay here then, right? Because you don't need the mine, right?"

I stared at him. I truly did not know where to begin with that.

He tried again.

"Well, boss, I mean, I think you make an excellent point. I think you're making an excellent point, but, like, I mean to be rude, but to get this gem, we have to go into the forest where the orcs are, and this is more of a…"

I let him get that far. It seemed fair. Then:

"Is that any of my problems? Am I getting paid with fees to, you know, make sure that the mine is productive? What am I paying you for? If all you do is eat the cheese, and the mine is not producing gold, then I'm wasting my cheese, aren't I?"

He swallowed.

"A waste of cheese," I said. "That's what you are. Unless you go into the forest, find the gem, get the bloody dwarves to mine. Is that how you want to be remembered?"

There is a particular moment in any negotiation when the other person's will simply gives way. You can feel it — the shoulders drop a fraction, the eyes stop calculating. Zeleen reached that moment. He reached it loudly.

"I think we could… we could make that happen, Mr. Boss. Mr. Right."

Behind him, I heard him screaming at his were-rats that they hadn't done it yet, that this was completely taken care of, that Mr. Boss had every confidence in them. Or words to that effect.

Brewbarry, for his part, was tapping his halberd against his palm with the methodical patience of someone who has learned that visual communication saves time. It did seem to accelerate Zeleen's enthusiasm.

I waited until the noise subsided.

"Alright," I said. "So you keep the gold rolling, we'll keep the cheese rolling, and we'll both be friends forever."

It is not the most stirring speech I have ever given. But it was accurate.

What followed was the more interesting conversation — the one Vukradin was not part of.

Horia, once Zeleen's delegation had departed into the forest with the specific energy of people who have been told exactly what consequences await them, turned to us with something approaching relief.

"I was gonna propose you give me some money so I can go buy a fake gem, but you know what? Your solution is way better, man."

I did not say anything. There is wisdom in letting a person finish arriving at your conclusion before you confirm it.

Brewbarry was already ahead of it.

"Let's call the fake gem Plan B," he said, "and in case the were-rats, you know, fail — which I have high expectation of them failing — "

Sensible. I co-signed that assessment with a small nod. The were-rats were motivated, which was something, but motivation and competence are not the same resource, and I had not formed a high opinion of their reserves of the latter.

The funding question was where it got interesting. Horia wanted fifty gold pieces sourced from the mine's own accounts. Brewbarry's reaction was immediate and genuine.

"You want gold. You… you're working a gold mine. You see the irony of what you're just saying, right?"

Horia, to her credit, had a document for this.

"According to… look, I'm a big believer in contractual obligations and procedures and rules. And if you'll notice, we have a no-skimming clause that Mr. Bucherton insisted we put in."

Brewbarry stared at her.

"How about the Barbarian in your face clause? Did you read that in your contract?"

Horia elected to propose a solution before that negotiating point was developed further.

"I'm perfectly happy to have an addendum signed by Mr. Brewberry. Thumbprints."

"Alright, I'm out of ink. How about I draw a little blood from you, so we can sign that addendum?"

There was a brief pause. Horia appeared to perform some rapid recalculation.

"I think, I think we could assume that the addendum is signed."

She tried to lean in conspiratorially and encountered the geometry of a three-foot dwarf attempting to whisper to a seven-foot Goliath. She was aiming approximately at his kneecap. To her credit, she adjusted, looked up, and delivered the intelligence that mattered:

"I do suggest we keep this little addendum between ourselves. I don't think Mr. Rukradin would be open to these kinds of negotiations."

Brewbarry looked momentarily puzzled.

"What do you mean? It's honest work. You're, you know, using gold from the gold mine to buy a fake gem so that we can mine more gold."

I kept my expression entirely neutral. The logic was not wrong, exactly. It was just the kind of logic that Vukradin would spend forty minutes unpacking from the wrong direction — and we didn't have forty minutes, and the mine needed to function, and a man whose theology includes the phrase *conflict-free gold* was perhaps not the ideal audience for a scheme involving a fake mystical gem and a superstitious foreman who believed veins of earth metal had feelings.

Some things are better handled quietly.

I said nothing. That was my signature on the addendum.

---

## Brewbarry — A Hero's Welcome in Phandalin

The road into Phandalin felt different when people were glad you were on it.

I don't know how to explain that. My tribe never had a road like this. We had paths through rock and wind, and when you came back from somewhere, people checked whether you'd brought the right things home. They didn't run to meet you. They didn't put their small hands up for you to hit with your large ones. But these children did, streaming out from between buildings, bare feet on the dirt, and I had to slow down so I didn't knock any of them over.

I hit back. Gently. Every hand.

They were cheering for something. For us, I suppose, though it took me a moment to understand that. The dragon parts helped. We'd brought pieces of what had come down on this town — scales, a claw, evidence that the thing was real and that something had ended — and the crowd read that faster than any announcement. A murmur went through, then a shout, then something that was properly loud in a way that pressed against my chest from the outside rather than from within it.

I stood at the edge of that sound and let it happen.

Harbin Wester found his way to the front of it, naturally. The man has a gift for finding fronts of things. He was already talking by the time I registered he'd started, his voice arranged into the particular shape of someone taking a careful share of something they did not earn.

"This is exactly why we needed a privy council with the extraordinary talents of Mr. Vucrodin. And without… I just cannot stress enough how with his undefatigable support and his management to lead this group of heroic adventures, the threat of dragons has abated."

I looked at Vukradin. Vukradin had the face he always has when Harbin speaks — attentive, gracious, containing something that is neither of those things. The crowd didn't notice. They cheered again. I let it wash over me and decided it was fine. Harbin had been afraid. People who are afraid do strange things with credit. I understand that.

The festival came together the way Phandalin does things — quickly, practically, with someone's cousin producing a fiddle from somewhere and someone else arriving with bread that was still warm. The town green filled in around us. I found that I kept being handed things: a small cake, a ribbon from a child who had apparently decided I needed one, the corner position at the longest table, which seemed to be the seat of honor and which I occupied because it fit.

And then the innkeeper came.

I know the innkeeper. I have spent meaningful time at his bar. He is a man who pours drinks with a considered expression, like each one is a small judgment he is making about you. He had a bottle under his arm that he was treating differently from usual bottles — keeping it against his body rather than carrying it by the neck — and his expression was the specific one of someone about to give you something they've been saving.

"I always gave you the good stuff," he said, and set a cup in front of me. "This is the very good stuff. And Brewbarry," — he said my name correctly, which matters more to me than it probably should — "I only have two barrels. So you're just gonna have to, like, remember that."

I picked up the cup. I looked at it. I looked at him.

"I'll be the judge of that," I said.

I drank.

It was better. It was genuinely, measurably better — the difference between a fire that heats a room and a fire that *is* the room. I held it in my mouth for a moment before I swallowed, because I have learned, since the tribe, that some things are worth the extra second. Then I set the cup down and looked up at the festival happening around me, and the children still moving through it, and Vukradin laughing at something across the green, and I thought: I signed a thing. I pressed my thumb into wax for this town. I brought my halberd to a mountain for this town. And this town gave me its best barrel.

That is an exchange that makes sense to me.

I was still sitting with that when Ser Kaelen appeared at the edge of the celebration with the particular posture of a man who needs to ruin something pleasant. He moved through the crowd without taking any of it in, which told me the urgency was real. He found Soma first.

"Soma," he said. "We must speak urgently."

I watched them. The fiddle kept playing. Somewhere a child knocked over a cup and someone laughed. I put my hand around the special reserve and waited to find out what the pleasant thing was that was about to end.

---

## Soma — Urgent Council at the Temple of Lathander

We came down from the mountain smelling of dragon and cold stone, and somehow ended up in a temple discussing politics. That's the job, apparently.

Valphine's acolytes met us at the door with the particular enthusiasm of people who have found religion and want everyone to know it. They showed her their new blisters. Sun damage, mostly — too much time in the courtyard, not enough shade. I looked at the angry red patches on their forearms and thought: there are easier ways to demonstrate devotion. I did not say this. I've learned to keep certain observations to myself.

Ser Kaelen was already waiting inside, which told me the news was urgent enough that he hadn't stopped to consider whether we wanted it yet.

He told us about Adabra.

She'd found out about the Whispering Grove. What really happened — the treant, the concealment, all of it. And she had decided, in the way that Adabra always decides things, that the correct response was to convene a gathering and be loudly right about it in front of as many people as possible.

"Adabra has found out about what really happened at the Whispering Grove," Kaelen said. "She has decided, she has gone with her allies and is going to attempt to convince the druids that your reckless interventionism has brought harm to the forests."

I'd been waiting for this. Not hoping for it — waiting for it. There's a difference.

I thought about the last time I'd spoken to Adabra, and how she'd looked at me with that particular expression that means *you understand*, and how I'd let her think that. It sat in my chest like something undigested.

"She's confused," Kaelen continued, with the careful manner of a man delivering a medical diagnosis. "She sees the harm that was caused by those who defended against the dragons, and thinks that the problem was that the defenders against the dragons were the problem, not the dragons. She sees the planar incursions as… not the symptoms, because she never experienced those symptoms. She experienced the consequences of war."

"Yeah," I said. "She's bringing her own war out here."

There wasn't much else to say about it. Adabra had drawn a conclusion from her evidence and her evidence was real — she just hadn't seen the part that came before it. That's not stupidity. It's a gap. Gaps don't argue themselves closed.

I asked whether the Lords Alliance or the Order could do anything about it.

Kaelen shook his head. "The Lord's Alliance doesn't want to intervene, and the Order of the Gauntlet, although we would like to intervene, cannot, because, well, the War of the Dragons, and then the War of the Giants, and then the war… Let's just say there's fewer of us to go around these days."

"It's all on us, huh?"

I didn't say it with despair. I said it as a fact, the way you'd note that it's raining. He confirmed it was.

Then he mentioned the staff.

He said it carefully, the way men of sword and action say things they don't entirely understand. He said it held symbolism among the druids — standing, he called it — and that it might tip the balance at the conclave if I were willing to bring it.

"Standing with the druids — I'm barely a druid myself\!"

It came out more blunt than I intended, but it was true. Meril sent me out after a year. One year of learning something that takes decades, with a staff I still don't fully understand, from a teacher who thought sending me off with it was the right thing to do and never quite explained why. I know what the staff does in my hands. I don't always know what it means.

"Yes, but apparently this staff is… I don't… look, look, druids have rituals and beliefs that I, as a… as a man of sword and action, don't always understand," Kaelen said. He had the grace to look slightly embarrassed about it.

I can respect that. Honest uncertainty is underrated.

Then he told me the rest of it.

"The only caution I give you, Soma, is that there are some there who have suggested that they should even evaluate whether you should retain the staff."

The room didn't change. The light was the same. The blistered acolytes were still blistered somewhere behind me. But something went very still inside my shell, which is where I keep the things that are not for public inspection.

*Retain the staff.* As if it were a matter of proper custody. As if Meril's hands weren't in the grain of it, and Xanth's recognition of it hadn't meant what I knew it meant.

The staff has more leaves than it did when Meril left it with me. I've noticed. I notice things like that.

"They're not taking it from me," I said. "This has at least sentimental value."

Sentimental. I chose that word deliberately, because it is the word that ends conversations. Nobody argues with sentiment. What I did not say is that the staff is the last physical thing that connects me to Meril, and that I am forty-five years old, and that whatever time I have left I intend to spend it with the things that matter close at hand. What I did not say is that I don't fully know what this staff is yet, and that the not-knowing feels important — like something I need to work out for myself, in my own time, without a conclave of druids deciding the answer for me.

Kaelen nodded. Then he put his hand on the hilt of his sword — not drawing it, just touching it, the way you do when something resonates — and said, with a kind of quiet conviction: "I agree with you. A stat — that no order can take my sword."

It was a little mangled, as declarations go. But I understood what he meant. Some things are yours not because you earned them or because you're qualified to hold them, but because they chose you and you chose them back, and that's its own kind of legitimacy.

I held the staff a little tighter and said nothing more about it.

Kaelen's attention had already shifted to Brewbarry, who had his halberd out in that way large people sometimes carry weapons — not threateningly, just present.

"Is that a halberd?" Kaelen said, with the expression of a man who has opinions about polearms. "Because that is a very fine halberd, Brewberry. We should parry."

Brewbarry looked at him with the patience of someone who has had this offer before. "You don't want to have a conversation with it, trust me."

I looked at the two of them — a knight admiring a weapon that had very recently helped fight a dragon, and a Goliath who was done being interesting about it — and felt something loosen slightly in my chest. Not everything, but enough.

The conclave was coming. The druids might try to take the staff. Adabra had already decided we were the problem.

I am old. I have seen much. I am not going to give anyone my staff.

# Chapter 37 The Intervention

---

## Valphine — Return to Phandalin and Political Briefing

The road back to Phandalin always smelled the same: pine resin, horse dung, and that particular quality of surface-world optimism that I've never quite learned to predict. We had come from Dragon Barrow and the mine with the sort of success surface towns prefer to make public before anyone asks too many questions about the cost. The Underdark taught me to read a court from the smell of it. Up here, everything smells like possibility, which is functionally identical to naivety. I find it charming, briefly, and with the certain knowledge of how fragile it is.

Harbin Wester was waiting for us at the town boundary with the full theatrical apparatus of gratitude: banners, townsfolk arranged in the semicircle that people arrange themselves in when someone important has told them to look festive, and that particular smile he wears when he's already drafting the speech he's going to give about himself. The festival had probably been his idea within the hour of receiving word that the dragon was dead.

To his considerable credit, he praised us warmly. He extolled our bravery, our sacrifice, our decisive role in ending the dragon threat that had hung over the region like a promise of ruin. He mentioned, with the modest pride of a man who has rehearsed modest pride, that it was his own foresight — his establishment of the Privy Council — that had brought such capable heroes to Phandalin in the first place.

I filed it away. Not with resentment. Resentment is a surface-dweller luxury, the emotional equivalent of standing in a rainstorm and being surprised that you're wet. Harbin had done exactly what any competent political creature would do: lashed his name to a victory before the victory had time to acquire its own history. Back in Menzoberranzan, my mother's rivals would have done the same thing before the body was cold. At least Harbin waited until we were in earshot. I found that almost touching.

The party received this with varying degrees of awareness. Brewbarry beamed. Vukradin accepted it with the earnest dignity of someone who does not fully understand what is being done to him. I smiled and clasped my hands and let the light of Lathander fall agreeably on my face. The performance cost me nothing.

It was Ser Kaelen who broke the pageantry — he drew Soma aside with the quiet urgency of a man delivering genuinely bad news, which in my experience means it is either bad news or he wants something. The rest of us drifted after them the way the party always drifts, because we have collectively never managed to let anyone have a private conversation that doesn't involve us.

Adabra, it seemed, had been busy. She had taken the events at the Whispering Wood — specifically Lyra's failure, which we had caused, depending on how generously you framed the sequence — and had been using them as a lever. Ostracizing Lyra from the order was the visible blade. The deeper cut was Soma: Soma as a dangerous interventionist, someone who had disrupted the natural order, who held Meril's Staff without the Enclave's blessing, who was teaching others to interfere rather than observe. Adabra was a true believer, which always makes them more dangerous than the merely ambitious. Ambition you can negotiate with. Faith is inflexible.

I watched Soma receive this information. She absorbed it the way she absorbs most things — slowly, from the outside in, like rain soaking through bark. It would settle into something useful eventually. That is her particular genius, and I mean that without condescension; patience is a form of power I respect entirely, even if I would find it suffocating to practice.

While Kaelen continued, my attention moved where it was more profitably spent. Vukradin asked whether my church was a charity.

The Temple of Lathander's Searing Pain of Justice. My contribution to Phandalin's spiritual life, housed in the restored bones of Tresendar Manor and accumulating, with gratifying steadiness, the wounded, the curious, the desperate, and the few with enough sense to understand that healing is more honest when it does not pretend pain was an accident.

Yes, I explained, it was charitable. We provided healing, food, shelter, community, and care to the broken and inconveniently alive. All of that was true. The doctrine was also true, though less suited to polite summary. Lathander's light is not gentle. It is power made radiant, pain made holy, correction delivered without apology from the sky itself.

Vukradin, to his credit, performed a small and elegant piece of duplicity: he made the transfer of unclaimed property to my church sound like moral hygiene rather than patronage. I admired it. He would almost certainly have objected to that interpretation, which only made the maneuver more interesting. Sincerity, in Vukradin, often wears the appearance of strategy so perfectly that I doubt even he understands the advantage he is taking.

I kept my expression entirely neutral. Inside, something that was either amusement or theological vindication observed that Lathander had arranged this quite tidily. Lost property flows toward the church. The church grows. The church heals people, which is genuinely good, which is genuinely painful against my very nature, which is to say genuinely pleasurable, which is to say that the Morninglord has arranged things so that goodness costs me something, and I find that an entirely satisfying way to worship. If there is a god listening, they have an excellent sense of irony.

It was, naturally, at this moment that Bryn and Giles came around the corner.

I had known they were conducting a fundraiser. A public religious parade through town, apparently. I had perhaps not inquired too closely into the precise method.

They were scarred. Sunburnt in the specific pattern that only comes from extended exposure combined with repeated contact between skin and something fibrous. Self-flagellation is one of those practices surface folk condemn most reliably when it is sincere enough to make them consider what their own comforts are protecting. They were smiling the way converts smile when they have done something they believe was worth the cost, which is to say: beatifically, with the particular radiance of people who have just proven something to themselves. The town watched them pass. The town's expression was varied.

I regarded them with the satisfaction of a priestess watching doctrine acquire flesh. This was Lathander's light stripped of pastoral decoration. Not the sentimental sunrise painted in temple glass for people who want divinity to resemble a kind grandfather. This was power and pain meeting in public, hurting the body, clarifying the will, and still serving a charitable purpose. My congregation understood more than I had perhaps credited them for. They had gone into the streets of Phandalin and demonstrated that healing need not be soft, that faith need not be comfortable, and that a worthy god may burn and still be worshipped.

The fact that most of the watching townspeople looked unsettled was, I thought, somewhat beside the point. The Underdark never celebrated Lolth's gifts with comfort, either. The surface world would adjust. It always does, once it understands that the pain on offer comes with genuine healing attached.

I looked at the faint burn marks tracing Bryn's forearms and felt the clean satisfaction of an arrangement beginning to hold. Lathander was attentive. The pain was landing where I had directed it. Whether Harbin Wester and his council would recognize this as spiritual growth, public service, civic liability, or some mixture of the three remained to be seen.

I folded my hands and let Harbin's festival continue around me, and watched, and waited, and filed everything away.

---

## Vukradin — The Road to the Druid Council

The road out of Phandalin smells like pine resin and cold mud, the kind of morning where the mountains are still wrapped in cloud and the light hasn't quite committed to being light yet. I have my flute in my hand — not playing, just holding it, the way some people hold worry beads — and I am thinking about Adabra.

I like Adabra. This is inconvenient, given everything.

She has always been direct with me. Fair, too, in her way. The last time we spoke about the grove, she listened to what I told her, accepted it, and paid me five gold pieces for the trouble, which is not how villains behave in songs or in life, generally speaking. We simply mean very different things. She thinks Soma carrying Meril's Staff is a violation of something sacred and structural. I think Soma is perhaps the most careful person I know, and that sacred structures are occasionally wrong. This seemed to me, at the time, like exactly the kind of disagreement that would improve once I had the chance to speak with her properly and remind everyone that they were all trying to protect the same land. I know what words can do when they are chosen well. I also know what happens when an audience decides it likes you. I was once a much purer artist than I am now, and purity, while very satisfying to the person practicing it, pays terribly. Give the audience what it needs, let them believe it was what they wanted, and suddenly everyone is much happier to hear.

Then Thomas found us on the road to the council, and the hoping became somewhat harder. We were going there to defend Lyra, which is the sort of sentence that sounds noble until you notice how often noble sentences are just weather reports for political traps.

He materialized the way Lyra's people do — not from nowhere, exactly, but from the specific nowhere that well-trained agents occupy when they want to seem casual about it. He had the look of someone delivering bad news they'd been rehearsing. One of Lyra's trusted agents, carrying Lyra's anxieties, which are considerable and usually well-founded. He told us Adabra was moving to ostracize Lyra.

Ostracize. Not debate. Not challenge. *Ostracize.*

I turned this word over a few times while Thomas kept talking. Ostracism is not a philosophical disagreement resolved through patient dialogue. Ostracism is a verdict. It means Adabra has already done the arithmetic, already knows the votes, and called this council not to discover an outcome but to announce one. That is a different kind of gathering than I had been preparing for.

The detail that landed heaviest: Reidoth was away. The nominal head of the local Emerald Enclave chapter — absent. And Adabra had called the council during his absence.

I have sat on the Privy Council. I know exactly what it looks like when someone calls a meeting they intend to win before it starts. You pick your moment. You pick your audience. You make sure the people most likely to complicate your majority are occupied elsewhere. It is not dishonest, precisely. It is just the architecture of a predetermined conclusion.

"Even if Reidoth were present," Thomas said, with the particular weariness of someone who has thought about this extensively, "he could not stop Adabra from speaking."

No. Of course not. But presence matters. Presence is a kind of pressure, even when it cannot be translated into procedural obstruction. Reidoth standing in that glade, saying nothing, watching — that changes the texture of what Adabra can do. His absence gives her cleaner acoustics for her purposes.

We talked about reaching Reidoth. We kept it as an option, held in reserve, not dismissed — the way you keep an alternate chord progression in mind when you're not sure how the main one is going to land. Maybe. Maybe it becomes relevant. We did not send for him yet.

What I kept turning over, walking that road with the flute still unplayed in my hand, was the timing. Not the ambition — I understand ambition, even ambition I disagree with — but the *precision* of the timing. This was not opportunism. This was a scheduled event. Adabra had looked at the calendar, identified the window, and placed her move there. That is something closer to certainty than confidence. She believes she has the numbers.

Soma was ahead of me on the path, her shell catching the grey morning light, Meril's Staff in hand. The staff that is the whole of this argument, the artifact around which all of Adabra's parliamentary maneuvering has been constructed. The leaves on it have been diminishing. Soma knows this. She carries it anyway, and she carries it because Meril trusted her with it, and because something that powerful needs someone careful rather than someone credentialed.

I believe that. I believe it plainly and without reservation.

The question — the one I was walking with, the one Thomas's warning had sharpened into something with an actual edge — was whether believing it would be sufficient. Whether the council we were walking toward could be moved by the truth of it, or whether it had already been arranged to reach a different conclusion.

When we talked through our strategy, Soma said the thing plainly, in that steady way of hers: she was not going to apologize for interventionism. Not soften it or reframe it into something more palatable to druidic conservatism. She had intervened because the situation required intervention. If that was the argument, then that was the argument.

I heard her. I did. And then, because I am very good at words and very accustomed to being useful with them, I made the small, fatal translation from *Soma should say this* to *I can make Adabra's case look weaker before Soma has to say it*. It felt natural at the time. Helpful, even. If Adabra had arranged the council, then surely the first thing to do was loosen the arrangement. Find the weak beam. Press there. Let the audience hear the creak.

It did not occur to me, walking that road, that I might become one more thing standing between Soma and the moment she needed. That realization would require a much less comfortable glade.

The mountains were still in cloud. The road wound on ahead through the trees. I turned the flute once between my fingers and thought: *read the council when you get there, Vukradin. You don't know the acoustics yet.*

But I had a feeling. And the feeling was that this was going to require every word I had.

---

## Soma — Adabra's Accusation

The staff told me what I needed to know.

I did not like it.

The glade was already full when we arrived. Druids settled in the grass. Hands on knees. Eyes forward. The air smelled of damp leaves and old bark. No kettle. No roof. No polite little pause where everyone pretends this will be simple.

Adabra did not waste time making anyone comfortable.

I held Meril's staff and used its power of Resonance to see if she was controlled by some alien power. 

I pressed it toward Adabra and held the question steady in my mind: was she deceived, was she controlled, was she lying to herself, was something wearing her conviction like a coat.

The staff answered plain.

No. No. No.

Vukradin muttered it before I could say anything myself. "Yeah, no, she's a true believer."

There it was.

I had been watching Adabra since she opened her mouth. She did not raise her voice. Did not wave her hands. Did not dress anger up as grief. She stood there steady as a fencepost sunk deep.

I knew that kind of steady. Coastal folk get it when they have tied the same knots for forty years and think the sea owes them agreement.

It does not.

"I will be brief," she said. "I am not here to argue philosophy with you. I'm here to tell you what I found."

I believed her. That was the problem.

She had walked to the sacred grove in the Whispering Wood herself. Three weeks ago. Her own feet. Her own eyes. The treant was dead. Roots collapsed. Crown black. The grove ruined around it.

Not wounded. Not sleeping. Dead.

She said it like someone setting stones in a row. No extra weight. No wasted breath.

I looked down at the staff in my hands.

For a long time it had just been the thing Meril left in my cabin. A good staff. A friend's staff. Something to keep near the door with the walking sticks and old line. I did not know it would become a question everyone else expected me to answer.

The leaves were more than before.

I noticed. Of course I noticed. You notice when something old in your hands begins to wake up. You notice when the net mends itself in the night. You notice when an old tool changes in your grip.

Meril had come to me late. Stayed longer than most travelers. Took tea. Taught me names for things I had been doing by instinct for years. Then he left me this staff and moved on.

Old shell, new druid. No use pretending otherwise.

Adabra wasn't finished.

"The last time I spoke with this party, I believed the ritual had failed. The tree had still lived. When I heard there was a planar creature collecting materials near the grove, I was — I will say this plainly — pleased. I called it a planar magpie. I said it was the world working as it should. I gave the bard five gold pieces for what he told me."

She didn't look at Vukradin when she said it. That was almost worse.

"That day, I believed him."

I have known shell-sprouts who lied to my face and I've known shell-sprouts who lied to everyone including themselves and I've known shell-sprouts who told the truth and watched people not believe them anyway. Vukradin has a gift for making things sound true. It's not a flaw, exactly — it's the thing that makes him useful and frustrating in equal measure. But Adabra had walked to the grove. She had pressed Lyra's people for answers. She had spoken with others who were there that night. She had found the gap between what was reported and what existed, and then found more gaps where the stories should have joined. And then she had come here.

"I'm not asking you to condemn anyone today," she said. "I'm asking you to look at what I found before you listen to anyone tell you what it means."

Falcon was sitting beside her. I noted that the way I note a change in weather — not judgment, just fact. Falcon, who has been watching us since before we were worth watching. Falcon, who has opinions about how the balance should be kept and who should keep it. Falcon, sitting next to Adabra in the council circle, while she laid out a case against us with the calm precision of someone who has had weeks to be certain.

The staff was quiet in my hands.

Vukradin tried. He is nothing if not persistent, my bard shell-sprout, and he has survived worse audiences than this one. He leaned into the cross-examination the way he leans into most things: charm held back, just enough space for logic.

"Between when you heard this account — which you claim was false — and when you saw that the treant was dead. How much time between those two points?"

Adabra: "Two weeks."

"Two weeks. A lot can happen in two weeks. How do you know that what you heard from me wasn't true, and that the treant died within that two-week period before you personally went and saw that it had died? You don't know that."

It was a reasonable point. It was the kind of reasonable point that sounds convincing right up until you look at the woman it's aimed at and realize she's already considered it, walked around it, and decided it doesn't change what she found.

Adabra didn't answer that moment — she didn't need to, not yet. The council would weigh it. Maybe some of them would find purchase in it.

Then Thomas stood up. And Lyra. And they said the quiet part in full sentences, out loud, in front of everyone:

"Lyra asked us to go deal with the metastasized treant. We tried, we failed, the party showed up, they killed it."

No shame in Thomas's voice. None in Lyra's either. Not defiance exactly. Just: *this is what happened*. They had failed to save the metastasized treant, the party had killed it, and the killing had stopped a planar infestation from spreading farther. The same register Adabra had been using the whole time. The same weight.

I held the staff a little tighter and said nothing.

The thing about true believers is that they are hard to beat with facts, because they have facts too. Adabra went to the grove. Fact. The treant was dead. Fact. The grove was ruined. Fact. Vukradin had given her a story that did not match what she found. Fact. We killed the treant because something worse was coming through. Also fact.

Facts do not always sit together nicely. Anyone who has kept a small house knows that. The roof can need mending and the fish can need cleaning and the guest can need feeding all at once. None of those things stop being true because your hands are full.

I am old. Old enough to know nature is not one thing with one answer. Old enough to know letting rot spread is also a choice.

But I was new to this. New to councils. New to being looked at like I had stolen something holy. New to holding Meril's staff while people who had worn the Enclave longer than I had worn the word druid decided what it meant.

And the staff kept answering me.

That was the part I did not like to say out loud. The more I stepped in, the more it woke up. The more I stopped waiting for the world to settle itself, the more power came into my hands. Not owned. Borrowed. Maybe offered. Maybe expected.

Maybe Meril knew.

Maybe he had stood between these two ideas too long. Let things be. Step in. Trust the wilds. Protect them. Maybe he had not found the answer. Maybe he thought some old tortle from a quiet cabin, who had spent more years keeping paths safe than arguing doctrine, might see what the rest of them had handled too often to see clearly.

Or maybe he just left a friend a staff.

Both could be true. That was the problem with old friends. They leave you simple things that get heavier later.

Adabra would call that the problem. She isn't wrong that there's a problem. She's wrong about what it is.

I could not prove that to her.

The staff had already told me so.

---

## Vukradin — The Emerald Enclave Schism

The staff had already told us the worst useful thing: Adabra was not enchanted, deceived, or consciously lying. She believed every word she was saying.

That should have simplified matters. It did not.

A liar gives you something to expose. A puppet gives you strings to cut. A true believer gives you a circle full of people nodding along because conviction sounds very much like truth if no one interrupts it in time.

So I interrupted.

Soma had said what she meant to do. She was not going to apologize for interventionism. She was not going to pretend the party had been passive observers when we had, in fact, intervened. She meant to stand there and say the difficult thing plainly.

I heard her.

And then I did what I do when a crowd starts leaning the wrong way: I stepped toward the lean.

Not to convince Adabra. That was not the move. Adabra was rooted. The move was to make the council hear the weakness in her case before Soma had to carry the whole weight of it. If Adabra had arranged the council, then the arrangement needed loosening. A little pressure at the right joint. A little dissonance. Give the audience permission to doubt the first melody they had been handed.

This was, I believed, helpful.

I was very nearly wrong in an instructive way.

Then Falcon stood up.

I knew Falcon. Or I thought I did — the comfortable retired adventurer at his comfortable hunting lodge, the man who had once asked us to protect that lodge and whose fire we had watched from a distance when it burned. There was a version of Falcon I had assembled from available evidence: melancholy, yes, and perhaps a little embittered, but fundamentally on the right side of things. People who build hunting lodges and invite heroes in for meals are not, in my experience, the kind who turn on you in council circles.

I had not updated this model recently.

"I came because someone finally sat long enough to listen," he said. His voice had the particular weight of a man who has rehearsed his grievances until they feel like philosophy. "You can keep cheering your victories. I've seen what comes after them."

This, I thought, was a general statement. A mood. The kind of thing a retired adventurer says when the joints ache and the trophies have gathered dust. I was still being charitable.

Then he pointed at me.

"She's right! She's right about what needed to happen. You all wanted it simple. Well, this is what simple looks like. Trees are dead because of men like *him.*"

The circle. The entire circle, looking at me.

I want to be precise about what I felt in that moment, because I think precision matters here and I am, above all things, a precise person. It was not embarrassment. I have been on stages, I have been reviewed badly, I have played to audiences that did not want to be played to. Embarrassment is something I have developed a tolerance for. What I felt was closer to — bewilderment. A specific bewilderment, the kind that comes when someone changes the key signature mid-phrase and doesn't tell you, and suddenly you are hearing a song you thought you knew as something entirely different and you cannot find where you went wrong.

Trees are dead because of men like him.

Men like me.

I pulled Falcon aside. I needed to understand, yes, but I also needed him to stop making me the evidence. There is a difference. I am at my best when I am finding the note that lets everyone come in together. I am not at my best when someone points at me in public and tells the circle I am the rot in the tree.

"What's going on here?" I asked him. "Like, I thought we were… Why are you pointing me out in front of this group? What did I do?"

He said: "You intervened, and in your intervention, people died. Adabra sees it clearly."

There it was. Adabra had given him a song simple enough to sing while wounded. Intervention leads to death. Regret becomes wisdom. Everyone still fighting the fire becomes responsible for the smoke.

I like Adabra. I do. She had been fair to me once, and I do not discard that lightly. But she had found Falcon at his most hollow and handed him a frame that made his pain feel principled. He was wearing it like a good coat.

"Didn't you used to intervene?" I asked. "Like, what… why the change of heart?"

"And look what it got me."

I looked at Falcon. I thought about the lodge. The comfortable life. The years of reputation and the stories people told at festivals about the great Falcon of the hunting lodge. I thought about all of it, and I thought about the fact that he was standing in this circle pointing his finger at me while wearing boots that adventuring had paid for.

"As far as I can tell," I said, keeping my voice even, keeping it musical, keeping it in the register where things land cleanly, "it got you a beautiful hunting lodge, and lots of fame, and glory… and a whole life built on a lie. Got you a lot, that intervention. But now, now… you're gonna screw me over."

He started to speak. I did not let him finish.

"I could tell everybody about what you did. About how you intervened."

This is not a threat I am proud of. I want to note that. I am not a man who thinks of himself as making threats. But when someone attacks me, something in me reaches for the nearest sharp instrument, and unfortunately I am usually carrying several in my mouth. I had done nothing Falcon had not done. I had done it for the same reasons he once had. And he was standing there in the council circle using me as his confession booth, pointing at me as his evidence of the world's wrongness, and I found that I was angry in a way that was clean and simple and not particularly musical.

"Ever since I spoke to Adabra," he said, dismissively, "I have learned the error of my ways."

"Well then," I said, "you should have no trouble repenting in front of the group and explaining the errors *he* made."

I meant it as a calling of the bluff. What I had not accounted for was that Falcon would call it back.

He stood. He addressed the council. And what followed was — I will give him this much — a genuine confession. He told them about his adventuring days. About the dragons he had failed to fight. About the lodge that burned while heroes stood by. About the baubles and the money and the way nature had eventually torn it all down regardless. He wept, a little, I think, or something close to it. "Once again, nature tore it down," he said. "Once again, the Falcon Lodge was burnt. Once again, I asked heroes to intervene, to protect my lodge, and what did they do? They did nothing, and my lodge burned down."

It was, objectively, a very good speech.

It was also, objectively, still entirely about him.

I had wanted him to confess that his attack on me was hypocritical. What he had done instead was confess that his entire life was tragic, which is not the same thing, and which — somehow — made him more sympathetic to the council rather than less. I had handed him a spotlight and he had stepped into it beautifully and used it to make himself the protagonist of a story about his own failure, and the circle was watching him with something close to compassion, and I was still standing there being the thing trees had died because of.

I am a musician. I know when an audience has turned.

"I'm going back to Phandalin," I said. "You all can stay. I do not like the personal insults. I'm not interested in helping Lyra anymore."

Nobody tried to stop me. That was its own information.

Behind me, as I left the circle, I heard Brewbarry — loyal, enormous, dear Brewbarry — ask the remaining party the only question that had ever truly been in his register: "Do we kill all these people, or do we leave?"

And Soma, patient as tide and twice as inevitable, pulled him back from it. "Without the druids, the land is even more screwed than with half of them."

I was already away from the circle. The trees were easier to look at than the council, which was a relief.

I had one thing left I could do from here. Sending is not the sort of spell bards are expected to have unless they have made a few unusual choices, and Magical Secrets is a wonderfully literal name for the sort of choice I had made. I reached for the particular resonance that connected me to Reidoth — the old ranger, the man who actually understood what these woods were before they became anyone's ideology — and I sent my voice toward him across the distance between us, the way a melody travels through open air. I told him what was happening. The schism. The council. Adabra's faction and Falcon's confession and the circle that had turned before anyone had voted on anything.

Reidoth needed to know. Someone sensible needed to know.

Then I walked back toward Phandalin, with the sound of the council fading behind me, and I composed nothing. Some things don't want to be songs yet. Some things need to sit and get cold first before you can find the key they're actually in.

---

## Soma — The Druid Council Debate

The moment Vukradin walked away, every eye in the circle came to me.

Right.

Just me, then.

My knees hurt. My hands hurt. The staff sat in my grip like it had always belonged there, which was a lie. I had not been a druid long enough for anything to always belong anywhere. I was an old tortle with a late lesson, a friend's staff, and too many people looking at me like I had answers stored somewhere under my shell.

I am not naturally a speaker. I am a watcher. I sit with things. Weather. Tracks. Sick soil. A traveler talking too loudly over tea. You wait long enough, most things show you what they are.

Then you act.

Usually without an audience.

Meril never made speeches when he stayed with me. He made decisions. Quiet ones. He taught me names for things I had already known in my bones. Then he left me the staff, and I thought that was the end of it.

Shell sprout thinking, maybe. Even old shells can sprout in the wrong places.

Aldric Stone Path stood there with his arms folded and his jaw set. Weathered man. Mountain man. The kind of man who has said the same thing for years and been treated like wind through grass. Now someone had finally listened, and he had fastened himself to her for it.

I understood that.

Adabra had listened to him. That mattered.

But listening isn't the same as solving.

"I don't know how you don't see those as a problem as well, sir." I kept my voice level. Not soft, not hard — the voice I use when I'm telling someone a plain fact they've been avoiding. "She's bringing in yet another problem — these planar anomalies, these infestations, these blights on the land. Adabra says let nature take its course and get obliterated, basically." I let that sit for a breath. "What does Adabra say the solution is?"

Aldric opened his mouth. Closed it.

I was not trying to shame him. I wanted the answer. If there was one, I wanted it plain. I had spent too many hours watching the staff lose leaves and wake up new powers in my hands to pretend I knew everything.

Old in the body. New in the craft.

Bad combination some days.

Adabra herself stepped into the silence. Her voice was careful, measured — the voice of someone who has given this argument many times and believes it completely.

"The displacement is downstream of the intervention. The answer is to stop the cascade by withdrawing. The ecosystem finds equilibrium without help."

Equilibrium.

I had heard that word a lot lately. People like it. Smooth word. Clean edges. Sounds like a pond going still after someone stops throwing rocks.

That was not what we had seen.

I thought of the planar rift at Falcon's Cabin. Soil gone grey. Grass dying in rings. Talosians with their hands in the earth like they were teaching it to rot. That was not a pond settling. That was a wound under a bandage you were too polite to lift.

"Equilibrium comes in many forms." I heard my own voice come out quieter than I expected — not because I was uncertain, but because I wanted them to lean in. "One of these equilibriums could be the end of everything, of all life." I met Adabra's eyes. "That's not the equilibrium I'm here to protect."

Aldric sat down.

There it was.

Adabra noticed. I saw her notice. Her face did not change much, but something behind it shifted. Like a knot pulling tight.

She had lost something.

Not enough.

Then the centaur arrived.

I had not expected a centaur. Maybe I should have. Xanthopoulos knew the high valleys. His people saw what moved through mountain passes before lowland folk finished arguing about whether anything was moving at all.

Still. A centaur arriving in the middle of a druid council makes a person pause.

He was deliberate in the way that old creatures are deliberate. Not slow — considered. He looked at the circle. He looked at Adabra. He looked at me.
