Lightning doesn't feel like much in the instant it touches you — it's the half-second after, when every muscle in your body seizes and your teeth crack together and the light burns a white line across your vision. The bolt came down the gully and found Vukradin, found Xanth, found me. I heard the centaur curse in words I didn't know, the kind of raw syllables that mean the same thing in every language. I was still standing — barely — but the hit had reorganized something inside me, pulled the air out of my chest and replaced it with a kind of ringing absence. The anchorite stood at the far end of the field looking pleased with himself.

That was a mistake on his part.

Xanth got his longbow up fast — two shots, both of them high, lost in the trees. He looked disgusted with himself in the way only someone who genuinely expected better can look.

Then Soma moved.

I have watched Soma transform before. It never gets less strange. One moment there is a tortle elder, patient and heavy-shelled, and then the air warps and there is something enormous where she was — a giant elk, head down, already moving, already building speed before the form has fully settled. She hit the anchorite like a wall of forest. The ram drove him into the dirt and kept going, and when the dust cleared he was on his belly, prone, and Soma's voice came out of that massive throat with something like satisfaction: *On your belly.*

The anchorite had a moment to consider what it meant to insult a druid.

Around me, the orcs were finding their angles. One went for Vukradin again. Another tried Soma's elk form and missed. A third — one of the ones that was supposed to be neutral, which is a thing I find funny, the idea of a neutral orc in a fight — launched a javelin at the elk and got through. I saw Soma absorb it. She didn't go down, but she was eating damage now, which meant the clock was running.

Then Boney arrived.

Boney is a skeleton on a horse. I mean — Boney is a skeletal horse who carries a sword and fights like he knows what he's doing, because apparently that is a thing that happens when Soma is involved. He came through the middle of everything, found the prone anchorite, and hit him hard with that longsword. Two-handed, because Boney doesn't carry a shield. Of course he doesn't. He's a skeleton. I found myself briefly grateful that I didn't have to explain this to myself anymore.

The anchorite was still breathing. Not for long.

Orc 7 decided to charge into the cluster of us, which was brave, if unwise. My body moved before I decided to move — the halberd was already coming up, the first swing gone wide, and then Vukradin's gift was still in my hands, the advantage still warm, and the second swing connected with everything I had behind it. The orc went down hard and didn't get up.

From somewhere to my left, Vukradin's voice carried that particular note of someone who is trying to sound irritated but is actually just amused:

*"How are we gonna win them over if you're slaughtering them?"*

I didn't have an answer for that. The orc had charged. The sword was already out. What exactly was I supposed to do.

Valphine spoke for me, which was fair: *"But he came to attack us."*

And then it was my turn.

I raged. There is something that happens in that moment — not loss of self, not quite, but a simplification. The gully got cleaner. The anchorite was prone. He had lit up Vukradin with lightning. He had lit up Vukradin.

I moved through the two orcs between me and the anchorite. They both took their swings. Neither of them connected. Sometimes the world wants you to get somewhere, and it steps aside. I brought the halberd down on the prone anchorite — once, with the weight of Bless behind it, with the certainty of Vukradin's gift still singing faintly in my muscles — and the hit was clean and total. I could feel how little was left in him.

The second swing missed. But then Soma pointed out the obvious thing — he was prone, which meant I had advantage — and Valphine rolled again.

Eighteen.

The anchorite died.

Vukradin: *"Alright!"* And then, because he is who he is: *"No more killing things, let's convert everybody!"*

I stood over the body and thought about that for a moment.

Orc 9 came at Boney next, which I hadn't expected — something about the skeleton offended him, maybe, or maybe Boney just had the look of something valuable. I saw the swing coming and I stepped into it. The hit landed on me instead. While raging it felt like being tapped on a bruise — real, but containable. Boney lived. I figured Soma would want that.

On the other side of the gully, Orc 10 threw a javelin at Valphine and missed completely, the kind of miss that has a story behind it. Orc 11 got through the elk's side for something.

Then Orc 2 — the first one, the one Vukradin had redirected — apparently decided he'd been patient enough with all this fighting. He put his greataxe down. Not dropped it. Set it down. Deliberate. And then he said, in the way that men say things when they've run the numbers and don't like the result:

*"Give me money and I let you live."*

I looked at Vukradin. Vukradin looked at the orc.

*"The followers of Lathander don't make such demands."*

The orc picked his greataxe back up. Of course he did. He swung. And I heard, underneath the impact, underneath Vukradin's grunt of pain, the orc's whole logic laid out in the way he pulled back: *I don't want to kill you, man, I just want the money.*

Xanth's second round of shots went high. Both of them.

And then Soma, who had been taking hits in elk form and absorbing them one by one, made a decision. She stepped back, took the attacks of opportunity that came with moving — one of them connecting, one glancing off nothing — and then she dropped the form. Just let it go. The elk vanished. Soma was a tortle again, smaller, standing in the middle of a gully full of orcs, and she climbed onto Boney's back like it was the most natural thing in the world.

*"So we get the abomination and the delicious meat away from the guys,"* she said.

Boney moved. Orc 9 swung at him as he went. Missed.

And that was how it sat — the anchorite dead in the dirt, the orcs still circling, the gully still loud with the smell of lightning and blood and something that might have been possibility, if Vukradin had anything to say about it.

He usually did.

---

## Valphine — The Rout Of The Talosians

The anchorite went down hard. Brewbarry's axe found him before the man had finished a prayer, and I watched the last breath leave a fanatic of Talos with the same clinical attention I bring to most deaths — cataloguing it. The grammar of that moment: a priest of the storm-god, prone, exposed, cut down by a Goliath who was running through javelin fire to get there. Clean. I had assessed the tactical situation and directed Brewbarry toward the more dangerous caster first. In Menzoberranzan, you kill the spellcaster. You kill the spellcaster before anything else. Some lessons survive the border crossing.

I am not without sympathy for what Talos offers. I understand the appeal of a violent god — the permission structure, the theology that places destruction at the center rather than at the margin. In House Sotorra, cruelty was doctrine. Not worship exactly, but something adjacent: the acknowledgment that power expresses itself through pain, and that the god who sanctions your pain is the god worth serving. I have known that grammar. I left it behind not because it was cruel but because it was *small* — rationed through hierarchy, spent carefully, always in danger of running out. Talos is the same operation. Storms end. The sun does not.

Still. The priest had believed in something. That is worth noting.

Vukradin was already speaking in rounds, his voice working the gully below with something that is, I think, the most honest magic he does. Universal Speech is a strange faculty — not persuasion exactly, more like the removal of a barrier, the linguistic distance collapsed so that the argument can reach the person directly. He rolled a twenty-six. I watched the orcs on the ridgeline go through several stages in rapid succession: hostility, calculation, the particular slowing that happens when an idea enters a mind that wasn't expecting one. By the time he finished the passage, he had moved seven of them away from the fight. He had been speaking to Lathander the entire time before he corrected himself to say it. The slip was interesting. I filed it away.

My own contribution was less elegant.

The orcs speak their own language and I speak it too — one of many things from my education I have never bothered to discard. I called on them to kneel before the power of Lathander. I said it with the register one uses for commands to inferiors, which in my experience functions as a reasonable default. What I had not sufficiently accounted for was the compound liability: drow, undead skeletal horse, unstructured presentation, mid-combat exhortation. The orcs did not find the combination inspiring. One who had been neutral reconsidered his neutrality in my direction and returned to hostility. I was informed, with some diplomatic candor, that *orcs don't like drows.* This is not untrue. And I was informed that *this was not the best organized religious ceremony.* This is also not untrue.

The truth is that a drow cleric of Lathander leading a disorganized warband that includes a skeletal horse and a barbarian in a dread helm with glowing red eyes does not read as a coherent theological proposition to an orc who was already uncertain. Surface religious ceremonies have *structure* for a reason — ritual is the scaffolding that makes unfamiliar power legible. We had offered none. We had offered combat and a persuasive aasimar bard, which is better than nothing but not quite a creed.

*We gotta work on the spectacle a bit,* Vukradin said, which was rueful enough that I nearly said something kind in response. I didn't.

Brewbarry sent one fleeing orc off with a swing that missed — on purpose, we agreed, in the way that certain misses are on purpose, or at least that is how I have elected to record it. The halberd said *who has the violence*. The miss said *you are not worth the expenditure*. The orc understood both messages and ran. That is a reasonable outcome.

Orc nine did not receive the same consideration. I told Brewbarry to stomp him, and Brewbarry did, with the kind of directness that makes him an effective ally and a suboptimal negotiating partner. The non-lethal call arrived a moment too late. Someone noted it. Then: *too late.* Then: *alright, next time.* Vukradin was rueful again. I said, dryly, that Brewbarry would say *oops, sorry*, which is approximately what happened. The orc was dead. We had not intended it. These things accumulate.

Orc ten was handled more precisely. I called the target, declared the strike non-lethal with enough lead time to matter, and Brewbarry put the orc down cleanly, unconscious. Fifteen points of force through a non-lethal strike. One hit. The orc crumpled and stayed. Good. I had wanted at least one of them awake for afterward.

Then the flight.

There is a specific quality to the moment when a warband decides to stop being one. It does not happen in unison — it cannot, because each individual is performing a private calculation, and those calculations do not complete simultaneously. What you observe from the outside is a cascade: one orc decides the arithmetic no longer works, turns, runs. The next sees him go and revises his own figures. Then another. The whole structure dissolves not because anyone surrendered but because everyone, separately, concluded that the available options had narrowed to survival.

Orcs one, four, five, and eight. Then eleven. Then six — who *had* been receptive, who had been moving toward the argument, who looked around at the field and the fleeing companions and the bright light that is deliciously painful for me and decided that life was too short. He ran. I watched him go.

Vukradin called out to orc three as the man bolted: *see the way to Lathander!* The invitation was genuine, which is one of the more interesting things about him. He converts by reflex. He cannot watch a man run away from an argument without wanting to give him the last line. Orc three ran anyway.

What I felt, watching the gully empty, was not triumph. Something more complicated than that — the specific satisfaction of seeing a violent hierarchy disassemble, knowing I understand every structural reason it held together as long as it did and every structural reason it could not hold any longer. The anchorite was the keystone. Brewbarry removed him in the first exchange. Without the spellcaster, without the holy sanction, the orcs were a warband without architecture. Even the one who had believed — even the one who had thrown his javelin at the skeletal horse because an undead abomination offended his god — eventually ran.

I have known worse hierarchies than this one. I have served inside them, been assessed by them, been found insufficient by them. There is a particular flavour of satisfaction in watching the machinery of a smaller power come apart, when you are standing in the light of a larger one.

The gully was quiet. One orc unconscious. The rest: fled or dead.

Vukradin had been speaking to Lathander before he corrected himself.

I did not say anything about that.

---

## Vukradin — The Conversion Of Prutha

The last orc standing looked at me with the specific expression of a man who has just watched nine of his fellows die and is working out the arithmetic of the situation. He was holding his greataxe. I was watching his eyes.

I said everything I had to say to him. The words came from the same place they always come from — not performance, not calculation, but the genuine conviction that there is a true frame available to any moment and I am reaching for it. The sun above the gully. The Drow cleric wreathed in impossible light. The dead anchorite of Talos cooling behind us. I laid it out for him. The picture was honest. It required no embellishment.

He set down the greataxe.

He looked at Valphine — this radiant, painful, sunlit figure that no orc who grew up praying to the Stormlord could have anticipated encountering in a forest gully — and the working-out in his eyes resolved. He spoke in halting Common, his voice somewhere between wonder and practical assessment.

"Talos offer power. Lathander — more power. Lathander all-powerful."

Then he rushed toward Valphine and announced himself a follower of the Drow Cleric of Lathander.

I said, honestly: *"Wow. We've got a follower!"*

Because that was exactly what we had.

His name was Prutha. The party began debating conversion names before I could object — which I did, loudly, the moment Valphine suggested *Thrall.*

"Harsh, man, harsh. Wow." I let the word sit there. Then: "You know, I object. You know, he doesn't know what thrall means, but I know what thrall means. That is not appropriate for our new friend."

Prutha kept his name. As it should be.

What he told us next required more than just the one moment to take in. His great-uncle had been his clan's shaman. The Talosians killed him deliberately, early, to break the clan's resistance and make recruitment easier. They called the old man weak, abomination, thunder meat. And then they stood in front of what remained of his family and offered the terms Talos always offers: fight, or be what his great-uncle was.

Prutha had fought. Three skirmishes. Never killed by choice.

I thought about Corbin — briefly, painfully, the way I always think about Corbin now, a flash of the face and then the weight settling. I had pushed a man into something similar and called it recruiting. I wasn't going to do that accounting here, in the middle of a gully. But the thought was present.

Then Prutha reached into his clothing and produced a small bone whistle.

He explained that in his clan, the orcs had greeted the sunrise each morning by blowing a bone whistle. Eastern tradition, his great-uncle's tradition. He held it up. He looked at it for a long moment.

And then he blew it.

One note. Simple and thin and very old.

And then he began to cry.

Valphine read him with a sharpness I could not have matched — later she told me her read was simply that he'd realized how long it had been since he last blew it. That was what the tears were for. Not the dead uncle. Not the three skirmishes. Not the men who died in the gully behind him. The whistle. The gap between the whistle then and the whistle now.

I found I had nothing to say for a moment.

Then Prutha gave us the line his great-uncle had apparently lived by, the thing the old shaman had passed down before the Talosians named him thunder meat and put him in the ground:

*"Dawn comes for everyone, even those born in shadow."*

The table went quiet.

"Wow," I said. "It's quite poetic."

I meant it completely. I said to Prutha — honestly, reaching for the right thing to say to a man who had just blown a whistle for the first time in what seemed like years: "You're my first."

And then the thought arrived, the one I voiced aloud because it was obvious and true: "He wants to play the whistle at the dawn, but with Lathander, it's like it's the dawn all the time. So, like, he could play the whistle all the time."

The theology here was not complicated. Lathander is perpetual dawn. The whistle greets the dawn. The math resolves. I was genuinely pleased with how cleanly it fit.

Valphine reached for a Religion assessment of the situation and came back with a result she described only as "oh my god" in a tone that suggested the dice had not cooperated. Despite this, some kind of genuine insight arrived anyway — the notion that the whistle ritual could be woven into her own evolving practice, that Lathander was not moving further from her for it, but closer. I thought about Valphine's church, which the party had described diplomatically as a masochism cult, and then thought about the bone whistle and the weeping orc and the words about dawn and shadow, and I thought: well, this is how religions acquire depth. It is not always tidy.

I turned to Prutha and delivered what I had to give him.

"Lathander blesses you today, my child."

He looked at me. "What does that mean?"

"Lathander blesses you today, my child."

He thought about it. Then: "He thinks that's awesome." He had, apparently, lots to think about.

"Lathander — power. Beyond doubt. Lathander all-powerful." He turned back to Valphine and pledged himself again, firmly, in case anyone had missed it the first time.

Then he kicked the fallen anchorite's body several times to make a point about theological transition.

I disapproved of the corpse-stringing that followed — when Valphine and Brewbarry hauled the anchorite up as a warning, Prutha was visibly enthusiastic in a way that landed somewhere I preferred not to examine — but I didn't intervene. I had a follower. He had just blown a bone whistle and wept and recited a line of genuine beauty in his second language. I was prepared to allow him the kicks.

He laid out his terms for the path forward: orcs no longer following Talos, we spare. Committed Talosians are another matter entirely.

Convert or die. A fluid approach to religion, yes. But it worked for the anchorite in the other direction, didn't it? The Talosians had offered exactly that framing to Prutha's great-uncle, and the only difference between that moment and this one is which door leads somewhere worth walking through.

I asked about the orc who was still unconscious somewhere behind us. Whether he might be revivable, convertible, another Prutha waiting to emerge.

The answer was uncertain. We pressed on.

Someone — I will not say who — made a joke about whether Corbin would have liked Prutha.

The DM's response was that it was cold.

He was right. It was cold. And also, yes. Corbin would have liked Prutha very much. He would have recognized something in the whistle, in the terms offered and refused and survived through compromise, in the great-uncle's line about dawn and shadow. He would have been moved by it.

I was moved by it. I was carrying that, along with everything else.

---

## Soma — Arrival At The Circle Of Thunder

The last thing Xanth did before he left was make everyone uncomfortable.

He stood there, that enormous centaur, and looked at the staff the way you look at something you already know the history of. Not like he was admiring it. Like he was remembering. Then he turned to me — tapped me, actually, on the shell, his hand broad enough to cover half of it — and said, "This one, this one was blessed by Meril, and I know that she will do great things. Peace, Beauty."

Shell-sprouts. The whole lot of them were watching me after that.

I did not say anything. There is not much to say when a centaur you met two days ago understands something about you that you are still arguing with yourself about in the dark hours before dawn. The staff sat in my grip, quiet. Three leafs on the thing. I have been watching those leaves since Xanth told me Meril chose me on purpose, which is the kind of information you receive and then carry at arm's length while you figure out what to do with it.

Xanth looked at the staff one final time. Just looked at it. He was doing something with that look — measuring, or saying goodbye to it, or confirming something for himself — and yes, it made everybody feel a bit awkward. Vukradin broke the silence first, because Vukradin always breaks the silence first.

"Hooves off," he said, appreciating the phrase as Xanth did exactly that.

I watched the centaur go until the trees took him. Then: "You call that hooving. I'll show you hooving."

Nobody asked what I meant by that. Good. I wasn't sure I knew.

Prutha filled the space that Xanth left. She has that gift — or it is something else — of knowing exactly how to push forward when standing still feels like a waste of everyone's time. "We go kill other Talosian clerics," she said, and that was the plan summarized. Vukradin fussed with the name of the quest for a moment, the way he does when he needs to feel like things are organized properly: *Xanth the Centaur has offered to take us to the lair of the evil half-orc spellcasters.* Yes. And now Xanth had done his part. So.

We walked.

The hill rose out of the treeline before the storm did, which meant I saw it first as a shape — ninety feet of slope, trees gripping the sides of it like they were holding on. And then the clouds. They were already there above the crest, the kind of grey-green build that means something is gathering itself on purpose. Not weather. Intent.

The prepared description came all at once when we cleared the trees: a ring of standing stones at the summit, ancient and deliberate, and inside the ring — two figures moving. Dancing, in the loose sense of the word, in that way that is less celebration and more negotiation with something that does not speak. And around them: small, twitching, many-limbed shapes, capering.

"They're doing, like, a fire dance around it?" I asked.

"I guess you could say that."

Twig blights. Twelve of them, once we counted. I know twig blights. Every druid knows twig blights — they are the forest's bad faith argument, the thing that wears roots like a costume. Intelligence of four, which means instinct and malice and nothing else. Not convertible, I was told, as if I had been about to suggest a conversion.

The staff moved. Not visibly. It is never visible. But there is a difference between holding a piece of old wood and holding something that is paying attention, and standing at the edge of that clearing, looking up at the henge and the storm and the dancing figures, I felt very clearly that the staff was paying attention.

Meril, I thought, and did not finish the thought.

Whatever he had brought here — whatever he had walked toward in his last years, whatever had drawn him to this region and this particular rot in the world — it was up that hill. The Circle of Thunder had been there the whole time, waiting. And here I was at the treeline with one leaf left on a borrowed staff, arguing with Vukradin about a necklace.

"We do!" I said, because we were already into it. "I keep telling you we do."

"And I keep telling you, we don't, we're not allowed to use that, it's not ours. I am holding on to this necklace, you are not — I don't trust you with it."

I did not have time for this. I also did not want to say that what I was actually thinking about was not the necklace at all but the way the staff had just shifted in my hand, subtle as a change in tide direction, some dormant quality of it waking up in response to whatever was saturating the air at the top of that hill.

"Hey, look over there!" Tactically useful, also accurate. "Okay, they're not even — they're not clustered enough for Fireball. They're not even clustered for it."

Vukradin had something cone-shaped. He mentioned it. Valphine offered Spirit Guardians, which was sensible. "Spirit Guardians plus Dash," Vukradin said, and something about that combination appealed to him in a planning way that I recognized. "Anyway, I've got, like, Thunderwave or something, I think that works pretty well, right? Like — Thunderwave, oh..."

The hill was still there. The storm was still building. The figures in the henge were still moving.

I held the staff and looked up at the standing stones and thought three leaves. At least there wasn't a fourth. 

We stayed at the treeline and studied what we were walking into.

# Chapter 40 — Unraveling the Storm God's Secrets

## Vukradin — Pre Battle Observations At The Circle Of Thunder

The hill had been climbing for most of the afternoon, and then it stopped. The treeline thinned and there it was — a ring of standing stones against a sky that hadn't decided whether to storm. The clouds above the henge moved the way clouds don't move: inward, circling, held in place by something that wasn't wind.

Two half-orcs stood among the stones. Twelve twig blights ranged around them like a second ring, bark-dry and motionless. The half-orcs were moving — not marching, not chanting. Dancing. Slow, deliberate steps that traced a pattern I couldn't fully see from the treeline but could feel the shape of: something being called toward. Something being opened.

Corbin, I thought. Then I pushed the thought somewhere useful.

Valphine was the one who named it. She watched for a moment, head tilted, that particular stillness she gets when she's cataloguing rather than reacting. "Summoning ritual," she said. Quiet, certain. The way she identifies things she's seen before.

I looked at the dancers again with that frame on them. "Oh, yeah, you would recognize that in this," I said. "Animate Dead." Not a question. The motion had that quality — purposeful vacancy, movement in service of something absent.

Soma watched. Brewbarry watched. Neither of them saw it the way Valphine did, and I was moving through the same fog — you don't spend your early years in Phandalin studying Talosian summoning rites. What I felt was the thing underneath the recognition: we were already late. Whatever they were calling, they had been at it for a while.

"I wonder who they're summoning," I said.

"I mean, you'll find out," came back at me from somewhere to my left, dry as bark.

"No, we won't."

There are promises you make in a treeline. I meant it as one.

The tactical question resolved itself quickly in my mind into a single clean answer: Silence. You cast it on the chanters, you cut the ritual at its voice. I had used it before, at the lighthouse, at worse angles. "Alright, I'm gonna cast Silence," I said. "And I, once again, have no, like, connection to the board, so — but I am casting silence on whoever it is is chanting." I kept my voice level. "And if that is unknown, then the two spellcasters."

Except they weren't chanting. That was the thing. We waited, someone went back through the logic of it, and the answer came back clear and slightly absurd: the ritual had no vocal component. They were dancing. Twelve twig blights and two half-orcs turning slow deliberate circles in a stone ring, silent as a held breath.

"Is there no vocal component to the ritual?" I asked, already hearing the answer.

There wasn't.

"Awesome," I said. The word landed flatter than I intended.

I filed the Silence away. "I mean, if there's no vocal component, there's whole person — that's also an option." Hold Person. Different spell, same interruption. The ritual, we determined, would end only when the dancers stopped. The standing stones couldn't be disrupted from the outside; the movement had to cease. Which meant the people making it had to cease.

Brewbarry was watching the half-orcs step their slow circles in total silence. "So they're like, what, a pantomime club?" he said.

I looked at him. I looked at the henge. "Okay, guys," I said. "Do you want me to go with Silence or Hold Person?"

Before anyone answered, someone caught the gap: we hadn't rolled initiatives yet. The observation arrived with that particular mild embarrassment of a procedural thing almost skipped over in the press of the moment.

We rolled. I drew the short straw on the tie-breakers — except I didn't. I won them. Several, one after another, my name settling ahead of the others in the order like the situation had opinions. I had no strong feelings about going first. I had feelings about what going first meant I would have to do.

"Alright," I said, once the order was settled and clear. "So I've not cast Silence — because we're following initiative order, alright?"

The hill was still there. The stones were still there. The dancers had not stopped.

---

## Brewbarry — The Battle At The Circle Of Thunder

Twelve of them.

Vukradin said "Two twigs and an anchorite — the start of a joke," and he wasn't wrong, but twelve is not a joke. Twelve is a wall of dry, clacking wood and too many limbs, and they came fast. I was already moving.

That's the thing about the rage. People think it makes you stupid. It doesn't. It narrows everything down to what matters — where the threat is, where the gap is, where you can put your body to do the most good. The hill was steep and the standing stones were wrong, humming at a frequency that made my back teeth ache, and the two half-orcs at the center of it all were still dancing, still chanting, and I wanted to be the thing that made them stop.

The lightning bolt came before I reached them.

Vukradin had already said it, dry as old bark: "It's nice that we're all lined up in a perfect line there — that's convenient for him." He wasn't wrong about that either. The bolt split the air between us and I got my shoulder turned into it and the current raked through my scale mail and I was still standing because I am Brewbarry Root Smasher Ogalakadu and I have stood inside a dragon's breath, and a half-orc in a boar mask is not a dragon. Valphine took it worse. I saw her flinch — something going tight across her face that she would never call pain but that looked exactly like it. The invisible weight of Lathander's favor pressing on every swing, and it still turned against her twice. Some days the gods are funny.

Vukradin didn't waste the opening. He spoke and the two anchorites stopped dancing. Just — stopped. Mid-step, mid-chant, arms out, frozen like figures in a story someone stopped telling. She stopped them. Both of them. I've seen him do things with words that make no sense to me and probably never will, but this I understood immediately: they were objects now. Problems with a shape. I moved.

The handaxe is not my preferred statement. I am a big man with a big sword and I know how to use it, but the blights were between me and the anchorites and the blights needed clearing, so I pulled the rage up from wherever it lives in my chest — it feels like a door opening, every time, like something I've been keeping in a small room finally being let out — and I threw.

The first throw was embarrassing. The axe spun wide and I had a half-second of pure goliath shame before Valphine pushed something of her certainty into my hand, metaphorically speaking. Something steadied. The axe was already back in my hand — it comes back, that's the trick, that's what I've been calling the Elemental Cleaver, because naming things makes them more real — and I adjusted and threw again and the blight I hit came apart like kindling. The momentum carried: one blight dead, the kill gave me a heartbeat of something clear and sharp, and I used it to take a second. Two down. One more and I missed, and by then the battlefield was moving around me in all directions.

Boney bit a paralyzed anchorite. The skeletal horse that Soma has somehow convinced is a willing member of this party bit a frozen man in a boar mask and I respect that completely.

Prutha ran through blights like they were grass. He put a javelin into the second anchorite and then charged in with his greataxe and I watched him and thought, briefly, of myself ten years younger, when violence was still simple, before the exile made it complicated. The anchorite he hit was already staggered. Not down, but close.

Soma became an elk. I have seen this before and it still takes a moment. One second: Soma, tortle, shell, old and unhurried. Next second: enormous antlers, enormous mass, crossing the ground between them in a breath. She hit the anchorite like a wall of forest deciding to move. He didn't have a choice about it.

Then she turned and hit one of the standing stones.

I thought: is that part of it? The henge was wrong, humming, tainted — Talosian hands had been on it, doing whatever ritual left a deer split open at the center with its heart replaced by something black and glassy and cold. Breaking a stone felt right. Felt like undoing something.

Valphine was moving through the twig blights like a woman who finds this situation aesthetically appropriate. The radiant presences she called up swirled around her and the blights caught in them just — went. She crossed the field and the light went with her, sweeping the dry things from the hill in waves, and I kept count because counting kept me sharp.

The anchorites never got free. Vukradin said, somewhere to my left, "Stopping a man in his tracks — both men — that is a very powerful thing," the way a man says something he already knew but is pleased to have confirmed.

At the end, Valphine said her battle cry. She said: *"Die ye then."*

She hit the last anchorite and he went down and the chant that had been threading through the air for however long we'd been on this hill — it stopped. The clouds kept moving for a moment, like they hadn't been told yet. Then they peeled back. Slow. Reluctant. Like something exhaling. The pressure in my ears dropped. The ozone smell faded to wet leaves and old rain. The stones went quiet.

No thunderclap. Just the end of it.

I stood in the sudden silence and looked at the two bodies. Young, both of them. Younger than I expected under the boar masks. One with storm-cloud tattoos spiraling up his neck, each one with an open eye. The other with crossed boar tusks down both forearms. They'd been someone's devoted. Someone had put that ink in their skin and told them this mattered, and they'd believed it, and now they were dead on a hill in the Neverwinter Wood with their masks rolled free of their faces.

I didn't feel bad about it. I felt the way I always feel after a fight: present, tired in the right way, aware of every part of my body. Alive.

Vukradin called it: "Huzzah!"

I exhaled.

---

## Soma — Aftermath And Ideological Clashes

The ozone went first. Then the pressure in my ears released — not all at once, but in slow degrees, like something enormous exhaling. The clouds peeled back from the henge in a reluctant spiral, and the standing stones, which had been humming through my boot soles since we crested the hill, went inert without ceremony. No thunderclap. Just wet leaves and old rain, and the ordinary smell of a forest after weather.

I stood still for a moment and let the quiet settle.

It's a druidic site. That was the thing I kept returning to as I walked the circle. Valphine had asked it and gotten confirmation — anchorites don't build henges, they borrow them. Someone had stood here before Talos claimed it. Someone had read the standing stones as sacred in a different register entirely, and whatever ritual they had meant to conduct here had been slowly replaced, glyph by glyph, with something that killed deer and summoned storm and hummed with a frequency that made my back teeth ache.

The deer was at the center. I didn't approach it at first. I noted it from the edge of the circle the way you note a thing you will have to reckon with in a moment.

When I did walk over, the smell hit me: blood, cooled, and something underneath that had no name yet. The animal had been split throat to belly, a clean and deliberate cut, and where the heart should have been there was a hollow of black, glassy material. Not stone. Not metal. Cool to the touch, faintly humming the way the stones had been humming a minute ago. I put two fingers near it and pulled them back.

I have seen a blighted grove. I have seen what happens when something reaches into a living system and begins redirecting it. This was that, except it had been done to a specific animal, at a specific moment, for a specific purpose. The deer had been chosen. The thought sat in me like a stone.

The anchorites were younger than I expected. I looked at their faces after the boar masks rolled free — young half-orcs, the kind of young where the face still carries some softness under the tattoos. One had storm clouds and coiling spirals up his neck, each spiral containing an open eye. The other had crossed boar tusks repeated down both forearms in a different hand but the same ink. They had been marked by someone who knew what they were doing. Someone who had wanted them to carry Talos on their skin.

I did not grieve them. I want to be honest about that. But I noted the youth, and I noted the tattoos, and I thought about what Adabra had called me at the council — interventionist, as if that were a diagnosis — and I thought about what it had taken to get here, and whether the intervention had been clean.

It hadn't been clean. It rarely is.

The argument started almost before the blood had dried.

Vukradin moved toward the bodies with the particular energy he gets when a principle is at stake. Holy symbols of Talos, he said. We need to return them. To the people who owned them.

Prutha looked at the dead priests. Pointed. *We found owners*, he said. *We give to them.*

I watched Vukradin attempt to reason through this. He was persuasive — he always is — but Prutha's logic was not wrong so much as it was complete in a way Vukradin's wasn't.

"You're trying to reason with an orc," Brewbarry said, which prompted Vukradin to ask whether that was racist, which prompted Brewbarry to explain that he wasn't saying orcs were evil, only dumb. "I'm dumb, but he's dumber. It's like dumb and dumber."

I didn't intervene. That's the honest account.

While the three of them cycled through this, Prutha destroyed the symbols. I watched him do it with the same focused attention he brings to everything. Then Valphine placed one of the holy symbols on the skull of a fallen anchorite and smashed it with her mace, and the sound it made against stone was flat and final.

"God. Valfina, we need to have a conversation about conflict-free goals," Vukradin said.

He was not wrong that it was a conversation worth having. But the symbols were already in pieces, and the argument had already happened in the order it always happens with us: the principle is stated, the chaos proceeds, and then we discuss the wreckage.

Then Prutha said something quiet, and I paid attention.

"My great-uncle said dawn comes for everyone. Even these ones. I did not think dawn would feel quiet."

He was looking at the bodies. His own kind, wearing someone else's faith on their skin. There was nothing performative in it — no theater, no flourish. He stood there with the weight of what he'd said settling around him, and Valphine answered him simply: *Dawn is powerful.*

He grunted and waved his axe. But the grunt was different. It had something in it.

I have seen a lot of converts and a lot of grief, and I have learned not to confuse one for the other too quickly. But Prutha was carrying something real in that moment, and I let it stand.

That was when I felt it. The staff had been trembling since the cursed clearing — a low, persistent movement in the wood, like a vine testing a trellis. Now it stopped. I looked down.

Near the crown, where the three leaves curved outward, there was a fourth bud. Tight, green, the size of a thumbnail. It hadn't been there before the fight. It hadn't opened. The staff had simply added it, quietly, without announcement, the way a tree puts out a new shoot in spring because the conditions have been met.

The staff goes quiet. That was the thing I noticed most — the trembling had stopped, and the quiet was not absence but a different kind of presence. Something had been registered. Something counted.

I stood with that for a moment longer than I needed to, shell sprout.

Then Brewbarry was asking about the leather thong with the boar tusk — *sorry, a leather thong?* — and Valphine's face had gone very interested in the way it goes when something aligns with her theology, and she was already talking about putting it in her sacraments.

I looked at the fourth bud. The henge stood around me, inert now, its original purpose obscured beneath someone else's use of it, and somewhere in the center of it a deer lay open with a hollow where its heart had been.

The storm was over. The quiet was real. I wasn't sure yet what we had earned, or what it had cost, but the staff was still.

---

## Valphine — Exploring The Caves Of The Circle Of Thunder

The tunnel smelled the way the Underdark smells when it's trying to pass itself off as something harmless — stone cold, mineral-flat, a hint of something decomposing at the far edge of the air. I followed Brewbarry in, which meant I was already second-guessing the decision before my eyes had finished adjusting.

Soma had remained outside with Boney. The tunnel mouth was too narrow for an elk, which meant the tortle in elk form was standing watch in the wood while the rest of us walked into a cave because Brewbarry was thirsty. This is what passes for a plan among the Protocol Shell-Crackers.

"More dungeon than refrigerator," Vukradin muttered, somewhere behind me. "Just, you know, FYI."

I could hear Prutha's footfalls just ahead of his breathing — faithful, uncomplicated, following as she always follows now. Another convert who hadn't yet learned to be complicated about it.

"It's a wine cellar," I said. I didn't actually believe that, but Brewbarry's entire demeanor brightened when I said it, and sometimes that's reason enough to say a thing.

"It's perfect," he said. "But I'm with you, man." He meant it the way he means most things: entirely, without irony, at full volume.

The first cave had a boar in it.

This is something I should probably have anticipated, given that we had just dismantled an orc cult whose iconography consisted almost exclusively of boar imagery. Brewbarry spotted it and announced that the meat was over here, that they had clearly been preparing for a barbecue, that there was a bowl. He was delighted. Vukradin, for reasons I still find difficult to fully account for, decided the correct response was to insult it.

He looked at the animal — a living creature, rooting in the cave dirt, doing nothing to any of us — and opened his mouth to deliver what I can only assume he considered a devastating rhetorical blow. The boar stared back at him with small, flat eyes and registered nothing. No flinch. No wounded pride. Complete indifference. Vukradin looked briefly as though he'd been the one mocked.

Then the boar charged.

"That wasn't very nice," Brewbarry said afterward, with the kind of moral conviction he usually reserves for more significant moments. "Why would you insult the boar? The poor boar. That's mean."

"You're okay with boars," Vukradin said, "but orcs are just inherently evil."

"No," Brewbarry said, entirely serious. "I wasn't saying the orcs are evil. I was saying the orcs are dumb. Sorry. Different things."

I watched this exchange happen. I had questions. I asked none of them.

What I did instead was let the guardians that circle me finish it. They had been circling since the ritual, radiant and faintly impatient, and the boar — already diminished by Vukradin's second attempt, which had landed — took the full weight of them. Lathander's light doesn't apologize for what it does.

"You now have radiated pork meat," I observed.

"Perfect for barbecue," Brewbarry said, already moving. "Now all we're missing are drinks. Let me resume my search."

Vukradin made a small, self-vindicating gesture. "It was coming right for us."

"It was," I said. Because it was. After the insult he'd weaponized into something sharper, anyway.

The second cave was more interesting. The lintel above the entrance was carved — a boar pursuing a stick figure, which struck me as either symbolism or a warning, depending on who was being asked to read it. Several boars were penned inside, alive and unsurprised by our arrival. Brewbarry said, "I'm tempted to say this is boring," and waited for someone to appreciate it. I gave him nothing. Some gifts are better deferred.

The satchel was in the corner. Dark leather, sealed with wax, and the wax was stamped with a circle and a single vertical line through it — Talos's mark, simple as a scar. My fingers found it before the others had finished looking at the boars.

"We should destroy the symbol," I said.

"Do you want to see what's inside before you—"

"Destroy the symbol. Then open the satchel."

The wax broke cleanly. A good seal, actually. Someone had pressed it with care.

Inside: three letters, the same hand throughout, the ink slightly smeared at the margins from being folded in haste. I read them in order.

*Your storm holds. Grannoc's does not. He is reckless; he will summon before the Lodge is ready.*

So Grannoc was a name, and the name meant recklessness, and the Lodge — Falcon's Lodge, presumably — was part of a sequence. We had known the Talosian network was coordinated. Here was the coordination, in ink.

The second letter was the one that settled cold in my chest.

*The Chosen is pleased with the boy from the eastern clan. Continue feeding him with stories. Do not yet tell him about the leash.*

"Holyish," Vukradin said quietly, reading over my shoulder.

Someone is being cultivated. Someone young, from the east, being fed a narrative someone else designed. The leash not yet revealed. I know what that looks like from the inside — the House does not announce its arrangements until you've already agreed to all the terms. I read that sentence three times.

The third letter was the shortest.

*If the henge falls, the manse summons in its place. If the manse falls, I summon myself. The boar comes. The storm comes. You are not to fail twice.*

We had just taken the henge. Which meant the manse was next — had always been next, by design. And whoever signed these letters without signing them at all had already accounted for our success.

There was a fourth fragment, this one from the firepit in a different cave — half-burned, barely saved, a different hand entirely. Sharper. Less ceremonial. Brewbarry read it aloud, because someone had to.

*…dawn cannot rise where the storm already lives. — K.*

K. A single letter, signing nothing, claiming nothing. I had seen that signature referenced before, in pieces of intelligence that had never quite assembled into a face. I filed it where I file things I do not yet know how to use.

Vukradin was already moving toward the exit — impatient, or processing, or both. He walked quickly when his mind was full, which meant he walked quickly often. I let him go ahead.

I almost missed it. Almost.

The floor of the cave, at the far edge of the torchlight: a seam. A square cut too clean to be natural, too faint for someone in a hurry to bother noticing. Vukradin had just stepped over it without registering it at all — his mind elsewhere, cataloguing implications, composing the debrief he would give Soma later. He was already three steps past it.

I crouched. The compartment opened on the third careful press, hinged from within. Inside: a single vial. Dense, the color of polished steel catching light, sealed with a stopper that had never been opened. I turned it over in my fingers. The magic in it was unmistakable — deep and defensive, the kind that settles around its carrier like a second skin. Something that would make a person nearly impossible to put down.

I held it for a moment. The Underdark upbringing is very clear on what to do with things you find that other people didn't notice: you keep them. It's not a rule so much as a reflex, worn into you over years of learning that advantage has a shelf life and visibility is a liability.

But Vukradin was going to be walking into whatever came next — the manse, or the person behind these letters, or the thing the third letter meant when it said *I summon myself* — without this.

He would refuse it on principle if I told him. He would deliver a careful, sincere speech about conflict-free acquisition and the ethical provenance of magical resources, and then he would leave it in the cave, and then he would possibly die because of it. I am a servant of Lathander, who teaches that suffering is sacred — but there is a difference between suffering that illuminates and suffering that is simply avoidable and stupid.

I closed my fist around the vial. I stood. I walked to where Vukradin had paused near the tunnel mouth, gesturing to Brewbarry about something, his pack open at the flap where he'd been rearranging after the letters.

I tucked the vial into the inner pocket of his pack, behind the folded notes, where it would be cold against his back and feel like nothing unusual. He didn't turn around.

He carries it now without knowing. Lathander's light lands where it's needed, not where it's asked for. I've started to believe that.

---

## Vukradin — The Final Cave And The Map Fragment

Narux's cave was the smallest of the three, and it smelled like him — orc-sweat and old vellum and something faintly charred, as if lightning had moved through the space so many times that even the stone had absorbed the habit.

I let the others go in first. Brewbarry stepped past me, looked around, and said what he always says when a room disappoints him.

"There's nothing in this cave. Let's beat it."

Valphine found the shield first — buried under a folded cloth, unadorned but solid, the kind of thing you don't notice until it catches the torchlight at the right angle. She passed it toward the center of the room without ceremony. Unassigned. Another item in the ledger of things that belong to someone we haven't decided yet.

The vellum sketches were worse. Narux had drawn Gorthok the way a child draws a nightmare — four legs, a silhouette the size of a granary, tusks trailing what had to be lightning. The annotations were in Orcish, and Valphine translated the ones that mattered: *feed before unleashing*. *The leash holds only if the stones sing.* There was a leather cord beneath the sketches, looped and knotted, and I did not touch it. Whatever it was for, I already didn't want to own it.

Then the map fragment.

Torn along one edge, soft at the corners from handling. Neverwinter Wood laid out in a dead man's hand — three sites marked with cult semantics I had to work out by looking at them together. The Circle of Thunder, the henge we had just finished clearing, was X'd through. Not in defeat. In *advance*. They had expected to lose this place. It was a decoy, or a distraction, or a sacrifice — some term that amounts to the same thing, which is: we were never the threat they were afraid of.

The Woodland Manse was double-circled. Active. Grannoc, presumably, already moving through whatever Narux had started here.

And Falcon's Hunting Lodge was the target. Both lines converged on it. The whole plan pointed there.

Brewbarry looked at the lodge marker and arrived, inevitably, at his own conclusion. "That's where they hide the drinks," he said. "Hidden in plain sight."

I did not have the energy to explain that this was not what hidden in plain sight meant.

"Okay," I said, because we were all looking at it and someone needed to say something. "We knew that we wanted to go to those places anyway. Those are the next places we have to go."

I pulled out the running list — the one I keep, partly for myself and partly because no one else will. I crossed off the entry. Horse with no rain. Done. The Circle of Thunder — what we had gone there to do, what we had told ourselves we were doing for Corbin — was finished. "I'm going to cross off the horse with no rain," I said. "We've completed it. And amazingly enough, the map leads us to the next two locations."

Both entries were marked in red.

"They are marked in red," I started, "because — " and I paused, because I knew how it sounded — "I have not yet gotten an apology."

From Falcon. From Falcon the hunter, who had been rude to us at the druid council, who had every reason to be rude to us at the druid council, as I was about to remember in a way I didn't especially enjoy.

The conversation that followed was one of those conversations where everyone already knows the answer but is waiting for someone to say it in a way that doesn't make them feel terrible. Valphine offered to be the one who smoothed the way, to talk where I might only push — and I appreciated it, because it meant I didn't have to ask. She had apparently already laid groundwork at the druid conclave — proposed a musical instrument to Falcon, some gesture of good faith, and he had said yes to it. I had not known this. I made a note.

And then came the part I had been not-thinking-about.

"Okay, guys — Falcon, the hunter, wants us to go to these places, but you know, he was pretty mean to us at the druid council, he doesn't really like us very much..."

I was working up to something, a defense, an accounting. Then the thing I had glossed over in the briefing reasserted itself. The Woodland Manse. The orcs. The timeline.

We were supposed to deal with the Woodland Manse. We didn't. Then the orcs attacked Falcon's lodge.

"We didn't deal with that," I said, and I meant it as a statement of fact, but it came out sounding like a plea. "It can't be in five places at once, you know? There's only so much we can do."

Falcon had put his faith in interventionists. That was the word — *interventionists*. And the intervention had not come, and the blowback had been considerable.

"His girlfriend died?" I heard myself say.

Brewbarry said, "Excuses, excuses," with the cheerful blankness of a man who has never once in his life felt accountable for a consequence he didn't personally cause with his hands.

I turned the map fragment over, looking at the torn edge, the ink bleeding slightly where the vellum had gotten wet at some point. Narux's planning. Narux's dead hand pointing at Falcon's lodge, at the Manse, at all the places we had not yet been. The light-footed boots — reward, promised, contingent on work we hadn't done before the damage was done.

Valphine was already thinking through the broker angle. That was something. Falcon was ready to give the apology, apparently — he'd had time to process it, or Valphine had helped him process it, or both, back in some conversation I hadn't been present for. The red markers on the list didn't have to stay red.

I set the map fragment down on the vellum sketches, the torn edge lined up with nothing, pointing nowhere in particular. The leash coiled underneath. The shield still unassigned.

There was only so much we could do. I knew that was true. I had said it aloud and I believed it.

I just kept finding, when I looked at the red markers, that believing it didn't move them.

# Chapter 41 A Storm is Coming

## Brewbarry — The Spoils of War

The shield was on the ground between us when I got a proper look at it.

Dark oak. Iron bands. Carving on the face — stones in rings, old work. Names worn but legible up close. Druid craft, Soma said, or something like it. Then a hammered iron disc nailed over the center, Talos's bolt on the front. Different hands.

Vukradin clocked it immediately. "Oh… oh, right. That'll stand out like a sword." He said it the way he says most things — like the information was obvious but he wanted everyone to know he knew. "The Cleric of Lathander is wielding a shield that has the image—"

He didn't finish the sentence. He didn't need to.

Valphine was already looking at it. She tilted her head, the way she does when something interests her more than she wants to let on. Then she reached out and wiggled the bolt. It popped free in her hand. Just like that. The whole Talosian centerpiece, loose as a bad tooth.

It had been stuck on crookedly from the start. The original carving underneath was never sanded smooth — you could still feel the druidic geometry through the iron disc, the older pattern asserting itself. The bolt sat on top of it the way a brand sits on skin: something added, not something belonging. Valphine turned the shield over. The leather straps inside were newer than the wood by a generation, at least. They smelled of pine tar and wet dog.

And there, on the inside of the rim: a half-orc tally. Four notches and a slash. Five somethings. Kills, maybe. Storms weathered. Hard to say.

Vukradin didn't hesitate. "Clearly, clearly not yours. We need to return it. Please mark it as blood."

Valphine said: "Done."

That was that. Or it should have been.

Prutha had been watching the whole exchange with the expression he gets when adults are doing something confusing. He is enthusiastic in ways that don't always map onto the situation. He pointed at Valphine and said what he thought was a helpful suggestion: "Valphine, use good shield! Valphine, cleric of sun!"

Valphine started to answer him — "You can use my shield" — but Vukradin cut across it.

"You can give them your shield, you can do whatever you want with your shield. But that shield?" A pause. "Not yours."

Prutha protested. Vukradin looked at him directly.

"Not yours either."

Prutha went quiet. Valphine confirmed the point without warmth: "It's not mine. Be sure." She tucked the shield under her arm and said she'd hold it until the owner turned up. Sensible. I thought about the half-orc with five somethings marked in wood. Probably dead. Probably won't come asking. Vukradin's right anyway.

Vukradin added, almost as an afterthought: "I recommend you guys do a better job hiding your crown merchandise." He meant the Talosians. He meant the bolt. It was good advice delivered with the tone of a man who has given up expecting it to be followed.

Then someone brought up my halberd.

I had the halberd from the shipwreck. It's mine. It fights well. I didn't see the need for extensive discussion.

Vukradin did the thing where he looks surprised and isn't. "You got a new halberd? Yo, what is going on here, guys?"

"I got a divorce," I said.

That got a pause. Then Vukradin, bless him, provided the cover story without being asked: "This is the halberd you've had all along. You just sharpened it on a sharpening stone. It just looks more lethal."

Valphine added: "And it looks a little waterlogged."

"You're mistaken," Vukradin said, to no one in particular. "This is the halberd you've had all along. You just sharpened it on a sharpening stone. And it just looks more lethal. That's totally fine. You can sharpen your halberd as much as you want. No problem with that."

I said nothing. The halberd sat against my leg, waterlogged and lethal.

Valphine started talking about Lathander then — the real Lathander, her version. The argument was that people have it wrong about what light is. It isn't tender. It burns twelve hours a day, it hurts everything it touches at full strength, and when it's gone people beg for it to return. They're afraid of the dark. That's not worship of goodness. That's worship of power. Power that's in plain sight, unashamed, causing harm constantly, and called *good* because the alternative is worse.

Vukradin listened. Then he said: "I like that the cleric is changing the god instead of the god changing the cleric."

Nobody disagreed with that.

Valphine talked about Lathander. I thought about my Talos. The Uthgardt one. Storms. The thing you don't argue with. I was sober when they found me guilty. That was a long time ago.

The halberd is mine. The shield belongs to whoever has five somethings marked in oak. That's the accounting, and it's simple enough.

---

## Vukradin — Planning the Next Move

The henge was behind us — stone and ash and the smell of something that had been wrong for a long time finally being allowed to stop. I didn't linger on it. That was done. The question was what came next.

Two choices. That was how it was framed. Woodland Manse, where another ritual was building toward something loud and terrible, or Falcon's Hunting Lodge, where whatever the Talosians had left behind was still festering. Infinite choices, technically. Two, really. Manse or lodge.

I didn't deliberate.

"I decided what I was going to do," I said. "I refuse to do anything more for this guy unless I get an apology from him."

Soma's response was immediate. "The Falcon? Yeah, fuck that guy."

It wasn't the diplomatic register I usually aimed for, but I wasn't inclined to correct her. What Falcon had done at the Druid Council — standing up in front of people I needed to persuade and taking shots at me, making himself useful to Adabra at my expense — that had cost us something. It had cost *me* something. I had been building something careful there, and he had leaned into it like a man who didn't understand the weight of what he was handling.

I had let it sit. I had not contacted him. Valphine had been working something with him in the background — I knew that much, though not the specifics — and I'd decided the next move was his to make. But now the lodge needed clearing, and apparently that required me to act first.

"You want me to call him? I mean, I can call him, but he better apologize."

Soma offered a fallback — the kind she offers when she's already decided the apology won't come. "If you don't get the apology, you can just, you know, we can take care of the infestation and not exactly protect the hunting lodge. Just… just throwing that out there as, you know, possible ways out of this mess."

I appreciated the option. I also didn't want to need it.

The tone was the thing I was thinking about. Demanding an apology from a man who has already moved on makes you look small. I needed it to land differently. I wanted *audacity* — a tone that communicated I was doing *him* a favor by asking at all.

"What's, like, the famous one… Lord Halueth Verres. Right? When they were confirming his elevation to Lord Magister of Neverwinter, there was that war-mage from Waterdeep — what was her name — she came up at the hearing and testified against him. The artifact thing, the contract with the apprentices, the whole mess. He got the seat anyway. And then *ten years* later, Lady Verres calls her on a Sending. Out of nowhere. 'Are you finally ready to apologize to my husband for what you said at his confirmation?' Ten years later. So ridiculous. And the war-mage actually replied. So that is the indignant tone I'm taking. Lady Verres on a Sending."

I burned a Sending — twenty-five words, one shot.

"Are you finally ready to apologize for the way you treated me at the Druid Council?"

Valphine added, from beside me: "Clarify the context. We're considering whether or not to go clear out all the, you know, the unwanted guests at your hunting lodge."

"Yeah, no, I'll add that." I felt the sending still holding. "The party — my friends here tell me we need to go take care of your problem at the hunting lodge. But are you ready to apologize for what you did at the Druid Council?"

Twenty-five words. You learn to compress.

The response came back the way it always does — not sound exactly, more like thought arriving from outside your own head, carrying another voice's weight. Falcon's voice. He said: *I apologize.* Categorical. Not hedged. No qualifications attached, no *I'm sorry if you felt* construction that isn't an apology at all. Just the thing itself, and then: *he's on the road home. Hopes to see us there.*

I stood with that for a moment.

"Sounds like he did apologize, though." I heard myself say it. "Okay. We can go."

He apologized clean. Valphine had done the groundwork — I could feel it in how ready the answer was. But the ask had been mine. Done.

We spread the documents out and went through them again — Flenz's sealed letters, Narux's vellum sketches, the half-burned scrap from Yargath. Their plan was clear enough: a summoning ritual confirmed underway at the manse, a backup ritual waiting if the first failed, and if both failed, whoever was in charge would attempt the summoning personally. The redundancy of it was deliberate. These people were not being casual about this.

The Talosian attack on the lodge puzzled me. It had been their lodge — we'd learned that the hard way — so why turn on it now?

"Scenario 1," Valphine said, sorting it for her own amusement, "they're Talosian, and then we destroyed them. Scenario 2 is that they're not Talosian, and we get some alloys."

I had no clean answer for either. It was the kind of question that would only resolve once we were there.

Prutha knew something, or thought he did. He'd heard songs about the lodge — heard the orcs had taken it, heard about the dogs. He didn't know who was staying there now. But he confirmed the three factions: former tribe, Talosian-aligned, independent. And the Anchorites had moved their own people through. That last detail felt important in a way I couldn't fully articulate yet.

I inventoried what I had left. Two minor workings spent, two serious ones gone — one of them just now on Falcon's apology. The count wasn't encouraging.

"You know, I've got… I've burned through the small workings and two of the heavy ones."

"I just need something to resist lightning," I said. "Do we have anything for resisting lightning?"

Soma checked herself and came up empty. Valphine suggested obscuring mist, or darkness — something to complicate targeting. I turned it over.

"Well, Darkness prevents targeting, but lightning doesn't require targeting."

A fire spell doesn't care whether it can see you. Neither does a storm. That was the problem.

"Well, we definitely need to camp at some point, right? It's more than a day's journey to the lodge, right?"

Closer than that. Hours away — not a day's march.

I looked at the others. Valphine wanted the rest — wanted to be full going in, which was reasonable. But if the ritual was already moving, time spent recovering was time we might not have. And we didn't know that it was active today. Only that it would be, eventually.

"Valphine, you can decide."

She looked at me the way she does when she's deciding whether I mean it. I was being genuine.

"We'll approach the hunting lodge from the south, though," she said finally.

Good enough.

"Let's head to the lodge," I said. "We can scope it out, we'll be there before long, and we can see what we see. That's fine."

I meant it. Whatever we were walking into, we were walking in with what we had. I had made do before with less.

---

## Valphine — Arrival at Falcon's Hunting Lodge

Two stories, timber and stone, a guest house off to one side, stable and smithy joined at the hip. The palisade newer in patches than the wood it had been lashed to — repairs under pressure, mismatched grain, the patchwork look of something rebuilt by people who could not afford to plan. Not vandals. Tenants.

Three figures on the walkway, watching the treeline. They'd seen us already. Two had bows up, not raised to draw but not lowered either — the posture of people who had made the calculation and not yet decided. The third was calling something down into the courtyard. A warning, or an order; I couldn't yet hear the words.

Falcon had asked me to mediate his apology to Vukradin. That gave me the floor here in advance. The dead partner, the attack the party had failed to prevent, the cloak left on its hook — I had inventoried all of that before we walked in.

I was wrong about the cloak.

Prutha moved before any of us spoke. He was watching the banners, and then he said it clearly, loud enough for the walkway to hear: *"Eastern Heart! Three banners on the wall! I see one! Sun and Stone! My uncle's clan march! They are family!"*

He was already walking. Deliberate pace, hands open — not a supplicant's gesture but a recognition, an announcement of his own. I watched him cover the distance to the gate. Close enough to be heard clearly, he stopped, brought his right fist to his left shoulder, and lowered his head. Then he reached inside his armor and drew out the bone whistle — the same one whose tradition had moved me more than I'd expected when I first saw it — and blew the dawn prayer into the cold air.

I held very still.

The sentry on the walkway lowered her bow. Returned the gesture. Shouted something down into the courtyard, and Prutha answered, and then she shouted back toward the gate.

*"Bring your drow, bring your Aasimar, bring all of them. We are listening."*

*Your drow* — possessive, not categorical. Prutha's. I noted what that meant for the alliance she thought she was joining.

Soma settled Boney near the stables once we were through the gate — *"Here you go, Boney. Chill out here"* — and the skeletal horse did not protest, which was more than could be said for some members of this party in new situations. Then we moved into the courtyard proper and I took in the full picture.

Women with infants. Old men around a cookfire, tending something in an iron pot. Children playing a stick game against the far wall, engrossed, not frightened by us yet. A dozen warriors spread through perhaps thirty orcs total. They watched us the way I have seen people watch strangers in places they did not choose to be but cannot leave. Recognition without welcome. Evaluation that was also calculation.

Refugees, then. Not raiders. The distinction mattered.

She came down from the walkway. Middle-aged, gray at the temples, broken nose healed badly, a scar along her jaw that had been made by something old and serious. She was wearing Falcon's hunter cloak over her own leather — not his colors or insignia reworked, not a trophy displayed with pride, but practical: the warmest thing in the closet, worn by someone who was cold. Someone who had been inside the lodge and taken what she needed.

Vukradin said something quiet to me. *"Not worthy."* I agreed, out loud, to confirm that I was paying attention to the same thing and had arrived at the same conclusion: this was not the moment to press on a dead man's cloak. *"Not worthy."* He said it once more, satisfied, and I let the matter close.

Vorga's elder was already looking at me. He leaned on his spear the way men do when the spear has outlasted their sons. His eyes had gone to my holy symbol the moment I walked into the courtyard, and they had not left it. Not hostility. Hunger. He had been praying a long time and had not been answered. I could see it without him saying it.

He said one word. Orcish. Direct.

*Dawn.*

Menzoberranzan does not reward readable expressions; the habit holds even under the open surface sky. But the word *Dawn* in his mouth touched the bodily place my own conversion had started from. The doctrine answered before the analysis did.

I looked back at him. I answered in Orcish, because he deserved that, and because it was the right instrument for the conversation.

"Yes," I said. "I'm the bringer of the dawn."

I let him see the symbol clearly.

"A painful dawn."

---

## Soma — Negotiations at the Hunting Lodge

Vorga looked at Valphine funny. Then she looked at Prutha funny.

I caught it — the slight tilt of Vorga's head, the flicker of calculation in her eyes as they moved from the drow to the orc at our backs. I said nothing. That's the kind of look you wait on.

Valphine had opened in orcish, which left the rest of us standing in the lodge courtyard with nothing to do but watch two people have a conversation we couldn't hear. I am old enough to be comfortable with that. Brewbarry was not. He shifted his weight from foot to foot like a man who had misplaced his axe and only just noticed. Vukradin tilted his head as if proximity to the sounds might eventually yield meaning.

What I could read was posture, and Vorga's posture said: not hostile. Occupied, maybe. Tired, certainly. The twenty or so people arranged around the courtyard — elders, children, a man who moved like his hip had been broken and reset badly — were not the profile of a raiding force. They were people who had found an empty building and moved into it the way animals find caves. Not conquest. Necessity.

I'd seen that before. After Cryovain came down, every creature that could move had moved. Refugees with claws.

Valphine switched to Common.

"I say they come from the southern mountains, and I told them about the Talosian nearby."

Vukradin turned immediately. "Oh, were they displaced by the dragon?"

"Sounds like it," Valphine said. Then, with the timing she uses when she wants the room to tilt: "dragon that you killed."

Vukradin looked at the assembled orcs. Then, louder than necessary: "We killed the dragon."

They stared. Twenty people, some armed, some not, all of them going absolutely still. Even the children stopped moving.

"You killed Dragon?" Vorga said it the way you say something when you're testing whether you've heard correctly.

"Yep," Vukradin said. He had, I noticed, put his chin up slightly. He does that.

Vorga processed this. "Dragon is dead. That home is now free to go back?"

"Yeah, you can go back," he said.

Valphine added, mild as water: "But something worse than the dragon."

It was accurate. I found myself nodding. The current occupancy of Icespire Hold was, as far as any of us knew, unconfirmed. Vukradin pivoted to an offer of escort, which was kind of him and potentially over-optimistic, which is also very much him.

Vorga chose to stay. She walked him through it — and I watched her do it. She had made this calculation before, in other places, with other stakes; you could see it in how fast she got to the end. The open woods against a pursuing force. Twenty people who could not run fast enough. She pointed to an elder near the wall, a man whose age showed in the careful way he held himself, and said: these people don't move fast enough. The creature would catch up.

Vukradin tried. "Brewbarry can carry everybody." He glanced at Boney, at me. "Put everybody on Brewbarry's back, and we got Boney, too."

I said, "And you can ride the elk, I can carry a couple."

It was worth offering. The instinct to find another way is a good instinct, and I've learned to let the shell-sprouts exhaust the alternatives before I agree that the harder path is the right one. Vorga walked through it anyway. The elk, the horse, the Goliath — the old man near the wall would still have needed carrying, and carrying the old man was not the problem. The problem was what came after: a summoned creature moving through forest while you were moving through forest, slower, encumbered.

"Creature will catch up," she said.

She said it a third time, or some version of it, and I think it was the third time that Vukradin actually heard it.

Her plan was simple. Kill the creature here, where they knew where it was coming from. Then walk to Icespire in safety. She had done the geometry of survival already, before we arrived. We had just joined a plan in progress.

The note surfaced briefly — something about a boy from the East, recovered at the Circle of Thunder, someone had called him a good recruit. I looked at Prutha. He was standing quietly, as he often does. He'd learned that one early — stillness is safer than motion. I thought about the way Vorga's eyes had moved to him when Valphine first spoke. I didn't say anything.

Then Brewbarry screamed.

Not the battle-cry kind. The other kind — the kind that comes out of a man who has sustained a genuine personal loss.

He appeared from the interior of the lodge holding a broken cask. The wood was dark with old moisture. Whatever had been inside was gone, soaked into the floor planks, wasted. Not drunk. Wasted.

"I will kill them all!" he announced to the courtyard.

Vukradin moved toward him immediately, hands up, voice calibrated. Every scrap of warmth and persuasion he had went into promising more alcohol would be found, that calm was achievable, that this was a recoverable situation. Brewbarry accepted this in the way a man accepts a rain delay — reluctant, not persuaded, still certain someone was going to pay.

I watched him and thought: the Talosians had not met Brewbarry yet. That was going to be a short conversation.

Valphine cast something during the lull — not a healing. A bracing. The kind that makes a body harder to drop. I felt it in my shell.

"It can be healed," I said. "Semi-temporary."

"It's better than temporary," Vukradin said.

"No, it is," I said. "Because it can be healed."

Vorga suggested someone stand on the wall so the Falcon would see a friendly face and not come in shooting. Vukradin volunteered. "We're friends now," he said, warm — he'd been waiting on this reconciliation. "He apologized."

He stood up on the palisade and the Falcon came out of the trees with war paint on his face and a bow already in hand.

Vukradin's voice dropped an octave very quickly. "Jay! You made it! Oh, oh, oh, I'll come down, I'll come down, I'll come down."

The Falcon's bow came down when Vukradin reached him. They spoke. The Falcon listened to the whole of it — the orcs, the alliance, the incoming Talosians, the plan — and when Vukradin finished, he said: "You have my sword."

I thought about the last time I'd seen Falcon, at the Enclave council. The way grief wears a man differently than anger does. This version of him — paint on his face, weapon ready, walking toward a fight with something like purpose — was different from that one. Not healed. But aimed.

Valphine pointed at the map and said something about the gatehouse having fantastically defensible positions, and we put three orc archers at the arrow slits. They settled in. They knew the work.

The lodge went still. Waiting.

---

## Brewbarry — The Siege of Falcon's Hunting Lodge

The storm came wrong.

I knew it before I understood it — a pressure behind my eyes, sudden and complete, like a fist closing around the inside of my skull. Then the sky went. Afternoon to dark in a single breath, and the wind came up hard enough to rattle the palisade logs in their lashings.

The animals felt it first. The boars in Falcon's pens started throwing themselves against the walls of their enclosure, squealing in a register that made the hairs on my arms stand up. I've heard boars charge. This wasn't that. This was panic — pure, animal, knowing.
